# [CHAPTER 1The Modern API Economy](contents.xhtml#ch01a)

[Introduction](contents.xhtml#sc2_1a)

In this age of accelerated digital transformation, few concepts have been more central to the evolution of software systems than Application Programming Interfaces (API). Simply put, APIs are a set of rules and protocols that allow different software applications or services to communicate and exchange data with each other. It provides predefined functions or business services to interact with other systems to process and retrieve data. It can also be referred to as a contract between a producer of information/data and the consumer of this data. Examples of an API include weather applications that can use APIs to retrieve real-time weather data from a weather data service. Similarly, payment processors like Stripe or PayPal provide APIs for secure online payment transactions. Companies like Uber have created applications that interface with different APIs, offering various business services. For example, it interfaces with Maps APIs for geographic data, payment processing APIs, and communication APIs like Twilio.

APIs have become extremely important since software systems are no longer monolithic entities that exist in isolation. Through APIs, software components can share data, perform actions, and expand capabilities without understanding the intricate details of other software components.

The API economy refers to creating and consuming APIs that provide economic value to the entire software ecosystem. It allows for the monetization of APIs, increases the scope and reach of APIs, and helps with rapid prototyping and creation of new services by reusing different APIs and performing various business services.

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_2a)

In this chapter, we will be discussing the following topics:

  * Introduction to APIs
  * Consumers and producers of APIs
  * Essential components of an API
  * Types of API
  * API-first architecture
  * Emergence of API economies
  * Businesses around APIs
  * Rise of REST 
  * Legacy application transformations with APIs
  * Importance of API security



[Objectives](contents.xhtml#sc2_3a)

The primary objective of this chapter is to introduce the concept of APIs and then expand to discuss the idea of an API-first architecture and the emergence of businesses that expose their APIs as business services. At the end of this chapter, you will understand the basics of APIs, the concept of APIs and business usage, and the users and consumers of APIs. In addition, we will begin exploring REST architecture and why it has become essential in API-based software development. We will be setting the foundational concepts for the rest of the book.

[Introduction to APIs](contents.xhtml#sc2_4a)

An API defines the method by which different applications, systems, and devices communicate and exchange data. The API specifies the procedures, rules, and protocols for requesting data from a service or a resource and the format in which it receives the data.

Here is an illustration of how the API fits within the context of requesting information from another server or service. Refer to the following figure

Figure 1.1: A logical example of an API interaction

The preceding figure represents a logical view of an API being called and returning data. The advantage of an API is that the box depicted in the dotted lines above is often a black box to the client.

  * Example 1: When ordering food in a restaurant, if the kitchen represents a service (food), the customer is the user who then utilizes a server to order food, who in turn communicates with the kitchen and then brings the food to the customer. In this case, the server is the API acting as the conduit between the consumer and the kitchen. A diagrammatic representation is shown in the following figure:



Figure 1.2: Food service in a restaurant as an API illustration

  * Example 2: When a user wishes to search for flights from various airlines, they use sites like Expedia, Kayak, etc. These sites do not own the airline platforms. So, when the user searches for a flight, the platform sends multiple requests to the airline systems using APIs, getting multiple results and aggregating them for you to see. The following illustration depicts the process.



Figure 1.3: Travel sites aggregating data as an API illustration

An API has a lot of things going on behind the scenes and has some essential characteristics they need to adhere to, such as:

  * Communication protocol: This is a mechanism an API utilizes to transfer data over the network. These protocols are essential for APIs because they define the rules and standards for exchanging data between the systems. For example, Hypertext Transfer Protocol (HTTP), Simple Object Access Protocol (SOAP), Google Remote Procedure Call (gRPC), GraphQL, etc. are all commonly used protocols.
  * Data formats: When the systems need to return or send data, they follow a standard format for representing this data. Examples include JavaScript Object Notation (JSON), Extensible Markup Language (XML), etc.
  * Access levels: Access levels dictate how much information can be returned. Examples include public (everyone can access and use it), private (only authorized users or applications), or partners (only specific clients or partners have access to it).



[Consumers and producers of APIs](contents.xhtml#sc2_5a)

In the context of this book, it is essential to define who creates and maintains APIs (API producers) and who uses them (API consumers).

[API producer](contents.xhtml#sc3_6a)

We define an API producer as an entity, system, service, or application that designs, develops, maintains, and distributes APIs for other entities or systems to consume (API consumers).

Some best practices that a producer might want to follow include:

  * Designing the API: An essential aspect for producers is to define and decide on the structure, methods, parameters, and return values that the API will provide. Return values would include errors.
  * Documenting the API: Proper usage, key inputs, and expected outputs must be made clear in the API specifications. Documentation should include usage examples, including any error messages that could be generated.
  * Version control: API producers need to continuously update their APIs as services evolve. Versioning using major and minor releases is essential in keeping existing implementations intact.
  * Availability and scalability: Producers must design their infrastructure to handle the volume of requests and scale as needed based on business needs.
  * Security: Producers must implement necessary security methods to prevent unauthorized access to systems and the corresponding data.



[API consumer](contents.xhtml#sc3_7a)

We define a consumer as a service, application, or system that accesses and uses services or the data of a service provided by an API. The consumer makes a request using the API, receives the API results, and then processes and displays the data as required. For example, a mobile application fetches weather data from a weather service API; in this case, the mobile application is the data consumer.

Some best practices that a consumer might follow include:

  * Integration: Consumers need to have a good understanding of the API specifications as documented by the publisher. This will ensure correct API usage as per standards.
  * Error handling: APIs can fail for numerous reasons. Consumers must anticipate potential failures and implement error-handling mechanisms to ensure smooth user experiences.
  * Versioning management: APIs are dynamic, and different versions are released for numerous reasons, including security patches. Consumers should be aware of these other versions and make changes to the code depending on severity and release schedules. This would include backward compatibility to ensure systems do not break down.
  * Managing usage limits: Today's infrastructure allows for massive service capacity and has inherent protection for rate limiting. However, there is a potential for them to be abused or go beyond the threshold set by producers; a well-behaved consumer must also ensure they adhere to these limits to prevent disruptions.



There is a symbiotic relationship between API producers and API consumers. One does not exist without the other. An additional point is that API consumers can be producers and vice versa. Indeed, in many cases, teams are both consumers and producers of APIs.

The API world is dependent on the successful interaction between consumers and producers. While they have distinct roles, their collaboration ensures the practical usage of APIs.

In a typical system, there will be numerous producers and consumers. In Figure 1.4, you will find multiple producers (could be within one company or even third-party companies) that service various consumers using APIs. This is a straightforward representation of a very complex scenario. In subsequent chapters, we will build on this notion to explore more complex routing mechanisms using API gateways.

Figure 1.4: Simple interaction between an API producer and an API consumer

[Essential components of an API](contents.xhtml#sc2_8a)

An API request has a properly defined structure. This structure is standardized and, hence, understandable by the producer and the consumer. These key components must be understood when making an API request.

[Endpoints](contents.xhtml#sc3_9a)

The term endpoint is analogous to a specific IP address (IP:Port) or location for a particular service accessible by a client. So, in the context of an API, an endpoint refers to a specific URL and IP Port where the API is accessed. Each endpoint typically corresponds to a particular function or resource of the application. This function can perform multiple operations, for example, retrieving, sending, updating, or deleting data. So, the endpoint is defined by the combination of the URL path and the method (for example, GET, POST, PUT, DELETE) as described:

Example:

If we want to retrieve a list of books from a website that has an API for it, an example endpoint could be:

To retrieve all books:

https://api.testURL.com/list

To retrieve a specific book by its ID (for example, UZS123)

https://api.testURL.com/books/UZS123

In all instances, the testURL.com will translate to an IP address, e.g., 146.11.13.141, and usually default to port 80 (http) for unsecured and 443 for secured (https), so the example above would be:

https://146.11.13.141:443/list

The advantage of using a URL vs. an IP port is that the IP address could change, making maintenance of the code more complicated as all instances of the calling IP would need to be changed.

This is a simplified version. In reality, endpoints have requests attached to them, as explained in the next section.

[Requests and responses](contents.xhtml#sc3_10a)

Communications between a client (consumer) and a server (producer) using an API involves a request and the corresponding response.

The format and structure of data sent to and received from the API includes headers, body, and status codes.

Requests and responses are fundamental components of the HTTP protocol, facilitating communication between clients (like web browsers) and servers.

[Requests](contents.xhtml#sc4_11a)

A request is what the client sends when it wants to access services for data. This request consists of each endpoint, the corresponding HTTP method, a header, and the body. Requests have the following parts:

Methods

They indicate the type of action requested. Some standard methods are:

  * GET: Retrieve data.
  * POST: Submit or add new data.
  * PUT: Update existing data.
  * DELETE: Remove data.



Thus, a complete endpoint often refers to a combination of a URL and an HTTP method. For example, GET /books and POST /books are different endpoints.

Header

These provide meta-information about the request, such as authentication tokens, client information, or content type.

Body

This contains the actual data that needs to be sent to the API. Not all requests have a body (for example, a simple GET request).

[Responses](contents.xhtml#sc4_12a)

After the server processes the API request, it sends back a response to the client. A response also has multiple parts:

Status code

It indicates the result of the request. The response codes are divided into different classes based on their purposes. Some standard error code examples are:

  * 2xx: Success
    * 200 OK: The request was successful.
  * 3xx: Redirection
    * 302 Found: The resource is temporarily moved to a different location.
  * 4xx: Client Errors
    * 404 Not Found: The requested resource could not be found.
  * 5xx: Server Errors
    * 500 Internal Server Error: The server encountered an error.



These response codes are necessary because they communicate the outcome of a request by the server to the client. This helps developers design systems that can handle errors gracefully and give helpful messages. These messages also help in debugging API or server behavior.

Headers

Just like in requests, these provide meta-information about the response.

Body

Contains the data returned from the server, if any. For example, it might include details of the book that was requested.

In our example of retrieving books from a URL and applying a GET method, you would typically use a tool or software that allows you to make HTTP requests. A web browser typically uses the GET method by default, so if you were to paste an endpoint and if the endpoint supports it, you will see the results in a browser.

Most modern programming languages have libraries or built-in functions to make HTTP requests.

Here is a simple example in Python using the requests library that is available in Python:

import requests

response = requests.get("https://api.testURL.com/v1/books")

if response.status_code == 200:

print(response.json()) # Assuming the API returns data in JSON format

This example only checks for success and then prints the response data. A real example would have more robust error checking.

The v1 in the API URL is an example of good practice while defining APIs to maintain versions as part of the full URL, which will be explained in subsequent chapters.

[Data formats](contents.xhtml#sc3_13a)

APIs frequently transfer data between systems. Without a structured representation of the data, communication would be inconsistent and interpreted differently between systems. Hence, in the context of APIs, data formats determine how the information is packaged and interpreted between servers and clients.

Common data formats include:

  * JSON is a lightweight, text-based format that humans can easily understand. Its structure makes it easy for computers to interpret the data quickly, and it uses a key-value pair methodology to encode it. This format is widespread and utilized by APIs to exchange data.



Example (JSON)

{

"id": "UZS123",

"title": "Coding in Java"

}

Another example is the XML language. This markup language defines rules for encoding documents in a format easily understood by computers and in a form humans can read. It uses tags, which is similar to HTML.

Example (XML)

<book>

<id> UZS123</id>

<title>Coding in Java</title>

</book>

In most development environments, there are built-in language tools and functions to make a call to an API straightforward by calling relevant functions. The developer rarely must dive deep to create the API calling format described above.

[Types of APIs](contents.xhtml#sc2_14a)

While an API is just a mechanism for retrieving data, some classification can be applied to these APIs based on their access, usage, permissions, security, etc. Some standard ways of classifying APIs are as follows:

[External or public APIs](contents.xhtml#sc3_15a)

These API interfaces are intended for general use by external users, developers from different organizations, or sometimes any user who can access the system. They are typically well-documented and secure and might require some kind of authentication. Examples include Google Maps API.

[Internal APIs or private APIs](contents.xhtml#sc3_16a)

These APIs are usually created for internal use within an organization. They allow different teams in a company to utilize services, thereby improving productivity. They are not exposed outside the company's networks and have strict authorization requirements.

[Third-party APIs](contents.xhtml#sc3_17a)

These APIs are typically specialized functions that certain external vendors (third parties) provide, sometimes for a fee. This functionality might be such that organizations do not have the expertise or budget to build and might be cheaper to source from third-party companies. An example would be tax calculations, which are complex and change yearly.

[Web APIs](contents.xhtml#sc3_18a)

These are APIs available over the web, usually using HTTP or the HTTPS protocol. They can be public, private, third-party, or other combinations. Examples include RESTful service, SOAP services, and GraphQL.

[Database APIs](contents.xhtml#sc3_19a)

To provide a standardized method for interacting and manipulating data, many systems offer database APIs that allow Create, Read, Update, Delete (CRUD) operations at the very minimum without needing to write SQL or other specific code directly. Examples include Java Database Connectivity (JDBC).

[Operating System APIs](contents.xhtml#sc3_20a)

These highly specialized and specific APIs allow the software to interface with the underlying Operating System (Windows, Linux, etc.). This allows the developer to leverage OS functionality without knowing the details. Examples include file handling, memory management, etc. For example, the Windows operating system has many APIs that are available for consistent interaction with the underlying operating system.

[API-first architecture](contents.xhtml#sc2_21a)

Modern software systems are highly complex and distributed. The need for clear boundaries between systems and a robust communication mechanism across software components has become crucial. One transformative way for companies to adapt to this is to embrace an API-first architecture. In this architecture, the priority is the design and development of an API over the application's actual development. It centers around the idea that an API is the most crucial aspect of software development, and complex systems are built using a reusable set of APIs. The API contracts and functions are defined, with the implementation being secondary. This also allows potential consumers to test APIs and suggest changes before implementation, thus reducing development time.

The following figure represents an API-first design and test before build philosophy:

Figure 1.5: API-first design

The key benefits of this approach include:

  * Consistency: All APIs emanating from an entity strive for consistency in specifications, calling mechanisms, and error messaging. This promotes interoperability and reduces development costs by less training.
  * Parallel development: Once the API contract is defined, backend and front-end teams can work simultaneously on development.
  * Easier testing: The defined endpoints allow for mock testing and early discovery of potential integration issues.



[Key concepts in an API-first approach](contents.xhtml#sc3_22a)

The key concepts of an API-first approach are as follows:

  * Definition before development: API specifications are defined upfront, serving as a consistent communication contract between software components.
  * Decoupling: APIs separate system components (for example, front and back end of other services), allowing them to evolve independently and with different teams and development schedules.
  * Consistency: API's first principle ensures uniform interfaces across services and products, offering a consistent view to different developers. This ensures that as the ecosystem grows, developers can expect a standard method of interaction.



[Benefits of API-first approach](contents.xhtml#sc3_23a)

The benefits of following an API-first approach are as follows:

  * Speed to market: With a precise API specification, parallel development can occur. For example, while backend teams develop the system based on the API specs, front-end teams can simultaneously build interfaces and experiences based on the exact API specifications that have been defined and offer consistency.
  * Collaboration: API first design offers tools that provide mock API versions, allowing developers to interact with and test against these mock endpoints even before the implementation is complete.
  * Improved quality: Defined contracts reduce ambiguity and integration errors. This can lead to fewer integration bugs and more precise expectations between systems.
  * Scalability: APIs enable modular development, making it easier to scale parts of a system independently and based on demand.
  * Flexibility: API-first architectures allow easier integration with third-party systems, partners, or other external entities.



An API-first approach is a strategic choice and blueprint in software that focuses on clear communication and collaboration for robust systems and faster delivery. API-first architecture places the API at the forefront of the development process and helps organizations create smoother integrations, more robust systems, and faster delivery cycles.

[Emergence of API economies](contents.xhtml#sc2_24a)

The API economy refers to the commercial use of APIs as a business model and practice that revolves around the sale, consumption, and monetization of APIs. Simply put, it is a marketplace where companies expose their digital services as APIs to be consumed by other companies and unlock new revenue streams. By packaging core competencies and services into APIs, organizations enable third parties to integrate, leverage, and build upon those services in their applications, creating a vast ecosystem.

Some examples of companies include:

  * Stripe: In 2010, Stripe began as a service to simplify online payments. Stripe provides straightforward APIs for developers to embed into their applications rather than force businesses to integrate multiple complex payment gateways and networks. Stripes' API-first approach has revolutionized e-commerce, allowing businesses of all sizes to integrate payment processing with just a few lines of code.
  * Twilio: By offering APIs for messaging, Twilio stands as a testament to the power of API economies in telecommunications. Twilio has empowered developers to embed communication capabilities directly into their applications without needing backend infrastructure or to understand telecommunication service protocols and mechanisms by offering APIs for messaging, voice, and video communications.
  * Plaid: In fintech, Plaid offers APIs that allow developers to access user banking data to build a range of financial applications for analytics, budgeting, loan processing, etc. This has enabled companies to easily incorporate connections to various financial institutions without setting up individual links and protocols to different financial institutions, allowing for faster time to market.



The advent of cloud infrastructure has democratized software development and deployment, and as a result, one sees a proliferation of companies that expose their APIs and charge for them. Refer to the following Figure 1.6:

Figure 1.6: API economy

Consumers can utilize different API services from multiple companies to build their products, unlike in the past, when they had to build everything independently. Uber is an example where they incorporate APIs for payments, communications, and maps from third-party companies while focusing on their core competence.

However, there are implications for engineers and software architects while designing new systems. Some attributes they should be following are:

  * Modular architecture: In the API economy, software architectures are increasingly modular and decoupled. This fosters agility and scalability. Architects should consider how services can be consumed or exposed as APIs when building systems.
  * Security and governance: With the external exposure of business functionality, security becomes crucial. Rate limiting, authentication, and authorization are essential controls of any public-facing API.
  * Standardization and documentation: An API's success often hinges on its usability. Following RESTful principles, providing comprehensive documentation, and offering SDKs for popular languages can significantly enhance developer adoption.
  * Monetization and business models: The API economy has also brought new monetization strategies. Whether it is pay-per-use, tiered subscriptions, or freemium models, organizations have various means to turn their APIs into revenue generators.



[Businesses around APIs](contents.xhtml#sc2_25a)

Historically, companies operated in isolation with proprietary systems and data silos, with minimal effort made to allow integration between systems across companies. A whole industry sprung up around integration between different systems using various mechanisms. The emergence of APIs from mere technical instruments into foundational pillars supporting entire business models has sprung up businesses built around APIs, often unlocking new revenue streams and underpinning significant technological innovations.

Companies have been working in different ways to monetize the digital ecosystem they have built over several years. Some examples of this monetization are:

  * Direct monetization: Companies charge developers for access based on usage, data volume, features, etc. Pricing for this is based on various methods, including paying for use, having subscription models, etc. Companies like Twilio and Stripe succeeded with this approach, charging for functionalities like messaging services or payment processing.
  * Freemium models: Some companies offer basic API functionalities for free and have paid tiers providing advanced features or higher usage limits. This approach helps build a base of developers who can later convert to paying customers. It also lowers the cost of entry for companies to develop products and test the market.
  * Indirect monetization: A less visible, though equally powerful method is to use APIs to boost platform engagement or to gather data. Media companies like Facebook and X (formerly Twitter) provide APIs that enable third-party apps to interact with their platforms, thereby driving user engagement and data collection beneficial to the producing companies.



Traditionally, APIs were supplemental to primary products. Now, for many companies, the API is the product. Companies like Stripe (payment services) or Plaid (financial data connectivity) offer functionalities consumed primarily through their APIs, positioning them as primary products rather than supporting features. This has risen to the concept where APIs are a company's primary product offerings.

While an API economy offers many advantages, it has also added some complexity to the development cycle. Two examples are:

  * Security: Ensuring that APIs are secure from breaches is paramount. Misconfigured or vulnerable APIs can lead to significant data leaks or unauthorized data access. This could result in severe liabilities.
  * Quality of service: With businesses depending on API uptime, ensuring high availability and low latency becomes essential. While this is offset by choosing to host applications on public cloud infrastructure (for example, AWS, Azure, Google, etc.), an architect still has to design their services using scalable principles.



The ascent of API-centric businesses is a massive shift in the technology landscape. As digital products become more prevalent, APIs will remain the central building block, enabling companies to innovate by collaboration and take a best-of-breed approach to building software.

[Rise of REST](contents.xhtml#sc2_26a)

There are many protocols for the usage of APIs. However, in the last few years, the REST protocol has been the most widely used protocol by companies.

Representational State Transfer (REST) is a software architecture style for designing networked applications. It has gained immense popularity over the last two decades and is used extensively for building scalable-based web applications and services.

While REST is the architectural style, RESTful refers to the implementation of that style. In our discussions throughout this book, when discussing the approach, design, or architectural style of a system component or service, we will refer to it as REST principles. When we discuss the actual service, application, or implementation of an API, we will refer to it as RESTful. In short, we design using REST and implement using RESTful.

However, REST has gone beyond usage only for web applications. Many applications, even non-web applications, utilize APIs for inter-communication. APIs are also the backbone for microservice communications. Some key factors that helped in the rise of RESTful implementations are:

  * Simplicity: The REST principles emphasize simplicity and statelessness, which can reduce the complexity of server configurations and interactions.
  * Scalability: Due to its stateless nature, REST is inherently scalable, making it suitable for web-scale applications and large distributed systems.
  * Interoperability: REST is protocol-agnostic, although it is mainly implemented over HTTP. This allows for greater flexibility and ensures systems can interact despite differences in their underlying technologies.
  * Evolution of the Web: As the World Wide Web evolved, so did the demand for efficient, scalable, and standardized access to web resources. REST provided a conceptual model that aligned with this evolution.
  * Rise of mobile and IoT: The proliferation of mobile devices and the Internet of Things (IoT) has created a need for lightweight and efficient web services, further propelling REST to prominence.



REST's strength lies in its simplicity, enabling developers to build scalable and efficient applications. The following are the five fundamental principles that define this architectural style:

  * Client-server model: Separating the client and server layers promotes system modularity. Thus, the client is responsible for the user interface and user experience, and the server handles the data storage, retrieval, and information processing. This separation allows both parts to evolve independently.
  * Statelessness: This is an important concept that implies that every API request from a client to the server is independent and includes all the necessary information for the server to fulfill the request. Hence, the server does not need to store session information about the client between requests, simplifying server design.
  * Cacheable: Responses from the server can be labeled as cacheable or non-cacheable. Cacheable responses help reduce client-server interaction, improving performance and user experience as clients can reuse previous responses and not reuse network infrastructure with its inherent latencies.
  * Layered system: Within a RESTful implementation, an architecture can consist of multiple layers, each with specific functionality. Each layer only has knowledge of the immediate layer it interacts with. In Figure 1.7, each layer is a set of services, and each service communicates across layers using APIs. In subsequent chapters, we will examine the complexities of such interactions between services, their challenges, and the best practices to handle them.



Figure 1.7: Layered, modular API-driven architecture

This layered approach increases modularity, scalability, and flexibility in systems architecture. This modularity ensures that changes in one layer minimally affect other layers and promotes scalability and maintainability.

  * Uniform interface: The foundation of REST's simplicity is its uniform interface. A consistent interface simplifies and decouples the architecture, making the overall system more predictable and easier to interact with.



As the usage of REST increased, more complex web applications were built, evolving into what is now called Web 2.0.

[Web 2.0](contents.xhtml#sc3_27a)

Web 2.0 represents the second phase of the internet's evolution. Static HTML pages and limited interactivity characterized Web 1.0. Web 2.0 emphasized user collaboration, sharing, and creating web content. Websites transitioned from being informational billboards to platforms that facilitated user participation. Notable examples of Web 2.0 include blogging platforms, social networking sites, wikis, and video-sharing platforms.

Several key features define the Web 2.0 era:

  * User participation: Content creation shifted from a centralized model (Webmasters) to a decentralized one. For example, platforms like Wikipedia allow users globally to edit and contribute.
  * Rich user interfaces: Asynchronous JavaScript and XML (AJAX) became prevalent, making web pages more responsive and interactive without requiring page reloads for every action.
  * Social networking: Sites like Facebook and Twitter emerged, changing how people interact, communicate, and share online.
  * Folksonomy: Tagging of content by users became common, allowing for organic content categorization based on user-defined tags.



Here is why APIs were fundamental to Web 2.0:

  * Integration: APIs enabled different web services to integrate seamlessly. For instance, a travel blog could pull in Google Maps to show locations, or a news website could embed tweets directly into its articles.
  * Mashups: APIs empowered developers to create mashups — combining features from multiple sources to produce new services. A classic example is a weather application that uses mapping and meteorological data from different providers.
  * Platform growth: Companies like Facebook and Twitter allowed third-party developers access to their platforms via APIs. This led to the creation of countless applications and integrations, driving user growth and engagement.
  * Standardization: RESTful APIs, which use standard HTTP methods, became popular during this period, promoting a standardized approach to building and consuming web services.



The emergence of Web 2.0 and APIs reshaped the internet landscape. Web 2.0 introduced sophisticated interactivity and user participation in which APIs acted as the glue and facilitated integrations. Today, as we advance into Web 3.0 and beyond, the principles and innovations from this transformative era still serve as foundational cornerstones.

However, we cannot escape some real-world problems, such as the coexistence of legacy applications (mainframe applications, older monolith applications, etc.) and the modern cloud and API-based newer applications. In the next section, we talk about how legacy applications are being transformed using APIs to encapsulate the underlying business logic and make it available to applications that can consume these legacy APIs for their business needs.

[Legacy application transformations with APIs](contents.xhtml#sc2_28a)

Legacy applications, particularly those on mainframes, often contain years or decades of valuable business logic and data. However, they might need help integrating with modern applications or platforms. They are also harder to access and require intricate knowledge of decade-old systems, which either exist in a select few or are lost forever. Transforming legacy applications by encapsulating the functionality in well-defined APIs helps businesses expose and leverage those functionalities in new and more agile environments.

There are many benefits to transformation using APIs. Some examples are:

  * Interoperability: APIs allow different software solutions to communicate with each other, making legacy systems more adaptable to changes.
  * Scalability: Modern API solutions can handle large volumes of requests, ensuring that the legacy system can meet growing business needs. However, keep in mind that there is a limitation on the scalability of the underlying legacy system, which might be challenging to mitigate.
  * Cost efficiency: Encapsulating business functionality and exposing them as APIs allows organizations to leverage existing investments, especially for sparsely used business logic and data.
  * Flexibility: APIs can serve as a bridge to modern technologies, enabling the introduction of contemporary features like cloud integration or mobile accessibility.
  * Risk reduction: APIs allow for a gradual transformation, enabling testing and transition at a manageable pace, reducing the risks associated with big-bang migrations.



Transforming legacy applications, especially mainframes, using APIs can benefit many organizations. It allows businesses to extend the life and utility of their legacy systems while embracing the advantages of modern technology. When done correctly, this approach ensures that legacy systems' invaluable business logic and data remain central to an organization's IT ecosystem, even in the age of digital transformation.

[Importance of API security](contents.xhtml#sc2_29a)

We will end this chapter on one of the most critical aspects of API design for the architect: API security. In this age of cloud computing and the ever-expanding digital ecosystem, APIs are used for all kinds of transactions, some with extremely sensitive data being transferred. Hackers try to probe APIs for all sorts of vulnerabilities, and the architect must ensure that API security is a critical principle embraced throughout the design and implementation of APIs.

There are some fundamental principles (non-exhaustive) that need to be applied for robust API security:

  * Data integrity and confidentiality: Ensuring that the data exchanged by APIs is unaltered and confidential is a cornerstone of API security. Any breach or alteration (intentional or unintentional) might lead to the exposure of sensitive information, including user credentials, financial data, or even personal information.
  * System availability: A robust API security framework guards against Denial-of-Service (DoS) attacks that make systems unavailable. Uptime is critical to many systems, and APIs are frequent targets of hackers. Sound design principles for APIs and their implementation on infrastructure go a long way to mitigate this risk.
  * Authentication: Users of an API (systems, other services, users) must be appropriately identified and verified before requests are accepted, processed, and replied to. This is especially important when the underlying data is sensitive. Companies frequently enforce different authentication tiers depending on the user access level required.
  * Traceability: The usage of APIs can and should be logged, and traceability should be enforced. This will help identify the kind of operations requested and the identity of the requested user.



As suggested above, neglecting API security can lead to significant consequences, both tangible and intangible. Thus, a robust API security strategy is indispensable in today's software-driven world and cannot be an afterthought. Critical to this is a resilient approach to cyber threats as they evolve. Any design of an API should be able to defend against new types of attacks. We will examine this critical topic in Chapter 6, API Security.

[Conclusion](contents.xhtml#sc2_30a)

In this chapter, we set the background of an API and the concept of a consumer and a producer of an API. We then discussed why APIs are important, especially in today's world where APIs are monetized and increasingly companies are building their platform around APIs. We explore a common pattern for deploying APIs, the REST pattern, and its importance in the last few years. This chapter has laid the foundation for the rest of the book as we dive deep into the toolkit essential to a robust architecture for today's API first world. 

In the next chapter, we expand our understanding of REST and discuss a few more API architectural styles.

[Key terms](contents.xhtml#sc2_31a)

  * Application Programming Interfaces (API): A set of rules and protocols enabling software applications to communicate and exchange data.
  * API-first design: API-first design is an approach where the API is designed before the software application itself, ensuring seamless integration and consistency. It prioritizes the API as the foundation of application development.
  * API Economy: The API economy refers to the commercial use of APIs as a business model. It revolves around the sale, consumption, and monetization of specialized APIs.
  * API specifications: API specifications are the defined rules and protocols for building and interacting with an API.
  * API contract: A formal agreement between the API provider and consumer that defines the API's functionality, performance, and usage terms.
  * API consumer: A consumer is a service, application, or system that accesses and uses services or the data of a service provided by an API.
  * API producer: An API producer is an entity, system, service, or application that designs, develops, maintains, and distributes APIs for other entities or systems to consume (API consumers).
  * REST: REpresentational State Transfer is a software architecture style for designing networked applications.
  * RESTful: RESTful refers to the implementation of the REST architectural style.
  * JavaScript Object Notation (JSON): A lightweight data-interchange format used in APIs for transmitting structured data.
  * Extensible Markup Language (XML): A markup language for encoding data in a structured, hierarchical format.


  * API monetization: The process of generating revenue from an API through various business models, such as subscriptions, pay-per-use, or revenue sharing.




# [CHAPTER 2Beyond REST](contents.xhtml#ch02a)

[Introduction](contents.xhtml#sc2_32a)

While RESTful APIs have become the de facto standard for web APIs, Application Programming Interface (API) architecture has constantly evolved. REST has been popular due to its simplicity, scalability, and maintainability. The stateless nature, client-server architecture, and standard HTTP methods are intuitive and scalable. As a result, it has resonated with developers, allowing for easy adoption and fostering a new generation of web-based applications.

However, digital ecosystems have evolved, requiring more unique architectures and protocols beyond REST. This chapter will cover these new paradigms, their strengths, and applications, and how they address REST imitations. This chapter will give you insights into the shifting landscape of API design and equip you with concepts to choose and design the best-fit solution for your applications.

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_33a)

In this chapter, we will be discussing the following topics:

  * Evolving digital ecosystems
  * Review of REST
  * GraphQL: A flexible architecture
  * gRPC: Efficient communication for microservices
  * Open Data Protocol
  * Other emerging API architectures
  * Transitioning from REST
  * Future outlook



[Objectives](contents.xhtml#sc2_34a)

While REST architecture for APIs is widely used, organization needs have changed over time. Alternative architectures are now available for API producers and consumers. In this chapter, we will examine some challenges enterprises face when using REST.

We will also discuss some alternatives available in API architectures (GraphQL, gRPC, OData, SOAP, etc.) in greater detail in this chapter and [Chapter 4](ch04.xhtml), Crafting APIs. At the end of this chapter, you will understand the limitations of REST architectures, other alternate API architectures, and some factors to consider if you are considering moving from REST to one of these different architectures.

[Evolving digital ecosystems](contents.xhtml#sc2_35a)

The past two decades have witnessed a monumental rise in digital complexity. From monolithic architectures, the world moved to microservices, decoupled modules, and serverless architectures. In addition, the volume and diversity of data has grown exponentially. Today’s user demands real-time, relevant, and dynamic data. This has led to the proliferation of real-time applications and interactive web services.

These applications have intricate requirements and have begun to stretch the principles upon which REST was built. The one-size-fits-all approach, though robust, sometimes lacks the finesse and agility that these newer applications demand.

[Review of REST](contents.xhtml#sc2_36a)

In the previous chapter, we briefly discussed REST and the fundamental principles that govern RESTful APIs.

RESTful APIs are an architectural style for designing and building applications that communicate over networks using a client-server paradigm. They rely on a stateless and cacheable communications protocol. It leverages existing web standards and protocols making it easy to implement and consume RESTful APIs in various platforms and languages. In most cases, the HTTP protocol is used for communications.

This style of API exposes a set of resources that are consumed by applications needing that particular business functionality.

The most common HTTP methods used in RESTful APIs are:

  * GET: Retrieve a resource or a collection of resources.
  * POST: Create a new resource.
  * PUT: Update an existing resource.
  * DELETE: Delete a resource.



RESTful APIs use HTTP status codes to indicate the success or failure of a request.

An example of REST architecture for making a RESTful API call depicting the different components is shown as follows:

Figure 2.1: Example RESTful API call

RESTful APIs have proven their usefulness ever since they were introduced. However, it does have its challenges that modern applications need to address.

[Limitations of REST](contents.xhtml#sc3_37a)

Despite all the advantages of REST mentioned earlier in the chapter, it has its limitations. As web technology has evolved, especially with the proliferation of cloud computing, real-time applications, and heightened security concerns, REST's constraints have become more pronounced. This section delves into the limitations of REST and discusses practical challenges, security issues, and the implications of these constraints in a cloud-centric world.

With the emergence of social media platforms, developers found that existing API architectures, like REST, had practical limitations. They were too lengthy and structured, making it hard to produce unstructured news feeds efficiently.

We will examine some key challenges that REST users face:

  * Fixed-structure data exchange: The REST API usually follows a fixed structure to receive a response. While this rigid structure makes it easy to use, it is often inefficient. A flexible structure adds complexity to both the service's producer and consumer. Hence, some trade-offs need to be considered.
  * Inflexible data format: REST typically communicates using JSON or XML, which may not always be the most efficient format for all use cases. Businesses dealing with multimedia or binary data might find this restrictive.
  * Data over-fetching and under-fetching: When consumers make an API request, they have no control over the amount and format of data they receive. In many cases, it receives more data than it needs (over-fetching) or less data than required (under-fetching).

The following is a REST example using the GET method:

GET https://api.example.com/books/list

will return all books and all the attributes that the publisher sends, even if we are only interested in the book's title.

This is not an efficient use of resources. In cloud environments, over-fetching can lead to unnecessary costs and reduced performance. Under fetching, however, might require multiple API calls, introducing latency, which is detrimental to a sound user experience. While this is not necessarily due to the requirements of the REST framework, the implementation could exacerbate this challenge.

  * Multiple round trips for related data: REST APIs often require clients to make multiple requests to retrieve data, slowing down applications.



For example, a call using the GET method to a REST API:

GET https:///books/author/5 // Fetch an author

GET https:///authors/5/books // Fetch books by that author

While one could write an API that allows for both options, it does remove the simplicity of the API.

  * Lack of real-time capabilities: REST is traditionally request/response based and hence natively does not support push-based data or real data streaming. Applications requiring real-time updates are hampered by using REST, e.g., live sports updates, financial stock prices, etc. Utilizing services like AWS Lambda or Azure functions in a cloud environment becomes challenging due to REST's lack of real-time support.
  * Security concerns: REST does not inherently prescribe security but relies heavily on underlying protocols, usually http or https. While https provides data encryption, REST does not define authentication or authorization, instead relying on API keys, tokens, or other mechanisms. If not implemented correctly, REST's reliance on external mechanisms poses a risk due to the lack of in-built security features and heavy dependence on external mechanisms.
  * Versioning and evolution: REST does not prescribe a standard or default mechanism for versioning. In today's fast-evolving API landscape, this can lead to challenges with each producer utilizing different standards for versioning, leading to an inconsistent approach. Services often evolve independently, and the lack of a consistent versioning strategy leads to compatibility issues, resulting in service failures.

The following URL example shows a simple example to depict the different paths for two different versions of the same API:

http://api.example.com/v1/users //Version 1

http://api.example.com/v2/users //Version 2

The above shows two different URLs with different API implementations at those two URLs.

  * Inefficient support for non-CRUD operations: REST’s design revolves around standard Create, Read, Update, Delete (CRUD) operations. This sometimes does not fit well with actions that do not align with these operations. This can lead to non-intuitive API endpoints or workarounds that distort the RESTful principles.



For example:

POST /users/1/resetPassword // An action that doesn't fit into CRUD operations

  * Statelessness: While statelessness ensures scalability, it also means that all required information must be sent with every request.

This can be inefficient for businesses with frequent transactions or interactions and introduce redundant data transfer.

  * Scalability with increased traffic: Even with their stateless nature, scaling REST APIs during high-traffic periods can be challenging, especially if they are not designed with scalability in mind. Companies might face downtime or performance issues during traffic spikes. Hence, it is critical to design APIs considering future scalability requirements.
  * Testing under real-world conditions: APIs might behave differently under various conditions like network latency, server downtimes, or concurrent access.

Companies often struggle to replicate these real-world scenarios in a testing environment, leading to unexpected issues in production.

  * Maintaining test data: Good testing requires relevant and diverse data sets. However, managing, updating, and ensuring the integrity of test data over time becomes a challenge.
  * Chaining of requests: Some workflows might require a sequence of API calls, where the output of one API call serves as input for the next.

Testing such chained requests accurately and ensuring the whole workflow's integrity can be complex.

  * Security testing: Given the open nature of APIs, they are vulnerable to various security threats, like injections, Denial of Service (DoS) attacks, and data breaches.

Companies face challenges in thoroughly testing APIs against all possible security vulnerabilities and ensuring compliance with security standards.

  * Large number of parameters handling different HTTP verbs and status codes: RESTful services use various HTTP methods (GET, POST, PUT, DELETE, etc.) and can return numerous HTTP status codes.

Ensuring that every method and every possible response code has been tested under various scenarios can be tedious and often overlooked.

  * Versioning issues: As mentioned earlier, versioning is a challenge in RESTful services.

Testing across different API versions and ensuring backward compatibility adds another layer of complexity.

  * RESTful API testing: RESTful API testing must cater to several challenges that must be addressed upfront while designing the APIs. GraphQL, gRPC, etc., alleviate some of these challenges. We will mention a few key ones here and cover this topic more extensively in [Chapter 8](ch08.xhtml), API Testing.
    * Endpoint coverage: The large number of API endpoints due to the nature of REST APIs requires more effort. Even less frequently used ones need the same effort.
    * Authentication and authorization: Testing different authentication methods and user roles for access control requires a clear strategy for security testing.
    * HTTP status codes: Verifies that the API returns the correct status codes for various scenarios.
    * Data validation: Ensuring the API can handle invalid or unexpected data inputs.
    * Versioning: Managing tests for different API versions if it has multiple versions.
    * Large number of parameters in API testing: RESTful APIs often interact with complex systems, which means they can have numerous parameters for requests. The presence of multiple parameters poses several challenges, including an increase in test cases, performance issues, complex validation programming, and increased chances of errors.



While many of the challenges mentioned above can be mitigated using various methods, there is usually a trade-off to complexity in implementation. The architect must keep the implementation and usage complexity while developing the APIs.

REST has undoubtedly paved the way for the rise of web APIs, offering simplicity and a model closely aligned with the web's foundational protocol. However, as the technological landscape evolves, its limitations in cloud computing, real-time applications, and security have become increasingly evident. By understanding these limitations, we understand the need to seek more suitable alternatives or extensions to cater to modern application needs.

Several alternative architectures and protocols have evolved to address REST limitations or solve specific use cases.

To explain the alternative API architectures, we will use an example to depict the different ways of representing the schema, sending a request, and getting the data:

  * We will depict the schema for a payment.
  * We will represent a query to retrieve payment details based on a customer ID.
  * We will show the output of this request.



In a real-world example, we would have authentication and authorization to retrieve the information along with strong error checks.

In the examples, we will depict:

  * The consumer queries for a specific payment's details using its ID.
  * The server responds with the payment amount, date, recipient details, and bank name as requested by the consumer.



For a RESTful service, the endpoints typically reflect the resources in the system. In our example:

Restful endpoints

These are two example endpoints we will use:

Payments: http://example.com/api/payments

Recipients: http://example.com/api/recipients

RESTful request

To retrieve the details of a payment with ID 12345, you would make an HTTP GET request to:

[http://example.com/api/payments/12345](http://example.com/api/payments/12345)

RESTful response

The server would return a JSON response, such as:

{

"id": "12345",

"amount": 250.00,

"date": "2023-10-21",

"recipientId": "6789"

}

If you also need to get the details of the recipient, you have to make an additional request:

http://example.com/api/recipients/6789

{

"id": "6789",

"name": "John Doe",

"bank": "Bank of Imaginary",

"accountNumber": "1234567890"

}

[GraphQL: A flexible alternative](contents.xhtml#sc2_38a)

GraphQL was developed by Facebook (now Meta) in 2012. They released it as an open-source project. It is a query language for APIs and includes a runtime to execute those queries.

GraphQL has been described as:

A query language for your API

GraphQL is a query language for APIs and a runtime for fulfilling those queries with your existing data. GraphQL provides a complete and understandable description of the data in your API, gives clients the power to ask for exactly what they need and nothing more, makes it easier to evolve APIs over time, and enables powerful developer tools.1

While REST is an architectural concept for application communications, GraphQL is a specification with an API query language and a set of tools. It operates over a single endpoint using http. So unlike REST, which exposes several URLs, GraphQL exposes a single endpoint for all these interactions.

The core motivation behind GraphQL is flexibility. As applications have grown more complex, with users demanding real-time data updates and personalized experiences, there was a need to build an efficient, robust, and flexible data query language. GraphQL was designed to empower developers to request specific data, avoiding over-fetching or under-fetching of data.

A pictorial representation of a GraphQL sample architecture is represented in the following figure:

Figure 2.2: Example GraphQL architecture representation

A key point to note in the diagram above is that GraphQL can interact with different types of elements to retrieve the data, for example, data that a microservice retries or updates, data from legacy systems, or even another RESTful API.

The following might better explain REST vs GraphQL interaction.

In REST, multiple URLs might be used to access different resources. For example:

  * /users to get a list of users
  * /users/1 to get a specific user
  * /users/1/orders to get a user’s orders



In GraphQL, we can access all these resources with a single request to the GraphQL endpoint and specifying the structure of the data we need in the query:

query {

user(id: 1) {

name

orders {

id

total

}

}

}

[Key principles and characteristics of GraphQL](contents.xhtml#sc3_39a)

GraphQL is a specification, an API query language, and a set of tools with some guiding principles that govern its usage. Some of the key principles that guide the design of GraphQL-based architectures include:

  * Strongly typed: GraphQL schemas are specific to the type of data they provide. This ensures data consistency and data integrity.
  * Hierarchical structure: GraphQL queries are similar to the structure of JSON objects and are hierarchical. They are composed in a format similar to the output they produce.
  * Real-time data with subscriptions: GraphQL subscriptions enable real-time functionality by pushing data to clients as changes occur to the subscribed data.
  * Single endpoint: GraphQL typically exposes a single endpoint for all interactions.
  * Descriptive: Due to its strong type system, GraphQL allows you to query and understand the underlying schema. This introspection feature will enable us to query the schema and discover available queries, subscriptions, types, and fields in a specific GraphQL API.



The following figure shows the sequence diagram illustrating the interaction between an E-commerce website (Client), a GraphQL API Server, an Inventory Database, and a Payment Service in the context of querying product details and placing an order:

Figure 2.3: Sequence diagram for a GraphQL interaction

[Key differences between REST and GraphQL](contents.xhtml#sc3_40a)

The following table highlights the differences between some common REST and GraphQL characteristics:

Characteristic |  REST |  GraphQL  
---|---|---  
Endpoints |  Multiple endpoints for different requests. |  Usually, a single endpoint handles all queries and mutations.  
Over/Under fetching data |  Frequent over or under-fetching of data. Requests return a fixed structure. |  Consumers specify the exact data they need.  
Versioning |  There is no explicit version, and it is producer-dependent. |  Schema evolves over time, and old fields can be deprecated. There is no explicit versioning.  
Flexibility |  Structuring responses has limited flexibility. |  Very flexible. Allows consumers to shape the response structure.  
Introspection |  Not usually introspective, though can be heavily coded to add this, increasing code complexity. |  Inherent introspection, providing tools to inspect the schema.  
  
Table 2.1: Key differences between GraphQL and REST

The code example of a GraphQL schema snippet is as follows:

type Payment {

id: ID!

amount: Float!

currency: String!

date: String!

recipient: Recipient!

status: PaymentStatus!

}

type Recipient {

name: String!

bank: String!

accountNumber: String!

}

enum PaymentStatus {

PENDING

COMPLETED

FAILED

}

Suppose the consumer wants to retrieve the amount, date, recipient's name, and bank details for a specific payment with ID 12345. Refer to the following code:

{

payment(id: "12345") {

amount

date

recipient {

name

bank

}

}

}

And a possible response:

{

"data": {

"payment": {

"amount": 250.00,

"date": "2023-10-21",

"recipient": {

"name": "John Doe",

"bank": "Bank of ABC"

}

}

}

}

[Benefits of using GraphQL in enterprise applications](contents.xhtml#sc3_41a)

GraphQL brings a lot of advantages when developing complex applications. Some key benefits of using GraphQL are listed as follows:

  * Efficiency: A single request from a consumer retrieves all the necessary data. This reduces the need for multiple trips to fetch data.
  * Rapid iteration: Since there is no need to maintain multiple versions of an endpoint, developers can evolve the API faster. This ensures that consumer applications remain up to date.
  * Strongly typed systems: For enterprise systems with a vast number of data formats and requests, this ensures data consistency and helps catch errors early in the development cycle.
  * Improved developer experience: Some tools (e.g., GraphiQL) offer an interactive interface for developers to understand the schema, test queries, and view the documentation.
  * Reduced payload: Avoiding over-fetching or under-fetching of data helps applications minimize bandwidth usage, especially in a cloud infrastructure. This has the benefit of decreasing operational costs.
  * Extensibility and flexibility: GraphQL schemas can be easily extended by adding new types and fields without affecting existing queries. This allows incremental updates without sacrificing backward compatibility. Challenges and considerations while using GraphQL.



[Challenges to adopting GraphQL](contents.xhtml#sc3_42a)

While GraphQL has many advantages, it does pose a few challenges that the architect needs to consider before adoption:

  * Learning curve: GraphQL could be considered more intuitive than REST for many developers. However, its methodology, patterns, and tools still have a steep learning curve. This is more so for teams deeply entrenched in RESTful paradigms. REST architecture generally has a better ecosystem and is supported by many reference implementations.
  * Caching complexities: REST can use http caching mechanisms. However, traditional caching mechanisms do not directly apply to GraphQL APIs. Normalized caching strategies can be quite complex to implement.
  * Performance concerns: GraphQL queries require careful design. Without that, the queries can be costly, especially requests for deeply nested resources.
  * Overhead: For simple applications, GraphQL might introduce complexity, increased costs, and overheads that might not be justified.



GraphQL is a major step in the world of APIs. It offers flexibility and an efficient means to interact with the data. While it has its challenges, its benefits in enterprise applications are substantial.

The decision to adopt GraphQL should be based on enterprise needs, and with proper understanding and implementation, it can be a powerful tool.

[gRPC: A modern alternative](contents.xhtml#sc2_43a)

gRPC is a modern, open-source, high-performance Remote Procedure Call (RPC) framework. It was developed by Google and was intended to improve existing communication technologies and aims to push the envelope on speed, scalability, and productivity.

The motivation behind creating gRPC was the need for a robust RPC framework that could help create faster, standardized, and more efficient communication between services. Earlier RPC protocols were functional but lacked some of these characteristics.

gRPC is built on the idea of calling a function or a method on a remote server as if it were a local procedure call. The main difference is that the call is made over a network. gRPC also provides data using the Protocol Buffers data format rather than a big chunk of data.

The following figure is a simple depiction of a client-server interaction using gRPC:

Figure 2.4: Simple implementation of a gRPC client-server architecture

In the preceding diagram, the service can be in any language (e.g., C++ or Java). The gRPC stub is automatically generated based on the service definitions written in protobuf. It provides a simplified interface for clients to make remote method calls and handles the serialization/deserialization of data and sending requests to the server.

[Key principles and characteristics of gRPC](contents.xhtml#sc3_44a)

gRPC has a few principles that must be considered before adoption. A few of those are listed as follows:

  * Protocol Buffers as IDL: Protocol Buffers (protobuf) acts as the Interface Definition Language (IDL) for describing the service and as the message format for data serialization.
  * Streaming: Using gRPC, consumers and producers can read and write data in real-time, allowing bidirectional streaming.
  * Pluggable: gRPC architecture is designed so its components are interchangeable. This means that authentication mechanisms, load balancing, and even the underlying transport protocols can be modified and adapted to suit specific needs.
  * Built-in authentication: gRPC has built-in support for Secure Sockets Layer/Transport Layer Security (SSL/TLS), Application Layer Transport Security (ALTS), and token-based authentication with Google, but it also supports plugging in your authentication systems.
  * Language agnostic: Arguably, this is gRPC's strongest feature. This means that a server written in Java can easily support a client written in Python or any supported language.
  * Deadlines/timeouts: gRPC calls can have associated deadlines. This means a client can indicate to the server the time period to complete a call.
  * Compression: Diverse data gets transferred between applications, and gRPC supports protocol and payload-based compression, ensuring optimal data transfer.



Here is the sequence diagram illustrating the interaction between an e-commerce website (Client), a gRPC Server, an Inventory Database, and a Payment Service in the context of querying product details and placing an order using gRPC:

Figure 2.5: gRPC interaction diagram

[Key differences between REST and gRPC](contents.xhtml#sc3_45a)

The following table highlights the differences between some common REST and gRPC characteristics:

Characteristic |  REST |  gRPC  
---|---|---  
Data format |  Typically uses JSON. |  Uses protobufs (binary format).  
Communication protocol |  Typically uses http/1.1. |  Uses http/2.  
Streaming |  Mostly request response though custom variations for streaming exist. |  Native support for uni and bi-directional streaming.  
API design |  Follows CRUD via http verbs. |  RPC-oriented, method-driven rather than resource-driven.  
Performance |  Text format can be less efficient than binary. |  Generally, it is more efficient due to the binary format and usage of http/2.  
Error handling |  Typically relies on http status codes. |  Uses a rich set of standardized status codes.  
API contract |  Typically uses OpenAPI/Swagger for documentation. Custom specs do exist. |  Uses .proto files for strict API contracts and documentation.  
Browser support |  Native browser support. |  It is not supported natively in browsers, though gRPC-Web offers a workaround.  
Supported languages |  Supported widely through multiple libraries but is inconsistent and possibly incomplete. |  Extensive support with native libraries in many languages.  
Connection handling |  Stateless by design. Each request opens a new connection. |  It uses long-lived connections and can multiplex multiple streams over one connection.  
Headers and metadata |  Uses HTTP headers for metadata. |  Uses custom metadata, which is more flexible with HTTP/2 headers.  
Connection Handling  |  Stateless by design. Each request opens a new connection. |  Uses long-lived connections. Can multiplex multiple streams over one connection.  
  
Table 2.2: Key characteristics differences between gRPC and REST

For the same example of the payment in gRPC:

Protocol Buffers Definition (.proto file)

syntax = "proto3";

package financial;

// Define the Payment message type

message PaymentRequest {

string id = 1; // Payment ID

}

message PaymentResponse {

float amount = 1;

string date = 2;

Recipient recipient = 3;

}

message Recipient {

string name = 1;

string bank = 2;

string accountNumber = 3;

}

// Define the PaymentService

service PaymentService {

RPC GetPayment(PaymentRequest) returns (PaymentResponse);

}

In a client-server gRPC model, server implementation code in Python is shown as follows:

import financial_pb2

import financial_pb2_grpc

class PaymentService(financial_pb2_grpc.PaymentServiceServicer):

def GetPayment(self, request, context):

# For demonstration, let's return a static payment for ID "12345"

if request.id == "12345":

return financial_pb2.PaymentResponse(

amount=250.00,

date="2023-10-21",

recipient=financial_pb2.Recipient(

name= "John Doe",

bank= "Bank of Imaginary"

)

)

return None

Client request (again using Python)

import financial_pb2

import financial_pb2_grpc

# Create a channel and a stub to communicate with the server

channel = grpc.insecure_channel(‘localhost:50051’)

stub = financial_pb2_grpc.PaymentServiceStub(channel)

# Make a request to the server

response = stub.GetPayment(financial_pb2.PaymentRequest(id="12345"))

# Print the response

print(response.amount)

print(response.date)

print(response.recipient.name)

print(response.recipient.bank)

Let us assume the server is running and listening on localhost:50051, the client would request and print out the payment details with ID "12345".

[Benefits of using gRPC in enterprise applications](contents.xhtml#sc3_46a)

The following benefits of using gRPC should be considered before this protocol is adopted for an application:

  * Performance: Binary serialization and efficient protocols can lead to faster data transfers, an essential requirement for enterprise applications.
  * Language independence: Enterprises usually have a diverse technology stack. With its support for multiple languages, gRPC ensures seamless integration across different platforms.
  * Rich communication: The bi-directional streaming feature allows for complex use cases, including real-time updates, chat applications, etc.
  * Tight API contracts: API contracts in gRPC are extremely tight due to the use of protobuf. This ensures consistency in communications.
  * Deadlines and cancellations: In an enterprise setup, some tasks might be time-sensitive, and gRPC's support for deadlines ensures adherence to these deadlines.

Automatic code generation: gRPC uses protobuf as its IDL. When the service and message structures are defined in a .proto file, gRPC tools can automatically generate client and server code in various programming languages (e.g., Python, Java, C++, etc.).




[Challenges to adopting gRPC](contents.xhtml#sc3_47a)

Some factors should be considered before adopting gRPC, as shown below:

  * Learning curve: Despite its many advantages, gRPC has a steeper learning curve, especially for developers transitioning from REST architecture.
  * Limited browser support: While gRPC-Web attempts to bring gRPC to the web, it is not supported natively in all browsers.
  * Debugging: Binary formats are efficient but much more challenging to debug than text formats like JSON and XML.
  * Mature tooling: REST tools are much more mature due to their adoption rates spanning many years. gRPC ecosystem is gaining traction but still lacks REST tools.
  * Interoperability: For internal service-to-service calls, gRPC might be an excellent choice; however, REST might still be the preferred choice for external APIs due to broader adoption.



[Advanced features of gRPC](contents.xhtml#sc3_48a)

gRPC has some features that make it very suitable for certain services. A brief description of these features can help understand when to utilize gRPC:

  * Streaming capabilities
    * Server-side streaming: The server can transmit a continuous flow of messages in reaction to a request from the client.
    * Client-side streaming: The client can send a stream of messages to a server.
    * Bidirectional streaming: Both client and server can send a stream of messages to each other independently.
  * Interceptors and middleware
    * Interceptors: Customize request/response processing for logging, authentication, and more.
    * Middleware integration: Facilitate the integration of gRPC with existing middleware solutions for monitoring, tracing, etc.


  * Deadlines, timeouts, and cancellation
    * Deadlines and timeouts: Set maximum times for requests to complete, preventing resource exhaustion. While this is usually done on the client side, it does require server-side coordination.
    * Cancellation propagation: Allows the cancellation of in-flight calls, which is helpful in microservices where a failure in one service can cascade.



[Best practices for gRPC development](contents.xhtml#sc3_49a)

There are some best practices that should be followed while developing using gRPC. Some of these leading practices include:

  * Efficient schema design:
    * Optimizing protobuf definitions: Designing efficient protobuf schemas to minimize payload size and parsing overhead.
    * Backward and forward compatibility: Ensuring changes to the .proto files are compatible with older and newer service versions.
  * Error handling:
    * Rich error model: Utilizing gRPC's rich error model to communicate detailed information about failures.
    * Consistent error handling: Implementing a consistent strategy for handling errors across different services.
  * Security considerations:
    * Transport security: Implementing TLS to secure data in transit.
    * Authentication and authorization: Using built-in mechanisms in gRPC to authenticate and authorize clients.



[Open Data Protocol](contents.xhtml#sc2_50a)

Open Data Protocol (OData) is a standardized protocol that helps to facilitate the production and consumption of queryable and interoperable RESTful APIs. Microsoft initiated OData in 2007, although it has been open-sourced since then. OData builds on http and JSON using URIs to address and access data feed services.

More details can be found here: [https://www.odata.org/](https://www.odata.org/)

An important use case for OData was simplifying how data is shared across the web, irrespective of the platform or the device. With the proliferation of cloud services, mobile and IoT devices, and the need for real-time data, there was a need for a protocol that could seamlessly integrate with various data models and offer a consistent experience to end users and developers.

OData is built on REST principles. It inherently supports CRUD operations using standard http methods. It extends these capabilities by offering support for querying data, which is significant when dealing with large datasets.

A simple OData architecture representation showing the key components and their interaction is shown as follows:

Figure 2.6: OData consuming application

The picture above explains the following interactions:

  * The communication between the OData client and the OData service happens over HTTP/HTTPS.
  * It uses standard HTTP methods (GET, POST, PUT, DELETE) and OData-specific query options and conventions.
  * The OData services act as an intermediary between the data sources and the OData client by exposing the data as a set of OData resources.



[Key principles and characteristics of OData](contents.xhtml#sc3_51a)

Some key points about OData that characterize its operations include:

  * Standardized protocol: OData provides a consistent and standard convention for representing and interacting with data.
  * Interoperability: OData is platform agnostic and can be used across various platforms and devices.
  * Support for CRUD operations: OData supports the familiar CRUD operations using standard http verbs.
  * Rich querying: OData offers a rich set of querying options, including filters, sort capabilities, and paginations.
  * Metadata: OData exposes a metadata document that describes the data model. This allows clients to easily understand and interact with the data dynamically.



The following figure depicts the OData service being used in an e-commerce website:

Figure 2.7: OData sequence diagram

[Key differences between REST and OData](contents.xhtml#sc3_52a)

We examine some key differences between REST and OData in the following table:

Characteristic |  REST |  OData  
---|---|---  
Querying capabilities |  Limited (depends on implementation) |  Rich querying options  
Metadata |  Not standard |  Exposes metadata about the data model  
Standardization |  Architectural style without strict conventions |  Standardized way to represent and use data  
  
Table 2.3: Key differences between REST and OData

OData services define their structure and capabilities in a service metadata document. The following code shows an example of such a metadata document:

<edmx:Edmx Version="4.0" xmlns:edmx="http://docs.oasis-open.org/odata/ns/edmx">

<edmx:DataServices>

<Schema Namespace="Financial" xmlns="http://docs.oasis-open.org/odata/ns/edm">

<EntityType Name="Payment">

<Key>

<PropertyRef Name= "ID"/>

</Key>

<Property Name="ID" Type="Edm.String" Nullable=" false"/>

<Property Name="Amount" Type="Edm.Double" Nullable=" false"/>

<Property Name="Date" Type="Edm.Date" Nullable=" false"/>

<NavigationProperty Name="Recipient" Type="Financial.Recipient"/>

</EntityType>

<EntityType Name="Recipient">

<Property Name="Name" Type="Edm.String"/>

<Property Name="Bank" Type="Edm.String"/>

</EntityType>

<EntityContainer Name="FinancialContainer">

<EntitySet Name="Payments" EntityType="Financial.Payment">

<NavigationPropertyBinding Path="Recipient" Target="Recipients"/>

</EntitySet>

<EntitySet Name="Recipients" EntityType="Financial.Recipient"/>

</EntityContainer>

</Schema>

</edmx:DataServices>

</edmx:Edmx>

OData service endpoint

An OData service will typically expose its data at specific URLs or endpoints. Assuming our base service URL is [http://example.com/odata](http://example.com/odata), we might have the following:

Payments: http://example.com/odata/Payments

Recipients: http://example.com/odata/Recipients

OData query
