To get the details of a payment with ID 12345, including the recipient details, you would make an HTTP GET request to:

[http://example.com/odata/Payments('12345')?$expand=Recipient](http://example.com/odata/Payments\('12345'\)?$expand=Recipient)

The $expand=Recipient part of the URL tells the OData service to include the related recipient entity in the response.

OData response

The server would return a JSON (or XML, if requested) response like:

{

"@odata.context": "http://example.com/odata/$metadata#Payments/$entity",

"ID": "12345",

"Amount": 250.00,

"Date": "2023-10-21",

"Recipient": {

"Name": "John Doe",

"Bank": "Imaginary Bank"

}

}

OData provides much more functionality, such as filtering, ordering, and pagination. It is worth noting that while OData gives you this flexibility, it also requires a more rigid structure and adherence to its conventions.

One of the key differences between this example and the GraphQL or RESTful examples is the number of requests. GraphQL gets all the necessary data in a single request. OData also allows this with the $expand parameter. However, with traditional REST, you may have to make multiple requests to gather data from related resources.

In real-world scenarios, it is common to see optimizations where specific endpoints might aggregate data to minimize these extra requests based on common use cases. For example, there might be an endpoint like:

http://example.com/api/payments/12345/withRecipient 

This provides both payment and recipient data in a single response.

[Benefits of using OData in enterprise applications](contents.xhtml#sc3_53a)

Consider the following benefits of using OData to help make a decision:

  * Consistency: OData's consistent data access across platforms is a prominent feature for use in enterprise ecosystems.
  * Reduced development time: With standardized conventions, developers can reduce their time developing and integrating APIs.
  * Flexibility: OData's rich querying capabilities allow clients to retrieve needed data, reducing data transfer loads.
  * Integration: OData can easily integrate with various platforms and is a key feature for interaction with multiple systems.



[Challenges to using OData](contents.xhtml#sc3_54a)

While OData has some great benefits, the downsides must be considered before adopting it. Some of these are listed as follows:

  * Performance: Extensive querying will lead to performance issues if not implemented efficiently.
  * Learning curve: OData has a steep learning curve for developers unfamiliar with the protocol.
  * Over-reliance on standardization: The strict conventions of OData can arguably limit developers' flexibility.
  * Security: OData deals with data; proper authentication and authorization mechanisms must be implemented cautiously.



OData offers a robust and standardized protocol for creating and consuming data APIs. While they bring many benefits, it is crucial to understand the context in which they will be implemented in an enterprise to determine suitability. Compared to other protocols, OData has not picked up steam.

[Other emerging API architectures](contents.xhtml#sc2_55a)

There are a few other API frameworks that are of importance to the API architect. Some of them are:

  * Websockets: Real-time bidirectional communication.
  * Server-Sent Events (SSE): This is a streamlined method for servers to send real-time client updates.
  * Event-driven architectures: This architecture allows applications to revolve around the reaction to events as they occur.



We will examine these in greater detail in [Chapter 12](ch12.xhtml), Real-time, Asynchronous, and Streaming API’s.

[Transitioning from REST](contents.xhtml#sc2_56a)

Transitioning from an established architectural style like REST to another architectural style is never easy and should be evaluated on its merits based on a few criteria. In many cases, REST might have to co-exist with other frameworks with specific needs. This section will examine the key considerations when contemplating such a transition and the hurdles organizations might face.

[Analyzing existing infrastructure and dependencies](contents.xhtml#sc3_57a)

Understanding the current ecosystem is essential to making the right decision. The following items should be considered before the selection of the right architectural style:

  * Existing API consumers: Identification of consumers of APIs, including internal systems, third-party integrations, and external users.
  * Dependencies: External dependencies and their readiness to change to new API architectures may deter architecture revamp.
  * Middleware and tools: Evaluate existing tools to determine their specificity to RESTful services (e.g., monitoring, logging).
  * Documentation: Assess the quality and comprehensiveness of the current API documentation. Understanding the need for change will guide the transition to a new architectural style.
  * Historical data: A review of the metrics, error logs, usage patterns, etc., will provide insights into the advantages and disadvantages of the current environment. Some metrics to look for are high measured latencies, inefficient data retrieval, high error rates, and increased payload response sizes, leading to higher bandwidth usage and scalability bottlenecks due to REST API statelessness.



[Evaluating the technical and business requirements](contents.xhtml#sc4_58a)

The push for transition should come from tangible requirements. The following are some aspects that should be considered before selecting the architectural style:

  * Performance: For microservices talking to each other, a protocol like gRPC with its binary format might offer superior performance to REST.
  * Data fetching: If under-fetching or over-fetching is a current concern, GraphQL might be an excellent alternative.
  * Real-time requirements: Web sockets could be considered over REST if real-time updates are essential.
  * Business growth: What architecture would be most suited for future scaling needs is a question that will help narrow the choice of future API architectures.
  * Stakeholder expectations: Sometimes, the push might be non-technical. Third-party partners and other business units might push for a different architecture to suit their purposes.



[Balancing multiple API styles](contents.xhtml#sc4_59a)

As organizations grow and transition, they might find themselves in a multiple API architecture environment where they simultaneously support REST and other architectures. In these situations, the following discipline should be introduced to the organization:

  * Unified documentation: Maintaining consistent documentation across architecture styles and tools is complex and needs to be planned. This will ensure a consistent experience for developers.
  * Interoperability: Tools and middleware supporting multiple API styles should always be preferred.
  * Training: Teams (producers and consumers) might be well versed in one style, e.g., REST; hence, investing in training across new architectures is essential.
  * Resource allocation: A balancing act in splitting resources across new and old architectural styles is essential.



[Selection of API architecture](contents.xhtml#sc3_60a)

Selecting a software ecostyle requires architectural decisions that need to consider future requirements. APIs are the vital mediator in systems decoupling, modularizing, and ensuring smooth evolution. We have examined some aspects of choosing the right API architecture style.

However, other factors must also be considered, like team expertise, future growth, and the appetite to introduce new technologies with their associated challenges. Making the right API choices will establish an adaptable, resilient, and efficient ecosystem.

[Future outlook](contents.xhtml#sc2_61a)

As we look ahead, several emerging trends and technologies will reshape how we think about and design APIs. We touch upon a few topics, some of which will be evaluated in subsequent chapters:

  * Edge computing: There is a proliferation of Internet of Things (IoT) devices and the need for fast processing of large data sets. As a result, edge computing and its interaction within the software ecosystem have become extremely important. Instead of relying on centralized data centers, edge computing shifts processing closer to the source of generation. This has implications for API design, such as:
    * Latency reduction: APIs designed for edge computing must prioritize low latency to ensure real-time data processing and responsiveness.
    * Localized decision-making: APIs must facilitate decision-making at the edge, allowing devices to operate independently even when disconnected from the main network.
  * Decentralized web: The decentralized web, sometimes called Web3, aims to create a user-centric web where users control all aspects of their data, including identities and transactions. This decentralization can be achieved using peer-to-peer networks and blockchain technologies.

The implications for API design include:

    * Interoperability: APIs must interact seamlessly with various decentralized platforms and protocols.
    * Data independence: APIs will be crucial in ensuring data access, transfer, additions, and deletions in line with user preferences and decentralized protocols.



[Conclusion](contents.xhtml#sc2_62a)

REST architectural style has become the de facto standard for many API interface developers in the enterprise world. However, with the challenges that REST has, alternative styles have evolved. We examined some of the shortfalls of REST as it relates to enterprise applications. In this chapter, we looked at GraphQL, gRPC, and OData. We will evaluate some other advanced styles in [Chapter 4](ch04.xhtml), Crafting APIs.

While different API architectural styles exist, and the important ones are covered in this book, it is important to understand that almost all API vendors provide some form of RESTful API support due to the large ecosystem that exists around this architectural style.

We take an example of interface schema, request, and response across all three API architectures to give readers a flavor of the different ways of developing for that architecture.

The process of enterprises moving to another API architecture is not easy, and we lay down a non-exhaustive list of factors that enterprises need to examine. Choosing alternatives also depends significantly on other non-technical factors and is not easy. It is the most likely situation that most enterprises will have a hybrid environment where different API architectures will reside next to REST. It becomes vital for an organization to ensure that consistent practices are followed in the development cycle and documentation, testing, etc.

At the end of this chapter, you should have a good appreciation of the various features of the different API architectural styles and a comparison of its features to the REST style. This also sets the foundation for our subsequent chapters.

[Key terms](contents.xhtml#sc2_63a)

  * gRPC: A high-performance, open-source framework for RPC using protobuf for efficient serialization.
  * GraphQL: A query language and runtime for APIs allowing clients to request specific data in a single request.
  * Statelessness: A design principle where each API request contains all necessary information, with no client state stored on the server.
  * Open Data Protocol (OData): A standardized protocol for building and consuming RESTful APIs with advanced querying capabilities.
  * Protocol Buffers: A language-agnostic data serialization format used by gRPC for defining service interfaces and message types.
  * WebSockets: A communication protocol enabling full-duplex, real-time interaction between client and server over a single TCP connection.

# [CHAPTER 3Fundamentals of API Design](contents.xhtml#ch03a)

[Introduction](contents.xhtml#sc2_64a)

In the last couple of chapters, we have gone through the history of APIs, examined the different architectural styles, and then presented examples of other ways of representing APIs in various styles.

In this chapter, we will talk about API design standards. These guiding principles, guidelines, and best practices help developers create consistent, usable, scalable, and maintainable APIs. These standards are formulated to make it easy to understand, use, and integrate with other applications.

We will start by putting together a set of guiding principles for API design that should help guide us from the concept to implementing an API. We will also discuss other factors impacting API design, such as naming conventions, URL structures, different HTTP standards, etc. We will also talk about some common API design patterns, which are reusable solutions that address common challenges to API development.

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_65a)

In this chapter, we will discuss the following topics:

  * Guiding principles for API design
  * API design standards
  * API development best practices
  * Design phase
  * Practical API design



[Objectives](contents.xhtml#sc2_66a)

API lifecycle management is creating, deploying, maintaining, and retiring APIs. It includes stages like design, development, monitoring, and management of APIs. The design phase for an API is part of this extensive API lifecycle management.

The design phase is very important and sets the tone for the rest of the API development. API design is one of the components needed for a successful API implementation.

Our objective in this chapter is to examine each topic that informs and guides the architect toward creating consistent, developer-friendly APIs that add value to an organization's API ecosystem. Common elements, such as security, encompass more than API design. Tools, such as the API gate, help maintain standards around API design. These elements are discussed in greater detail in subsequent chapters.

[Guiding principles for API design](contents.xhtml#sc2_67a)

The API development lifecycle has multiple phases. The API design phase consists of substeps and is depicted in the following figure as part of the overall lifecycle:

Figure 3.1: API design as part of the overall API development

To be consistent across all APIs, an API must be built around a set of design principles. These principles enable the design of consistent and reusable APIs. They underpin effective API design and ensure the developed APIs are functional, user-friendly, secure, and reusable.

The principles can be guided by high-level objectives (e.g., usability, consistency, etc.) as depicted in the following figure:

Figure 3.2: Guiding principles

The following section explains the principles and their characteristics in detail.

[Usability](contents.xhtml#sc3_68a)

The usability design principle's objective is to make the API easy to understand to the developers who will integrate it into their applications. A self-service portal for consumers to utilize these APIs goes a long way in fulfilling this objective. For a developer to have an easy-to-use API, it needs a few characteristics, which are as follows:

  * The API should have an intuitive design and should be easy to understand for developers, even if they are unfamiliar with the specific domain that the service is serving. A good API is usually easy to use.
  * For an API to be usable, it should have clear documentation. Using tools like Swagger, example-driven searchable documentation presented in an easy-to-understand manner is very helpful.


  * Any API implementation should provide clear and precise error messages, including error codes when something does not work. This will help developers identify, debug, and fix errors when using the APIs.



[Consistency](contents.xhtml#sc3_69a)

The consistency principle dictates that APIs should behave predictably and be consistent in all aspects of their definition.

The key aspects of consistency include:

  * Endpoints should follow a consistent naming convention, structure, and behavior. Uniform endpoints help in maintaining consistency in the code.
  * APIs deal with data and maintaining standardized data formats (JSON, XML, or other formats), ensuring adherence to consistency.
  * When working with different endpoints, each endpoint should ensure predictable behavior. Similar actions, errors, and other actions should be handled consistently across the API ecosystem.



Additionally, consistent naming conventions, parameter order, and response formats ensure the API is predictable and more manageable.

[Simplicity](contents.xhtml#sc3_70a)

Any API should be easy to use with a clean and intuitive design and avoid unnecessary complexity. By emphasizing that APIs are as straightforward as possible, they are more accessible to developers and promote wider acceptance.

The following concepts should be implemented in API designs to promote simplicity:

  * Only provide the endpoints necessary for the API's functionality. Having minimal endpoints keeps the interface simple and clean.
  * Every request from a client to an API should be self-contained. This is called statelessness and ensures that the client always provides all the information necessary to process the request when calling an API.
  * There is a need to avoid deeply nested resources. With a flat structure, API developers do not need to take special precautions, as in the case of nested calls.



[Flexibility](contents.xhtml#sc3_71a)

APIs need to evolve and adapt to changing business requirements without disrupting the service. There are multiple methods by which APIs handle flexibility. Some key characteristics promoting flexibility include:

  * Designing the APIs with extensibility ensures that new features and data can be added without a major rewrite or disruption to the service.
  * One of the easiest methods to achieve flexibility in the API design is to have a decoupled architecture. Here, different business services are decoupled with one API serving each service so that changes in one API do not impact the other API.
  * Versioning is an essential aspect of APIs and helps them constantly be upgraded. Implementing a consistent versioning approach to APIs allows updates and transitions to new ones without breaking existing integrations.

A subset of versioning is to ensure backward compatibility between versions of APIs. This ensures that existing clients do not break without adequate notice.




[Security](contents.xhtml#sc3_72a)

Any API should always be designed with security as a key principle. Incorporating the proper security measures ensures the API is protected against potential attacks.

There are several ways security can be enshrined as part of the development process:

  * Incorporate proper measures for authentication and authentication.
  * Use data encryption for both data in rest and data in motion (TLS, for example)
  * Protect against some common attacks by enforcing rate limits on API usage.
  * API should enforce input validation and sanitize all input to protect against injection-style attacks.



[Modularity](contents.xhtml#sc3_73a)

APIs that are divided into logical sections and separate the different functions promote modularity. This ensures that every API provides specific functionality. It has the following key features:

  * Modularity allows for the reusability of APIs. A modular API with fine-grained service enables different projects to use APIs for specific functionality, reducing operational and maintenance costs.
  * This helps maintainability as updating or modifying APIs does not impact other APIs.
  * Modular APIs are also easier to scale with growing demand. Scalability of only high-demand APIs also reduces overall costs.
  * Modular APIs are also more reliable as the modular nature of the APIs makes them less bloated.



These principles act as a guide when designing APIs. They help create APIs that are easy to use, maintainable, and scalable and provide a better development experience for developers.

[API design standards](contents.xhtml#sc2_74a)

When designing APIs, it is a good practice to follow some specific design standards. These could include naming conventions, URL structure, HTTP standards, etc. The following section will discuss some of these design standards and offer examples of industry best practices.

[Naming conventions](contents.xhtml#sc3_75a)

A diverse set of users, internal and external, can use APIs. Having clear and consistent naming conventions becomes critical for developers to understand APIs intuitively. In addition, having a consistent and clear naming convention acts as a self-documenting feature and enhances the understandability and usability of the API.

Some key principles that promote consistent naming include:

  * Descriptive and consistent names: Aim for clear and concise names to reflect the purpose of the resource, method, and other API elements. For example, a descriptive name could be product_inventory or user_profiles.
  * Nouns for resources: Following RESTful principles, using plural nouns for resources (e.g., users, products) is recommended. This helps users naturally conceptualize these entities.
  * Verbs for methods: These define actions that can be performed on resources. Use verbs like GET, POST, PUT, and DELETE to adhere to HTTP standards. It is also a good practice to use descriptive names for custom methods. For example, GET /users retrieves all users, and POST /products creates a new product.
  * Case sensitivity: Consistent case sensitivity is important for developers as it ensures predictability. Some examples are camelCase and snake_case.
  * Versioning in names: It is best practice for an API to use versioning. Incorporating the version number into resource or method names allows developers to quickly identify and use specific API versions. For example, /v3/users will indicate resources related to this API in version 3 of the API.



[Uniform Resource Locators structure](contents.xhtml#sc3_76a)

Uniform Resource Locators (URLs) are the addresses for the API resources. Structuring the URL scheme consistently ensures the clarity and usability of the API. Some good practices for creating meaningful APIs are listed as follows:

  * Resource-oriented structure: RESTful APIs typically follow a resource-oriented URL structure. This means the URL reflects the hierarchy of resources within the API. Take the case of an API for managing a library. The URL /books might represent the collection of all books, while /books/123 could point to a specific book with an ID of 123.
  * Descriptive path components: Each segment within the URL path should be a descriptive noun representing a resource level. For example, in the library API, /books/{id}/reviews clearly indicates reviews associated with a specific book identified by its ID.
  * Versioning in URLs: As discussed earlier, versioning the API can be helpful. One approach is to incorporate the version number into the URL path. For example, /v2/books might indicate resources related to books within version 2 of your API.



Maintain consistency in your URL structure throughout the API. This makes it easier for developers to predict URL patterns for accessing different resources and functionalities.

[HTTP standards](contents.xhtml#sc3_77a)

HTTP standards establish a common communication language between the clients and the API producer (service). There are a few common HTTP standards that ensure interoperable APIs:

  * HTTP methods: These methods define the type of operation requested on a resource. The most common methods used in RESTful APIs include:
    * GET: Used to retrieve a resource.
    * POST: Used to create a new resource or trigger an action.
    * PUT: Used to update or replace a resource entirely.
    * PATCH: Used to apply partial modifications to a resource.
    * DELETE: Used to delete a resource.
  * Request and response headers: Headers act like additional information attached to requests and responses. Request headers often include information like authentication credentials or content type, while response headers might indicate the status code or content length.
  * Status codes: These are three-digit HTTP status codes. They are used to indicate the outcome of an API request. Standard status codes include:
    * 2xx: Successful requests (e.g., 200 OK, 201 Created)
    * 3xx: Redirection codes (e.g., 301 Moved Permanently)
    * 4xx: Client-side errors (e.g., 400 Bad Request, 404 Not Found)
    * 5xx: Server-side errors (e.g., 500 Internal Server Error)
  * Content negotiation: This concept allows clients and servers to negotiate the data format in requests and responses. APIs can specify supported content types (e.g., JSON, XML) and allow clients to indicate their preferences in the request headers.



[Error handling](contents.xhtml#sc3_78a)

In the real world, APIs can encounter unexpected outcomes leading to errors. Effective error handling is critical to the user-friendliness of the APIs when things go wrong.

Some fundamental principles to create informative and helpful responses are listed as follows:

  * Use standard HTTP status codes: As discussed earlier, HTTP status codes are a standardized way to communicate the result of an API request. Error responses should always indicate the appropriate status code. For example, the "500 Internal Server Error" code would indicate a server-side issue.
  * Provide clear error message: Informative, consistent, and tailored error messages help provide actionable steps to the developer to resolve the problem. For example, instead of the generic "Internal Server Error," it is more beneficial to indicate "Database connection failed. Please Retry". It is also helpful to include additional details with the error response, such as relevant log messages.
  * Developer-friendly vs. user-friendly: Users prefer more straightforward explanations for errors; however, developers need more technical details to resolve the issue. Finding the right balance is essential.



[API versioning](contents.xhtml#sc3_79a)

APIs require updates and changes. To not increase the number of unique APIs, versioning plays an integral part in managing these changes and ensuring compatibility with existing applications.

Some common approaches to versioning APIs include:

  * URL versioning: This approach incorporates the version number into the API URL path. For example, "/v1/users" might point to user resources within Version 1 of the API, while "/v2/users" could indicate users within Version 2.
  * Header versioning: The client specifies the desired API version in a request header. This approach keeps the URL structure clean but requires additional logic on the server side to handle requests based on the specified version.
  * Media type versioning: This approach uses different media types (e.g., application/vnd.yourapp-v1+json) to indicate the API version within the request headers. This method offers flexibility but might require additional configuration on the client side.
  * Clear versioning documentation: Maintaining clear and comprehensive documentation for each API version is critical. This documentation should outline the functionalities available in each version, highlight any breaking changes between versions, and provide migration guidance for developers using older versions.



[Consistent data formats](contents.xhtml#sc3_80a)

APIs need consistent data formats to ensure smooth data exchange between the clients and servers. Establishing and adhering to data formats enhances APIs' overall usability and maintainability and allows for easy and consistent internationalization of the APIs

Here are some of the most common data formats used in API design:

  * JavaScript Object Notation: A popular and human-readable format representing data using key-value pairs. The JavaScript Object Notation (JSON) format is widely used due to its simplicity.
  * Extensible Markup Language: A widely used format that uses tags and attributes to define data structure. While Extensible Markup Language (XML) offers more flexibility and validation capabilities than JSON, it can be more complex.
  * Comma-Separated Values: This is a simple text-based format. Data is organized in rows and columns, separated by commas. Comma-Separated Values (CSV) are often used for data exchange between different systems but can be less efficient for representing complex data structures.



[Structuring data exchange](contents.xhtml#sc3_81a)

The clients and the API primarily communicate through the request and response bodies. These bodies carry the data being exchanged and have established clear conventions for their structure, which is efficient and predictable.

Here are some key design principles to follow when designing request and response body conventions:

  * Clearly defined data structures: Document the expected data structure within request and response bodies. Define the meaning and data types of each key or attribute.
  * Validation rules: Specify validation rules for request data to ensure the API receives well-formed and expected data. This helps prevent processing errors and improves overall data integrity.
  * Error codes and messages: Standardize error codes and messages within response bodies. This allows clients to handle errors and provide informative feedback to users effectively.
  * Versioning considerations: If your API uses versioning, consider how request and response body conventions might evolve with different versions. Document these changes clearly and provide migration guidance for developers using older versions.



Here are some common formats for structuring request and response bodies:

  * JSON: As discussed earlier, JSON is a popular and human-readable format that utilizes key-value pairs to represent data. Its widespread adoption and ease of use make it a leading choice for structuring request and response bodies in many APIs.
  * XML: XML offers a more structured approach to data representation using tags and attributes. This can benefit complex data structures or when validation is a critical concern.
  * Protocol buffers: This language-neutral format allows for defining data structures that can be efficiently translated into various programming languages. Protocol buffers can improve performance and reduce payload sizes compared to text-based formats like JSON.



The choice of format depends on the needs and considerations of the application. JSON offers simplicity and readability, while XML provides more structure and validation capabilities. Protocol buffers can be advantageous for performance and cross-language compatibility.

[Statelessness](contents.xhtml#sc3_82a)

If an API needs to track every interaction with a client, store the session data, and then use those to service new requests, it will become very complex and hinder performance. Statelessness is a core principle in RESTful API design that offers a more efficient alternative.

Statelessness has its advantages in API design, including:

  * Independent requests: Each request from a client is treated as a new and isolated transaction. Every request contains all the necessary data to be processed independently. The server does not store any information on any of the requests.
  * Scalability: Adding more servers solves the problem of increased API traffic, as they do not have to worry about session management across multiple servers.
  * Performance: Stateless APIs are generally faster as they do not need to store and retrieve session data for each session.
  * Simplicity: Since session management is not performed on the server side, it reduces development overhead and is more straightforward to design.



There are other implications as stateless APIs might require larger request payloads as they must include all necessary data for all requests, resulting in possible performance delays.

[Caching](contents.xhtml#sc3_83a)

Caching is a technique used to store frequently accessed data in a temporary location to optimize API performance. Some common caching strategies include:

  * Client-side caching: Web browsers and client applications can cache API responses locally on the user's device. This reduces the need to fetch the data from the server repeatedly. It increases performance for subsequent requests and reduces network traffic. An example is a weather app that could cache API responses for hourly forecasts locally on the client. Cached data is displayed within that hour without making a new API call.
  * Server-side caching: The server can cache frequently requested data in its memory or a dedicated caching server. This is effective when the same data has to be served to multiple clients. For example, an e-commerce website could cache popular product details on the server. User requests for the same products fetch data from the cache instead of querying the database.
  * Cache-invalidation: While it is not necessarily a strategy, it is an important aspect of caching as it ensures that the cached data is up to date. Cache expiration times and other parameters ensure that the cache is flushed under these conditions and that fresh data is loaded.



[Documentation standards](contents.xhtml#sc3_84a)

Without documentation, an API remains a mystery and requires experimentation to be understandable and usable. Documentation or the lack of documentation is frequently the biggest hurdle to adopting an API.

Here are some key elements of effective API documentation:

  * Comprehensive reference guide: Provide a detailed reference and up-to-date guide explaining the available resources, methods, parameters, and data formats supported by the API. Clear examples with code snippets and usage patterns provide clear and concise explanations.
  * Integration guide: Step-by-step tutorials help developers integrate the APIs into applications. This should also demonstrate methods to achieve specific tasks with the API.
  * Interactive API design tools: Tools like Swagger, Postman, etc., allow developers to explore functionalities and test requests and responses. These tools significantly enhance the developer experience.



We discuss this in greater detail in [Chapter 9](ch09.xhtml), API documentation.

[API development best practices](contents.xhtml#sc2_85a)

Developing a robust and user-friendly API requires more than just technical know-how. It involves understanding the end-users, their needs, and their challenges. By adhering to the following good practices, developers can ensure that their APIs are reliable, efficient, and easy to use:

  * User-centric: Understand who will use your API and what they need. Design the API to solve real-world problems for these users.
  * Use RESTful principles: Representational State Transfer (REST) is an architectural style that uses standard conventions and stateless communication. Adhering to RESTful principles will make your API more intuitive and easier to integrate.
  * Provide clear documentation: Comprehensive documentation, complete with examples, is crucial. Tools like Swagger or Postman can help automate this process.
  * Implement versioning: As your API evolves, ensure that changes don't break existing integrations by using versioning. This can be achieved using URI versioning (e.g., /v1/users) or header versioning.



Some examples of companies that have APIs available include:

  * [https://docs.stripe.com/](https://docs.stripe.com/)
  * [https://developer.paypal.com/api/rest/](https://developer.paypal.com/api/rest/)



It could be a good example of API development best practices and the documentation they support.

[API deprecation strategies](contents.xhtml#sc3_86a)

Deprecation strategies provide a structured approach to retiring APIs with minimal disruption to existing users gracefully. Deprecation strategies allow teams to remove APIs in a controlled manner to ensure that bloated or obsolete APIs can be upgraded. Security risks in an API need to be addressed frequently; only deprecating these APIs might remove the security threat.

Here are some key steps involved in a well-defined deprecation strategy:

  * Identify functionalities for deprecation: Conduct comprehensive evaluations of the API and identify functionalities that are no longer actively used, have security vulnerabilities, or are superseded by newer, more efficient alternatives.
  * Set a deprecation timeline: Establish a clear timeline for deprecation, providing sufficient notice for developers to adjust their applications. Consider a reasonable timeframe that balances the need for change with providing developers time for a smooth transition.
  * Announce deprecation publicly with adequate notice: Communicate the deprecation plan through various channels accessible to your developer community well in advance. Outline the functionalities being deprecated, the timeline for removal, and any potential impacts on existing integrations.
  * Offer migration guidance: Provide developers with clear migration paths and alternative approaches using the deprecated functionalities. Offer resources like code samples, tutorials, and updated documentation to ease the transition process.
  * Versioning and deprecation: If the API utilizes versioning, consider deprecating functionalities within a specific version while continuing to offer them in an older version for a limited time. This can provide developers with a grace period to migrate to the newer version before the deprecated functionalities are entirely removed. New or revised endpoints should exist parallel to the old ones due for retirement for a predefined enterprise-defined guaranteed deprecation period.
  * Sunset and removal: Once the deprecation timeline concludes, remove the deprecated functionalities from the API ecosystem. Ensure the documentation reflects these changes and update any API reference tools accordingly.
  * Monitoring retiring APIs: Using the advanced tools to monitor individual APIs being deprecated will help to understand if timelines need to be extended or can be shortened.
  * Feedback loop: Coupled with monitoring the retiring APIs, a feedback loop will encourage API consumers to reach out with concerns and help adjust timelines if needed.



[Design phase](contents.xhtml#sc2_87a)

A few key decisions must be taken during the design phase. Using the guiding principles, design standards discussed, and the steps outlined below will help us be consistent and document the entire design phase.

There are three critical stakeholders from an API design perspective:

  * API producer: Creators of the API's.
  * API consumer: Users of the API.
  * End users: They buy and use apps that access APIs. So, the end user is an indirect consumer of services.



Any API design decision should include the needs of these stakeholders.

The key steps that an architect should take as part of the design phase include:

1. Define the purpose and scope of the API: The first step is to define the purpose and scope of the API. Questions that should be asked and answered include the problem the API is solving. In addition, defining use cases and the target audience will help determine the functionality needed from this API.

2. Choose the appropriate API architectural style: We have discussed some API architectures commonly used in API development. These include REST, GraphQL, SOAP, and gRPC. Each style has advantages and disadvantages; it is up to the reader to evaluate the styles and choose the right one. This book contains information on the different styles, which will be helpful as data points for you.

However, an important aspect to consider is that enterprise standards in an organization play an important role in style selection. For a particular service, gRPC might be the right choice, but if the organization is not using it currently and is not equipped with the right resources, it might not be a feasible solution. Hence, practicality plays an integral part in this selection.

3. Design the endpoints and requests: Endpoints are the developer's most visible part of the API. They are the URLs that consumers use to access the API. This should correspond to a specific resource or collection of resources. The request method (GET, POST, PUT, etc.) should indicate the desired data. The request body should contain the necessary data based on the data needed.

4. Define the responses and data models: An API's return values should be clear, consistent, and well-documented. The response format (JSON, XML, protbuf, etc.) should be easy to parse and understand. The data model in those responses should also be clear and understandable, with clear property names and data types.

5. Implement security and authentication: Security is critical to API design. All security guidelines should be followed, and authorization and authentication mechanisms should be implemented using any number of formats (OAuth2, JWT, etc.). This is further discussed in [Chapter 6](ch06.xhtml), API Security.

6. Consider performance and scalability: Any API should be designed with scalability, reliability, and performance. It should be designed to handle high volumes and bursts of requests and respond quickly. Methods to achieve this include caching, load balancing, and horizontal scaling.

7. Document the API: The importance of API documentation cannot be underestimated. Clear documentation should include descriptions of the endpoints, requests, responses, and data models, along with examples and tutorials.

8. Testing: Testing is an important aspect of any API. As part of the design process, the tools and testing standards should be defined and associated with the specifications and standards used in the API design.

The correct API design tools can automate many aspects of the design phase. In the next section, we will sample some of the available tools.

[API design tools](contents.xhtml#sc3_88a)

Many tools are used in API lifecycle management and provide various components, including the API gateway. These tools and frameworks help developers design, build, test, and document APIs, streamlining the API development lifecycle.

A list of some popular API design tools are:

  * Swagger: An open-source API design tool that allows developers to define, document, and test APIs using the OpenAPI Specification (OAS) with a user-friendly interface for designing APIs and producing API documentation.
  * Postman: This popular API development tool allows developers to design, test, and document APIs. It has a user-friendly interface enabling users to send HTTP requests and view the responses. Mock servers and creating API documentation are well-known features of this tool.
  * Apigee: It is a complete suite API management platform from Google Cloud with tools for designing, building, testing, and deploying APIs. It has a comprehensive toolset for the entire life cycle and a robust developer portal for API documentation.
  * Stoplight: This API design and management platform allows developers to design, document, and test APIs using the OAS. It has an API design editor, automated documentation generator, mock servers, and testing capabilities. Stoplight integrates with other tools and platforms, making incorporating it into existing development workflows easier.
  * Insomnia is a powerful and flexible API tool that allows developers to design, debug, and test APIs. It has a clean and intuitive interface for making HTPP requests to the APIs and viewing the responses, with support for various HTTP methods, authentication, and request/response formats.
  * API platform: It is an open-source API development framework. It allows developers to design, build, and test APIs. It is built on top of the Symfony framework. It provides tools and libraries to streamline the process of creating RESTful APIs and GraphQL APIs. It promotes a design-first approach, allowing developers to define the API schema and generate the corresponding code and documentation.



[API design patterns](contents.xhtml#sc3_89a)

API design patterns are reusable solutions that can be used to solve common and recurring problems encountered during API development. These patterns are a foundation for creating tested and robust APIs.

The following are some common design patterns:

  * RESTful design pattern: The REST architectural style is widely used for designing web APIs. They have very clear principles (outlined in the next section) that make it easier for developers to build APIs and make them available for consumption.
  * Pagination pattern: APIs frequently deal with large sets of data. Fetching all datasets at once is inefficient. The pagination pattern addresses this by allowing developers to retrieve data in chunks. Pagination allows for data retrieval in pages (e.g., 20 rows per page), improving performance and user experience.
  * Caching pattern: This pattern stores frequently accessed data temporarily for faster retrieval. This is highly beneficial for repetitive data requests of the same type. This reduces the response time of APIs.
  * Circuit breaker pattern: This pattern automatically suspends access to an API that has failed for a predetermined period of time. This is a handy pattern as systems in the real world experience failures or outages. An example is an AI model API that does image recognition. If the API is down, this pattern prevents excessive requests until the model API is active again.
  * Idempotency: Idempotent APIs guarantee that an API called multiple times with the same parameters yields the same outcome. This is important, especially when the architect has to plan for network issues. As a result, the API needs to be called multiple times with the same parameters.



[RESTful API design](contents.xhtml#sc3_90a)

RESTful APIs are currently the most common type used on the web. They are designed to be stateless, have a client-server architecture, be cacheable, and have a uniform interface. Due to their simplicity, RESTful APIs are a popular choice for web services.

[Key principles of RESTful API design](contents.xhtml#sc4_91a)

Over the years, a few common design principles have been established as best practices. They are described as follows:

  * Client-server architecture: RESTful APIs should follow a client-server architecture. In this architecture, the server and client are separate components, communicate over a network, and operate and evolve independently. The client is responsible for the user experience, and the server is responsible for the business services, data retrieval, and storage. By separating the two components, it makes the API scalable.
  * Statelessness: RESTful APIs should be stateless. Stateless means that every request to the server should contain all the information needed to understand and complete the request. This is achieved by the server not storing any session state between requests.
  * Cacheable: Responses must define themselves as cacheable or uncacheable. The responses can be stored and reused for similar requests when they are cacheable. Responses should include cache control headers, which help prevent clients from reusing stale or invalid data.
  * Uniform interface: An API should expose a consistent and standardized interface. This means that the API should consistently expose resources and how the clients interact with those resources. This uniform resource is achieved through HTTP methods and URL structures.
  * Layered system: APIs should be part of a layered architecture. Each layer is responsible for a specific domain ( UI layer, data layer, infrastructure layer, etc.) This allows each layer to be modified or updated separately without affecting other layers. For example, adding a caching layer should not affect the underlying data layer. This improves the scalability of the entire system as layers can be scaled as needed to support demand.
  * Resource-oriented architecture: RESTful APIs should be designed around resources, which are the key entities to which the API exposes. These resources should be nouns (e.g., Users, order, products, etc.) and represented by unique URLs. Some examples include "/users" and "/orders" instead of "/createUser" and "/updateOrder". The API should allow CRUD operations on these resources using the appropriate HTTP methods (GET, PUT, POST, DELETE).



RESTful API designs that adhere to REST principles are simple, easy to use, and maintain. A well-designed RESTful API ensures the long-term viability of an API and provides a seamless experience. Although technology has evolved, REST principles have remained valid and provide a solid foundation for API development.

[Practical API design](contents.xhtml#sc2_92a)

Several crucial questions must be addressed when designing a system interacting with APIs to ensure the integration is successful, efficient, and secure. This section discusses these questions and gives guidelines on when to use an existing API or build a new one, the criteria for making this decision, and best practices for using a third-party API.

It is not necessary to create a new API from scratch at all times. Sometimes, it makes sense to reuse an existing API in an enterprise or to take an existing API and evaluate if it can be modified to suit a new business purpose. A robust third party API marketplace exists, and this is another option for a developer.

[Use existing API or build a new one](contents.xhtml#sc3_93a)

Deciding between using an existing API or building a new one is a significant decision that depends on various factors. There might be certain situations where creating a new one is the only option, but in most cases, a quick analysis will help make this decision more straightforward.

There are usually three options for deciding during the conceptual stage:

  * Build an entirely new API from scratch.
  * Use an existing internal API that the consumer can either modify to create a new API or get the producing team to expand the API.
  * Use third-party APIs.



A typical workflow would need to answer the following questions, shown in Figure 3.3:

Figure 3.3: Sample decision process to decide whether to build or use existing APIs

At each stage of the evaluation, a few parameters should be evaluated to check the suitability of the API. This follows our general design principles. A possible framework that helps decide and evaluate APIs is described in the following table, where API requirements are assessed against the following criteria:

Build or buy  
---  
Criteria |  Description  
Functionality |  Evaluate if the existing API provides the functionality you need. If it covers all or most of your requirements, it might be more cost-effective to use it rather than building a new one.  
Scalability |  Ensure the existing API can scale to meet your application's future needs.  
Financial |  Analyze the cost implications of both options. Using an existing API might involve subscription fees, but building a new one will require development, maintenance, and hosting costs. An existing internal API might already have its costs budgeted for other than additional usage costs, while the costs for an external API might initially be of a higher magnitude.  
Self-service and onboarding |  Developers can self-register, explore APIs, and use them immediately.  
Compliance |  APIs should comply with relevant regulations (GDPR, HIPAA, etc.) and provide features to help enforce these requirements.  
Time |  Consider the time constraints of your project. Building a new API can be time-consuming, and using an existing one could expedite your project's timeline.  
Reliability and performance |  Assess the reliability and performance of the existing API. Ensure it can handle your application's load and has a good uptime history. Be aware of any rate limits to ensure your application does not exceed the allowed number of requests.  
Security |  Evaluate the security measures in place for the existing API. Ensure it complies with industry standards and your organization's security policies.  
Support and community |  Check the level of support and community around the existing API. A strong community and responsive support can be invaluable.  
Integration |  Assess how easily the existing API can be integrated into your systems. Consider any potential challenges and the availability of SDKs or libraries. Topics such as authentication, rate limiting, and error handling should be evaluated. This needs to be evaluated differently for third-party and existing internal APIs. Building a new API gives more flexibility and control over these aspects at the cost of time and money.  
Maintainability |  An existing internal API will have the advantage of being more flexible when adding functionality. An external third-party API will be more rigid regarding structure and release cycles.  
  
Table 3.1: Criteria that determine building or buying an API service

Using an external API has some benefits; however, some steps must be followed to ensure they suit your purpose.

A few common steps before leveraging a third-party API include:

1\. Conduct API discovery: The first step is to research and identify third-party APIs that align with the requirements. Many organizations monetize their APIs; an online search can help locate a suitable API.

2\. Review API documentation: The provided documentation is a good benchmark for a suitable API. By examining the API documentation, we can evaluate the suitability of a particular API.

3\. Mock test APIs: Most third-party APIs provide some form of mock testing for their APIs to test requests, responses, and data output. This will help determine the suitability of using the API. In addition, using your API testing tools to create and run manual test cases is a good option.

[Common pitfalls in API design](contents.xhtml#sc3_94a)

While there are a number of standards and examples for creating APIs, some common pitfalls can reduce their effectiveness, leading to poor performance or not meeting the functionality expectations. In addition, developers sometimes overlook backward compatibility challenges and the need for safely and effectively deprecating endpoints.

The following section outlines typical mistakes in API designs. Furthermore, it provides tips on backward compatibility and effectively suggests strategies for deprecating endpoints.

  * Lack of consistency: Frequently, a significant cause of concern in organizations is the inconsistent naming conventions, non-standardized response formats, and the lack of consistent behavior of API endpoints. This confuses developers and leads to integration errors.
  * Ignoring HTTP status codes: Most API endpoints produce a particular set of standard HTTP codes. Developers sometimes either ignore these or misuse their purpose. This prevents clients from understanding responses more deeply, making error handling difficult.
  * Overcomplicating API endpoints: API endpoints should be kept as simple as possible. One common mistake is to design an endpoint that tries to do too much or produces too much data in a single request. This makes the API difficult to understand and use.
  * Poor or inadequate documentation: This is the biggest challenge facing API developers. The urgency to rush to code and deploy leads to insufficient or outdated documentation and has the side effect of complicated code debugging.
  * Lack of standards on security: A critical error is the failure to implement appropriate authentication, authorization, and data encryption. This can expose sensitive data and leave APIs vulnerable to attacks.



Incorporating the above considerations in your API design process will help ensure that the APIs remain relevant even during major API releases and the deprecation of critical APIs. Ultimately, this will drive the adoption of APIs and reduce duplication in API services.

[Conclusion](contents.xhtml#sc2_95a)

API design is a complex and extensive topic. A good API design starting point is the establishment of guiding principles that drive all the aspects of the API lifecycle. Having guiding principles will ensure that every API serves a purpose in the ecosystem; they could be technical or business-based. Beyond this, we discussed API design standards that enable consistency in naming conventions, URL structures, etc. These are often overlooked, leading to a proliferation of very difficult APIs to understand and use. An aspect of APIs that deserves special consideration in sound API design principles is the establishment of deprecation strategies. APIs have a lifespan. Improvements in technology, business reasons, or security considerations often lead an API to be retired or updated. Handling different versions of APIs and retiring them is an important consideration discussed in this chapter.

This chapter covered some key design principles and practical aspects of API design. However, some subjects are vast and require a more detailed examination. For example, security, API gateway, and documentation are all complex topics, with a separate chapter devoted to them. They will help the reader get a more balanced view of the other topics that aid in good API design practices.

The next chapter looks at additional architectural styles, including SOAP, gRPC, and real-time APIs using WebSockets. We do a comparative study of the different styles and evaluate factors to consider when choosing architectural styles.

[Key terms](contents.xhtml#sc2_96a)

  * Guiding principles for API design: A set of best practices and recommendations that help create well-designed, consistent, and user-friendly APIs.
  * API design standards: They are guidelines and rules that ensure consistency, maintainability, and ease of use across an organization's APIs.
  * Naming conventions: A set of rules for naming API resources, endpoints, parameters, and other elements to maintain consistency and clarity.
  * Caching: The technique of storing frequently accessed data in a fast-access memory to reduce the number of requests to the backend server and improve response times.
  * Documentation standards: Guidelines for creating clear, comprehensive, and up-to-date documentation for APIs, making them easier to understand and use.
  * API deprecation: The phasing out of an API or specific API elements, typically due to updates, security issues, or lack of usage.
  * API architectural style: The overall design approach for building an API, such as REST, GraphQL, or SOAP.
  * Endpoints: The specific URLs clients use to interact with an API, typically representing resources or functionality.
  * Request and responses: The communication between a client and an API server. The client sends a request to the server and receives a response with the requested data or an error message.
  * Performance and scalability: The ability of an API to handle a high volume of requests at scale to accommodate growth in usage.
  * API testing: The process of validating an API's functionality, reliability, performance, and security to ensure it meets the specified requirements.
  * API design tools: Software tools and platforms that assist in designing, documenting, and testing APIs, such as Swagger, Postman, or Apiary.
  * API design patterns: Reusable solutions to common API design problems, such as pagination, filtering, or error handling.
  * RESTful API design: An architectural style for designing APIs based on REST principles, emphasizing a stateless, client-server communication model using standard HTTP methods.

# [CHAPTER 4Crafting APIs](contents.xhtml#ch04a)

[Introduction](contents.xhtml#sc2_97a)

As discussed in the previous chapters, APIs are the backbone of enterprise systems, enabling seamless and platform-agnostic interaction between different components and services. The design and architecture of these APIs are crucial for the immediate functionality they provide and the scalability, maintainability, and flexibility of the systems they support.

We have discussed several different API styles in [Chapter 2](ch02.xhtml), Beyond REST. In this chapter, we introduce more architectural styles of API development to give the architect an understanding of the advantages and disadvantages of different styles. Each style and type offers distinct advantages and limitations. Selecting the right combination is like constructing a building using the proper foundation and building materials. The choices made at this stage will influence the API's performance, usability, and future adaptability.

This chapter discusses critical considerations and methodologies for choosing the appropriate API styles and types tailored to meet the unique demands of enterprise applications.

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_98a)

In this chapter, we will look at the following topics:

  * Simple Object Access Protocol
  * WebSockets and real-time APIs
  * Decision factors in selecting API styles
  * Future trends and considerations



[Objectives](contents.xhtml#sc2_99a)

Selecting the most appropriate API style is an important part of designing and writing an API. To help achieve this, we have looked at different API styles (GraphQL, gRPC) as part of [Chapter 2](ch02.xhtml), Beyond REST, and we continue looking at additional API styles in this chapter. In addition, there are other considerations that help determine the appropriate API style for a business function.

In this chapter, we will compare the features of the different styles using some business use cases. By the end, the reader should be able to look at the requirements for a business service using multiple dimensions and then arrive at a logical conclusion to select a particular style.

Keep in mind that in larger enterprises, it is very rare that only one style of API is available; due to legacy considerations, third-party interactions, and other technical reasons, there might be already prevalent styles.

[Simple Object Access Protocol](contents.xhtml#sc2_100a)

Simple Object Access Protocol (SOAP) is a protocol specification designed to exchange structured information between web services. It specifies a standardized message format and allows applications to talk to each other irrespective of the underlying platform or development language.

SOAP is an XML-based protocol. It uses XML to encode its messages. XML is a human-readable and machine-processable format, making it a good choice for complex enterprise environments where different systems coexist.

SOAP supports different communication protocols, including HyperText Transfer Protocol (HTTP), Simple Mail Transfer Protocol (SMTP), Transmission Control Protocol (TCP), and others. By supporting multiple protocols, SOAP can be used in different scenarios ranging from simple request-response messaging to more complex messaging patterns like request-response, solicit-response, and notification.

SOAP messages are encapsulated in an envelope that contains an optional header and a mandatory body. This header can include additional information for processing the message, like authentication credentials, transaction control, or routing information. The body part of the envelope contains the actual message data intended for the recipient.

One of the critical features of SOAP is its support for WS-* standards. These are a collection of specifications extending SOAP web services' capabilities, including WS-Security, WS-Addressing, WS-ReliableMessaging, WS-Policy, etc. These standards address common requirements in enterprise messaging scenarios, such as security (WS-Security), transactions (WS-AtomicTransaction), and reliable messaging (WS-ReliableMessaging), among others, and promote interoperability, governance, and compliance.

Despite the emergence of alternative approaches like Representational State Transfer (REST), SOAP remains a popular choice for enterprise-level web services where the need for formal contracts Web Services Description Language (WSDL), stateful operations, and standardization is critical. SOAP's emphasis on extensibility, neutrality, and independence from transport protocols makes it a robust choice for building interoperable, platform-agnostic web services.

[Critical components of SOAP architecture](contents.xhtml#sc3_101a)

The components in a SOAP request are detailed as follows:

  * Envelope: The root element that defines the XML document as a SOAP message.
  * Header: Contains application-specific information like authentication, transaction, etc.
  * Body: Contains the request/response information.
  * Fault: An optional Fault element that provides information about errors while processing the message.



Depicted in the following figure are the components that make up a SOAP request:

Figure 4.1: The structure of a SOAP request

The following figure shows the interaction between a SOAP server and a SOAP client. Each request and response includes the components of a SOAP request (depicted in the previous figure):

Figure 4.2: SOAP client and server interaction

[Key differences between SOAP and REST](contents.xhtml#sc3_102a)

While both SOAP and REST are used for web services, they differ significantly because of the following factors:

  * Protocol vs. architectural style: SOAP is a protocol with strict rules and advanced security, while REST is an architectural style with more flexibility.
  * Data format: SOAP exclusively uses XML, whereas REST is more flexible, supporting formats like JSON, XML, and others.
  * Statefulness: SOAP can be stateful and stateless; however, REST is typically stateless.
  * Security: SOAP has a standard set of security features, whereas REST relies on underlying protocols for security.



[SOAP advantages](contents.xhtml#sc3_103a)

SOAP has some inherent advantages that can be utilized while building SOAP-based APIs:

  * Security features: SOAP's support for WS-Security, a set of protocols that add security to SOAP-based web services, is one of its key strengths. This includes:
    * Encryption: Ensuring the message is only readable by the intended recipient.
    * Authentication: Verifying the identity of the sender.
    * Integrity: Ensures that the message has not been altered in transit.
    * Standardized protocol: SOAP is a standard protocol with well-defined rules, which makes it highly extensible, neutral, and independent of the underlying network. This standardization facilitates:
      * Interoperability: Easy interaction with other systems, regardless of platform or language.
      * Formal contracts: Use WSDL to define clear contracts for service interaction. This removes ambiguity.



An example of SOAP payment request and response interaction is shown in the following figure:

Figure 4.3: Client-server interactions for a SOAP service

[Limitations of SOAP](contents.xhtml#sc3_104a)

There are certain inherent limitations to using SOAP. Some of these are given below and influence the selection of an API architectural style.

  * Heaviness and complexity: SOAP's reliance on XML can lead to verbose messages, which are heavier and require more bandwidth. Additionally, the complexity of the SOAP protocol can lead to:
    * Slower performance: Due to the size and complexity of the messages.
    * Increased development effort: More time and resources are required to set up and maintain SOAP services.
  * Limited flexibility: While SOAP's standardization is a strength, it can also be a limitation. The strict rules and structures can limit flexibility in specific scenarios, such as:
    * Less freedom in data representation: This is because it is bound to the XML format.
    * Complexity in consumption: Clients must understand the WSDL file and adhere strictly to the service contract.



[Advanced features and best practices in SOAP](contents.xhtml#sc3_105a)

Advanced features in SOAP can be used to enhance SOAP APIs. Some of them are as follows:

  * Enhanced functionality using WS-* specifications: Different WS-* specifications can be used for enhanced functionality. For example, WS-ReliableMessaging for guaranteed message delivery, WS-AtomicTransaction for transaction management, and WS-Security for advanced security features. Using these specifications ensures consistency and patterns that are available and tested.
  * Specifications across different platforms and tools: SOAP can be used across various platforms using the inherent compatibility and support built into the SOAP specifications.
  * Utilize SOAP with Attachments (SwA): For scenarios requiring binary data transfer (like images or documents), use SwA. This allows sending binary data along with the SOAP message as MIME attachments.
  * Error handling using SOAP fault element: The SOAP Fault element helps provide meaningful error messages and categorize errors (client, Sserver, etc.) for easier troubleshooting.
  * Minimize XML overhead: Use efficient XML schemas and consider techniques like XML compression to reduce the size of SOAP messages.
    * Avoid unnecessary complexity in XML structures.
  * Implement caching mechanisms: Frequently accessed data should be cached and this will reduce the load on the server and improve response times.
  * Utilize asynchronous communications: Operations not requiring an immediate response benefit by using asynchronous communications by helping to improve performance.
  * Adhere to strict SOAP standards: Enforcing SOAP best practices and standards helps ensure maximum interoperability.
  * Integration with legacy systems: Leveraging SOAP's ability to integrate with legacy systems is often essential in enterprise environments.



[Implementing SOAP APIs](contents.xhtml#sc3_106a)

SOAP APIs are a good choice in the financial sector, where security and transactional reliability are paramount. Implementing Secure Transactions leveraging WS-Security for encrypted and authenticated communication and Integration with Legacy Systems as SOAP's standardization makes it easier to integrate with existing systems. This is especially true if SOAP is already highly used within the enterprise.

[Designing a SOAP service](contents.xhtml#sc3_107a)

Designing a SOAP service involves several vital steps to ensure it is robust, efficient, and maintainable:

1. Defining the service contract:

• Use WSDL to define the service contract. This includes specifying the service's operations, the format of request and response messages, and the protocols supported.

• Ensure the contract is unambiguous, as it is a formal agreement between the service and its consumers.

2. Creating SOAP messages:

• Design the structure of SOAP messages, keeping in mind the Envelope, Header, Body, and Fault elements.

• Optimize the size of messages to avoid performance bottlenecks, especially in network-constrained environments.

3. Handling namespaces:

• Properly manage XML namespaces to avoid conflicts, especially when integrating with multiple external systems.

• Versioning: Implement versioning strategies for the API to manage changes over time without disrupting existing clients.

Creating a SOAP schema (often referred to as a WSDL document) for a payment transaction involves defining the structure of the SOAP messages for both the request and response. The following example shows how such a schema might look for the payment transaction example discussed in the previous chapter. This schema is simplified for clarity.

The following is a WSDL specification example fragment for a payment transaction:

<?xml version="1.0" encoding="UTF-8"?>

<definitions xmlns="http://schemas.xmlsoap.org/wsdl/"

xmlns:soap="http://schemas.xmlsoap.org/wsdl/soap/"

xmlns:tns="http://example.com/payments"

xmlns:xsd="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema"

targetNamespace="http://example.com/payments"

name= "PaymentService">

<types>

<xsd:schema targetNamespace="http://example.com/payments">

<xsd:element name="GetPaymentDetailsRequest">

<xsd:complexType>

<xsd:sequence>

<xsd:element name="paymentId" type="xsd:string"/>

</xsd:sequence>

</xsd:complexType>

</xsd:element>

<xsd:element name="GetPaymentDetailsResponse">

<xsd:complexType>

<xsd:sequence>

<xsd:element name="paymentId" type="xsd:string"/>

<xsd:element name="amount" type="xsd:decimal"/>

<xsd:element name="currency" type="xsd:string"/>

<xsd:element name="date" type="xsd:date"/>

<xsd:element name="status" type="xsd:string"/>

<xsd:element name="sender" type="xsd:string"/>

<xsd:element name="recipient" type="xsd:string"/>

</xsd:sequence>

</xsd:complexType>

</xsd:element>

</xsd:schema>

</types>

<message name="GetPaymentDetailsRequestMessage">

<part name="parameters" element="tns:GetPaymentDetailsRequest"/>

</message>

<message name="GetPaymentDetailsResponseMessage">

<part name="parameters" element="tns:GetPaymentDetailsResponse"/>

</message>

<portType name="PaymentPortType">

<operation name="GetPaymentDetails">

<input message="tns:GetPaymentDetailsRequestMessage"/>

<output message="tns:GetPaymentDetailsResponseMessage"/>

</operation>

</portType>

<binding name="PaymentBinding" type="tns:PaymentPortType">

<soap:binding style="document" transport="http://schemas.xmlsoap.org/soap/http"/>

<operation name="GetPaymentDetails">

<soap:operation soapAction="http://example.com/GetPaymentDetails"/>

<input>

<soap:body use="literal"/>

</input>

<output>

<soap:body use="literal"/>

</output>

</operation>

</binding>

<service name="PaymentService">

<port name="PaymentPort" binding="tns:PaymentBinding">

<soap:address location="http://example.com/paymentservice"/>

</port>

</service>

</definitions>

This WSDL document above outlines:

  * The structure of the request and response messages for a GetPaymentDetails operation.
  * The types involved in the operation, including the request with a paymentId and the response with details about the payment.
  * The binding information specifies that the messages are formatted as literal XML and transported over HTTP.
  * The service and port definitions provide the endpoint URL where the service is available.



This example provides a basic structure. In a real-world scenario, additional details such as authentication, error handling, and namespace definitions might be necessary.

The above details on SOAP should be a good foundation for understanding SOAP APIs. These powerful and complex methods can be used in different situations.

[WebSockets and real-time APIs](contents.xhtml#sc2_108a)

Real-time APIs are in demand in the digital age. Some use cases include instant messaging, live sports updates, real-time financial training, and collaborative environments. Traditional HTTP-based communications are often not suitable as they do not meet applications' real-time requirements. They are based on the request-response paradigm, which is not optimized for low latency or continuous data exchange.

This is why real-time APIs have been introduced. They have changed how we think about and implement real-time, bidirectional communication in web applications.

WebSockets provide a persistent, full-duplex communication channel over a single TCP connection. This enables servers to push data to clients in real-time without needing a polling method. Realtime APIs are often built on or alongside WebSockets and expose specific functionalities and data streams that can be consumed in real time. This facilitates the development of interactive, dynamic user experiences.

[Introduction to WebSockets](contents.xhtml#sc3_109a)

WebSockets provide a full-duplex communication channel over a long-lived, single connection between the client and server. Unlike the traditional request-response model, WebSockets allow for persistent connections, enabling real-time data flow.

WebSockets have the following characteristics:

  * Persistent connection: Unlike HTTP, where each request/response creates a new connection, WebSockets keep the connection open, facilitating ongoing communication.


  * Protocol upgrade: A WebSocket connection is initiated with an HTTP handshake (Upgrade request), which then upgrades to a WebSocket connection if supported by the server.
  * Bi-directional data flow: The client and server can send data any time without waiting for a request from the other side.



The following figure depicts the data flow between the WebSocket client and server:

Figure 4.4: Interaction between a WebSocket client and server

Ideal use cases for web sockets include applications requiring real-time updates, such as chat applications, live sports updates, and financial trading platforms.

[Advantages of real-time APIs](contents.xhtml#sc3_110a)

Real-time APIs have certain advantages when being used in an application, including:

  * Instant data transfer
    * Reduced latency: Immediate data transfer without the overhead of establishing new connections for each message.
    * Realtime interaction: Enables applications to provide users with real-time feedback and interaction.
  * Enhancing user experience
    * Interactive applications: Improves user experience in online gaming, live streaming, and collaborative tools.
    * Push notifications: Allows servers to push notifications and updates to clients instantly.



[Design considerations](contents.xhtml#sc3_111a)

Websockets have considerable advantages and some challenges as well. A few considerations that should help determine the suitability of Websockets are detailed as follows:

  * Multiplexing:
    * Single connection for multiple channels: Using a single WebSocket connection to handle different types of messages or data streams.
  * Heartbeats and connection management:
    * Keeping connections alive: Implementing heartbeats to keep the connection open and detect disconnects.
    * Reconnection strategies: Automatically reconnecting in case of connection loss.
  * Binary data handling:
    * Binary messaging: WebSockets support text and binary data, enabling efficient transmission of non-textual data like images or custom binary formats.
  * Scalability challenges:
    * Connection overhead: Each WebSocket connection consumes server resources, which can become significant with many concurrent connections.
    * Load balancing: Traditional load balancing methods may need to be more practical due to the persistent nature of WebSocket connections.
  * Security implications:
    * Vulnerabilities: WebSockets are susceptible to security risks similar to HTTP, including cross-site scripting (XSS) and cross-site request forgery (CSRF).
    * Encryption: Ensuring data security over WebSockets requires implementing TLS (wss://), similar to HTTPS.



[Real-world applications](contents.xhtml#sc3_112a)

In the real world, there are a few cases where real-time APIs are appropriate. Let us discuss a couple of examples to emphasize the usefulness of real-time APIs:

  * Implementing a chat application:
    * Instant messaging: WebSockets facilitate real-time communication in a chat application and is a good showcase for a real-world application. For this application, a specific need is to handle many concurrent conditions. Any application using Websockets must manage multiple users and ensure efficient data transmission.

The following figure depicts the workflow of a chat application using real-time APIs:




Figure 4.5: An example interaction of a WebSocket-based real-time chat service

  * Realtime notifications in web applications:
    * Modern web applications can have the browser show real-time notifications. These could be event-based updates (e.g., shopping sites notifying users of flash sales or social networks updating feeds in real time).
    * These are often implemented using server-side event triggers that push notifications to clients.



[Best practices in WebSocket development](contents.xhtml#sc3_113a)
