While helpful, WebSockets require specific considerations to ensure appropriate use; otherwise, they become highly inefficient and use system resources unnecessarily.

Some of these factors include:

  * Efficient resource management:
    * Optimizing connection usage: Minimizing resource consumption per connection to handle more concurrent users.
    * Connection pooling and throttling: Managing the number of active connections to prevent server overload.
  * Robust security measures:
    * Implementing TLS: Using encrypted WebSocket connections (wss://) to ensure data security.
    * Validating input: Protecting against injection attacks by validating all data sent over WebSockets.
  * Scalability techniques:
    * Horizontal scaling: Deploying WebSocket services across multiple servers and using techniques like sticky sessions for load balancing. Using a load balancer like NGINX HAProxy or AWS Elastic Load Balancer (ELB) with sticky sessions enabled helps in this.
    * Using message brokers: Implementing message brokers or pub/sub systems to distribute messages efficiently across multiple servers. An example would be using Redis Pub/Sub for real-time messaging between WebSocket servers for a real-time stock trading platform. Factors to consider while choosing API style.



API architecture styles should be chosen based on an analysis of some key considerations. These should, at the very minimum, include a deep understanding of the business requirements and the specific needs of the application ecosystem. Factors such as the nature of the data exchange, the required scalability, security considerations, and the development timeline play a crucial role in this decision-making process. For instance, REST, with its stateless operations and cacheable data, might be ideal for public-facing APIs requiring scalability and simplicity. On the other hand, gRPC, with its efficient binary serialization, might be the choice for microservices needing high-performance communication.

When architecting APIs for enterprise systems, the focus often gravitates toward functional requirements—the specific tasks and outputs the API must accomplish or produce, as mentioned above. However, non-functional requirements (NFRs) play an equally critical role in shaping the selection of API styles. NFRs include scalability, performance, security, maintainability, and usability. These attributes do not define what the system does but how well it performs its functions. Some of these factors include:

  * Scalability: This factor is paramount for enterprises aiming to efficiently grow or handle varying loads. The choice between REST, stateless by design, and other protocols can hinge on how well they scale under load. Stateless architectures allow for easier scaling since each request is independent, and resources can be easily replicated or distributed across servers without concern for a shared state. In contrast, protocols like SOAP, which often rely on maintaining stateful sessions, may require more sophisticated scaling strategies.
  * Performance: Closely tied to scalability, performance is about data exchange and processing efficiency. For example, gRPC uses protobuf to serialize structured data. This can significantly enhance performance by reducing latency and bandwidth usage, making it an attractive option for microservices that require frequent, fast communications. On the other hand, REST APIs, with their human-readable JSON or XML formats, might be slower in data serialization and deserialization but are more versatile and more accessible to debug.
  * Security: Security requirements can also dictate the choice of API style. SOAP's built-in WS-Security standard, which supports message integrity, confidentiality, and single message authentication, makes it a strong candidate for systems where security is non-negotiable, such as financial or healthcare systems. Meanwhile, REST relies on HTTPS for encryption, which, while secure, may not offer the same level of built-in security features as SOAP but is more straightforward to implement and manage.
  * Maintainability: The ease of maintaining and updating APIs is crucial for long-term system health. GraphQL offers a highly maintainable API style due to its ability to request the exact data needed, reducing the need for versioning and simplifying client-side data fetching logic. This can lead to fewer breaking changes and a more stable contract between the API and its consumers. Conversely, REST, with its resource-oriented architecture, promotes a clear and well-structured API design that can also be highly maintainable if properly versioned and documented.
  * Usability: Particularly from the API consumer's (developers) perspective, usability influences the choice of API style. GraphQL shines in usability by allowing clients to query for precisely what they need, making it highly adaptable to the changing needs of client applications without requiring changes to the server. REST's HTTP verbs and status codes make it intuitive and aligned with the web's underlying protocols, offering predictability and ease of use.



[Comparing API styles](contents.xhtml#sc3_114a)

We have looked at different API styles in this chapter and also in [Chapter 2](ch02.xhtml), Beyond REST. In this section, we compare the different styles of architecture based on some key architectural styles. This should help the architect determine the right architectural style for a business function.

[REST vs. GraphQL vs. SOAP vs. gRPC vs. WebSockets](contents.xhtml#sc4_115a)

A brief overview of each style is as follows:

  * REST: An architectural style using HTTP protocols for Create, Read, Update, Delete (CRUD) operations. It is known for its statelessness and cacheability.
  * GraphQL: A query language for APIs and a runtime for executing those queries using a type system you define for your data. It allows clients to request only the data they need.
  * SOAP: A protocol for exchanging structured information in web services, using XML for its message format. It is known for its high security and extensibility.
  * gRPC: A high-performance, open-source framework that uses HTTP/2 for transport and protobuf as the IDL.
  * WebSockets: This protocol provides full-duplex communication channels over a single TCP connection, allowing real-time data transfer between a client and a server.



The key differences between the architectural styles include:

  * Protocol and data format:
    * REST typically uses JSON, XML, or other text-based formats.
    * GraphQL primarily uses JSON.
    * SOAP exclusively uses XML.
    * gRPC uses protobuf, a binary format.
    * WebSockets can transfer both text (like JSON) and binary data.
  * Performance and efficiency:
    * REST can have issues with over-fetching or under-fetching data.
    * GraphQL optimizes data retrieval by allowing clients to specify precisely what they need.
    * SOAP tends to be verbose, impacting network performance.
    * gRPC offers significant performance benefits due to HTTP/2 and binary serialization.
    * WebSockets provide real-time communication, reducing the need for repeated HTTP requests.
  * Use cases:
    * REST is versatile and suitable for general web services.
    * GraphQL excels in applications where clients need to query complex data sets.
    * SOAP is preferred in enterprise environments requiring formal contracts and high security.
    * gRPC is ideal for microservices and systems requiring high-performance inter-service communication.
    * WebSockets are used in applications needing real-time updates, like chat apps or live feeds.



[Comparing features across different architectural styles](contents.xhtml#sc3_116a)

The following table provides a guideline to the architect on comparing the different features of different architecture styles. This will serve as a quick feature comparison when selecting the right style for a particular business case.

Feature |  REST |  GraphQL |  SOAP |  gRPC |  WebSockets  
---|---|---|---|---|---  
Data Format |  JSON, XML |  JSON |  XML |  Protocol Buffers |  Text/Binary  
Protocol |  HTTP/HTTPS |  HTTP/HTTPS |  HTTP/HTTPS, SMTP, TCP |  HTTP/2 |  WS/WSS  
Performance |  Moderate |  High (efficient queries) |  Lower (verbose) |  High |  High (realtime)  
Security |  Depends on HTTP |  Depends on HTTP |  WS-Security |  Transport security (TLS) |  WS/WSS  
Scalability |  High |  High |  Moderate |  High |  Varies  
Realtime Capability |  Limited |  Limited |  No |  No |  Yes  
Complexity |  Low |  Moderate |  High |  Moderate |  Moderate  
Ideal Use Case |  General web services |  Complex data retrieval apps |  Enterprise systems with strict security |  High-performance microservices |  Realtime applications  
  
Table 4.1: Comparing different features across architectural styles

[Practical examples](contents.xhtml#sc4_117a)

Following are some examples of applications and suitable choices for the architectural style to implement the API:

  * E-commerce application:
    * REST: Good for basic product listing and CRUD operations.
    * GraphQL: Ideal for complex queries, like fetching products with specific attributes.
    * SOAP: Suitable for integrating with legacy enterprise systems for inventory management.
    * gRPC: Useful for inter-service communication in a microservices architecture.
    * WebSockets: Great for real-time features like stock updates or live customer support chat.
  * Social media platform:
    * REST: This can be used for straightforward data retrievals like fetching posts or user profiles.
    * GraphQL: Excellent for complex queries, such as fetching a user's friends, posts, and comments in a single request.
    * SOAP: Less preferred due to its verbosity and complexity.
    * gRPC: Ideal for backend services that communicate with each other, like notifications or message delivery systems.
    * WebSockets: Perfect for real-time features like instant messaging or live feed updates.



[Decision factors in selecting API styles](contents.xhtml#sc2_118a)

Some decision factors need to be understood when selecting API architectures. These can be broken into different categories, are described as follows:

  * Understanding business needs:
    * Business objectives: Identifying the primary goals of the application, such as improving user experience, ensuring data security, or optimizing for scalability.
    * Target audience: Considering the needs and technical capabilities of the end-users. For instance, performance and data efficiency become crucial if the audience primarily uses mobile devices.
  * Technical considerations:
    * Data format and structure: Determining the nature of the data being handled. Complex, interrelated data sets might lean towards GraphQL. Simpler, more uniform data could be well-served by REST.
    * Network conditions: Evaluating the network environment in which the application will operate. More efficient data formats like those used in gRPC might be preferable in situations with limited bandwidth or high latency.
    * Integration with existing systems: Assessing the need to integrate with legacy systems might influence the choice towards SOAP or REST, depending on the existing infrastructure.
  * Considerations: Performance, scalability, and complexity:
    * Performance metrics:
      * Response time: Measuring the API's time to handle requests and respond. gRPC generally offers the best performance due to HTTP/2 and binary data format.
      * Data overhead: Evaluating the size of data payloads. GraphQL can reduce payload size by allowing clients to request only what they need.
    * Scalability needs:
      * Handling load: Determining the API's ability to handle a large number of requests. REST and GraphQL are typically stateless, making them easier to scale. An example metric that could be considered is the Queries Per Second (qps) that the system needs to be designed for,
      * Resource utilization: Considering the server resources required per connection or request. While providing real-time capabilities, WebSockets might consume more resources due to persistent connections.
    * Complexity and maintenance:
      * Development complexity: Assessing each API style's learning curve and development complexity. SOAP and gRPC might require more specialized knowledge compared to REST.

 Maintenance over time: Considering the ease of maintaining and updating the API. With its stateless nature and cacheability, REST can be easier to maintain, whereas GraphQL requires ongoing optimization as data requirements evolve.




[Private vs. public APIs](contents.xhtml#sc3_119a)

Private and public APIs are usually treated differently and help determine the most suitable API style. This choice is influenced by various factors, including the intended audience, security requirements, data sensitivity, and the specific use cases the API aims to address.

[Private APIs](contents.xhtml#sc4_120a)

Private APIs are primarily for internal use within an organization. They serve as the building blocks of internal systems, facilitating communication and data exchange between different components of an enterprise's IT infrastructure. Internal development practices, the existing technology stack, and specific NFRs like performance and scalability often influence the choice of API style for private APIs.

API style considerations for private APIs are as follows:

  * Integration and compatibility: For organizations with a diverse set of internal systems and technologies, REST APIs are often favored due to their simplicity, wide adoption, and ease of integration with different platforms and languages.
  * Performance: In scenarios where internal microservices require high-throughput communication, gRPC might be preferred for its efficiency and low latency, thanks to its use of HTTP/2 and protobuf.
  * Flexibility and rapid development: GraphQL can be particularly advantageous for internal APIs that support rapidly evolving front-end requirements without frequent backend changes, offering flexibility in querying data.



[Public APIs](contents.xhtml#sc4_121a)

On the other hand, public APIs are exposed to external developers and third-party users. They are critical to an organization's external-facing infrastructure, enabling third-party developers to build applications that leverage its data and services. The choice of API style for public APIs is heavily influenced by the need to provide a seamless, secure, and intuitive developer experience.

API style considerations for public APIs are:

  * Ease of use and developer experience: REST is often chosen for public APIs due to its familiarity among developers, extensive support across programming languages, and ease of use facilitated by HTTP standard methods.
  * Flexibility and efficiency: GraphQL is increasingly popular for public APIs, especially when the consumer needs the flexibility to query for specific data, reducing bandwidth and improving application efficiency.
  * Security and compliance: While not a style per se, security protocols and standards have become a critical consideration for public APIs. SOAP's comprehensive security standards may still be preferred in industries where data sensitivity and compliance with strict regulatory standards are paramount.



Other factors that influence the implementation of public APIs are described as follows:

  * Security: Public APIs require robust security mechanisms to protect sensitive data and prevent unauthorized access. This necessity might influence the choice of API styles and protocols that support comprehensive security standards and authentication mechanisms.
  * Documentation and community support: Public APIs benefit from clear, thorough documentation and active community support to ensure ease of use and adoption. This consideration might sway the choice towards more widely adopted and supported API styles.
  * Scalability: The expected load and scalability requirements can significantly influence the choice of API style. Public APIs, which might need to handle unpredictable traffic volumes, require a scalable architecture, affecting the preference for stateless API styles like REST.



In summary, the distinction between private and public APIs significantly influences the choice of API style, driven by the unique requirements, challenges, and goals associated with each. By carefully considering these factors, organizations can select an API style that meets their immediate needs and positions them for future growth and innovation.

[Additional high-level guidelines for decision-making](contents.xhtml#sc3_122a)

Some other guidelines that are of value to an architect in deciding the architectural style include:

  * Assess data requirements: Choose GraphQL for complex data structures, REST for more straightforward data retrieval, SOAP for standardized enterprise communication, gRPC for efficient inter-service communication, and WebSockets for real-time data transfer.
  * Consider the application scale: Consider each API style's scalability and performance implications for large-scale applications.
  * Evaluate security needs: SOAP or REST with additional security layers might be preferable for applications requiring stringent security measures.
  * Factor in development resources: Consider the team's expertise and the learning curve associated with each API style.
  * Private or public APIs: Consider the audience for the APIs and the factors surrounding the usage.



Take a look at the following sample flow chart to help determine the type of API style:

Figure 4.6: A simple flowchart to help identify the API style

[Practical decision-making examples](contents.xhtml#sc3_123a)

In previous sections, we have compared the different styles of APIs using a set of features. However, choosing the appropriate API styles is also dependent on the application. To highlight this, we look at different types of business applications and their corresponding business needs. The business needs sometimes override the need to use specific API styles. The following examples look at diverse business applications and explain the choice of the API architectural style.

Example 1: Online retail application

Business need: Fast and efficient product browsing with dynamic content. A good choice could be GraphQL due to its efficient data-loading capabilities. This would allow clients to request specific product details, reduce bandwidth usage, and improve load times.

Example 2: Banking system integration

Business need: Secure and reliable communication between different banking systems. One primary option could be SOAP, given its robust security features and suitability for enterprise-level integrations.

Example 3: IoT device management

Business need: Managing an extensive network of IoT devices with real-time updates. A robust choice could be gRPC for IoT communications because of its high performance and efficiency in handling large numbers of small, frequent messages.

Example 4: Live sports update application

Business need: Delivering real-time updates to users during live sports events. WebSockets-based applications could be very viable, as they provide the necessary infrastructure for real-time data push, ensuring users receive updates without any perceptible delay.

In practice, in larger organizations, it is conceivable that the architect recommends multiple styles of API architecture to be used. For example, REST and GraphQL, depending on the service use case, could be used as part of the same ecosystem.

[Case studies: Selecting the proper API](contents.xhtml#sc3_124a)

Different industry sectors are more suited to specific API styles. In this section, we will look at some industry examples and then determine the factors that influence the choice of API style.

[E-commerce platform: REST vs. GraphQL](contents.xhtml#sc4_125a)

Scenario: An e-commerce platform with a diverse range of products, each with multiple attributes.

  * REST choice: Ideal for basic product listing and CRUD operations. However, it may lead to over-fetching data, as each product detail might require a separate API call.
  * GraphQL choice: Allows clients to request specific product information, such as size, color, and price, in a single query. This reduces bandwidth and improves performance, especially on mobile devices.
  * Decision factors: If the platform requires highly dynamic and customized content delivery, GraphQL would be more efficient. For more straightforward, less active content, REST could suffice.



[Enterprise system integration: SOAP vs. gRPC](contents.xhtml#sc4_126a)

Scenario: Integrating various legacy systems in a large enterprise, including finance, HR, and operations.

  * SOAP choice: Offers high security and well-defined contracts using WSDL, which is beneficial for integrating systems that require strict data validation and security.
  * gRPC choice: Provides high performance and efficient communication between microservices, which is helpful for real-time data processing and aggregation.
  * Decision factors: If the priority is security and adherence to enterprise standards, SOAP may be preferable. For performance and modern microservices architecture, gRPC is more suitable.



[Realtime chat application: WebSockets vs. RESTful long-polling](contents.xhtml#sc4_127a)

Scenario: Developing a chat application that requires real-time messaging.

  * WebSockets choice: Offers full-duplex communication, allowing for real-time, bidirectional messaging without the overhead of repeatedly establishing connections.
  * RESTful long-polling choice: An alternative where the client repeatedly polls the server to check for new messages. While more straightforward, it introduces latency and higher server load.
  * Decision factors: WebSockets are inherently designed for real-time communication and would be optimal for a seamless user experience in a chat application.



Some examples of using different styles are shown as follows:

  * Example 1: Social media platform using GraphQL
    * Background: A social media platform needing to serve customized content feeds to millions of users.
    * Challenge: Managing complex and varied data requests efficiently.
    * Solution: The implementation of GraphQL allowed users to fetch personalized content, such as posts, comments, and friend lists, in a single request.
    * Outcome: Reduced bandwidth usage, improved load times, and a more responsive user experience.
  * Example 2: Financial services API with SOAP
    * Background: A financial institution requires secure and reliable communication for transactions.
    * Challenge: Ensuring data integrity and security in communications between different banking services.
    * Solution: Adopting SOAP provided a highly secure communication channel with standardized protocols, meeting the industry's regulatory requirements.
    * Outcome: Enhanced security in data exchange and improved compliance with financial regulations.
  * Example 3: Online gaming platform with WebSockets
    * Background: An online gaming platform needs real-time interaction between players.
    * Challenge: Minimizing latency and ensuring synchronous gameplay.
    * Solution: Implementing WebSockets facilitates real-time data transfer, allowing for instant player interactions and game state updates.
    * Outcome: A highly engaging and interactive gaming experience with minimal latency.



[Summarizing key takeaways on API style comparison](contents.xhtml#sc3_128a)

Throughout this chapter, we have examined the different API styles, highlighted key features, and presented the appropriate choice for a particular need. Let us summarize the key takeaways on each one of the API styles that we have discussed:

  * Overview of API styles:
    * REST: It is ideal for general-purpose web services due to its stateless operations and cacheability, but it may need help with over-fetching data.
    * GraphQL: Excellently handles complex, interrelated data sets and reduces over-fetching, but can be challenging to implement and optimize.
    * SOAP: Highly secure and standardized, making it suitable for enterprise-level applications, but it tends to be lengthy and less flexible.
    * gRPC: Offers high performance and is excellent for microservices architecture but has a steeper learning curve and limited browser support.
    * WebSockets: Best for real-time applications requiring bi-directional communication, though it can be resource-intensive.
  * Decision-making insights:
    * Aligning with business goals: The choice of API style should be primarily driven by business objectives and the application's specific needs.
    * Performance and scalability: Consider the trade-offs between performance efficiency and resource utilization.
    * Complexity and future maintenance: Evaluate the development and maintenance complexity against the team's expertise and long-term application scalability.



[Future trends and considerations](contents.xhtml#sc2_129a)

API methodologies and technologies continue to evolve to cope with distinct and newer business needs. Some short-term trends that need to be evaluated by an architect while choosing API styles include:

  * Emerging API technologies: Stay informed about emerging trends, such as new API styles or enhancements to existing ones, which might offer better solutions.
  * Integration flexibility: As systems often need to interact with various external services, consider the flexibility of the API style for future integrations.
  * Monitoring and analytics: Regardless of the chosen API style, implement robust monitoring and analytics to continually assess and optimize API performance.



[Conclusion](contents.xhtml#sc2_130a)

While REST has become the de facto standard for web applications, several API styles are used. Through this chapter, we have looked at some additional styles for API creation. Specifically, we look at SOAP APIs and WebSockets. We went into some detail on each of these styles and looked at best practices and tips for using these styles.

In addition, we have examined some factors that need to be considered while choosing an architectural style. A simple comparison between the different architectural styles using industry examples and a table style format is a powerful tool for architects and is detailed in this chapter.

At the end of the chapter, the architect or engineer should be able to look at a business requirement for a service using multiple lenses (business needs, NFRs, etc.) and then determine the most appropriate style.

We are now aware of the proliferation of different API standards. The next chapter discusses the need to standardize API specifications with a focus on open specifications. We will discuss different existing API standards, including OpenAPI specification (OAS), RAML, API Blueprint, etc. An architect is much more effective by adopting one of these API standards to describe, design and collaborate on effective API development.

[Key terms](contents.xhtml#sc2_131a)

  * API style: The architectural approach and design principles used when creating an API, such as REST, RPC, or GraphQL. 
  * Resource-oriented API: An API style that focuses on exposing resources (e.g., objects, data) and the methods to interact with them, typically using HTTP methods like GET, POST, PUT, and DELETE. 
  * WSDL: An XML-based language used to describe the functionality and interface of a SOAP web service.
  * Envelope: The root element of a SOAP message that encapsulates the header and body elements.
  * Header: An optional element within the SOAP envelope that contains metadata about the message, such as authentication information or transaction IDs.
  * Body: The main element within the SOAP envelope contains the message payload, such as the request or response data.
  * Fault: An element within the SOAP body that conveys error information when a SOAP request fails.
  * SOAP binding: The specific protocol and encoding style used to transmit SOAP messages, such as HTTP, SMTP, or JMS.
  * Handshake: The initial HTTP request/response exchange between the client and server to establish a WebSocket connection.
  * Upgrade header: An HTTP header sent by the client to request a protocol switch from HTTP to WebSocket.
  * Connection header: An HTTP header sent by the client to indicate that it wants to establish a persistent connection with the server.
  * Sec-WebSocket-Key: A client-generated key is sent in the handshake request to the server to validate the WebSocket connection.
  * Sec-WebSocket-Accept: A server-generated response key is sent back to the client to confirm the successful establishment of the WebSocket connection.
  * Frame: The primary data transmission unit in a WebSocket connection, consisting of an opcode, payload length, and payload data.
  * Payload: The data transmitted in a WebSocket frame, which can be text, binary, or control messages.
  * Close frame: A particular type of WebSocket frame used to initiate the graceful closing of the WebSocket connection.




# [CHAPTER 5Embracing Open API Standards](contents.xhtml#ch05a)

[Introduction](contents.xhtml#sc2_132a)

Most companies use APIs for internal or external consumption. However, there is no unified standard for creating and defining APIs. Organizations build APIs based on their needs and standards if they exist. Developers take shortcuts during development to rapidly push their APIs out to market, often ignoring any standards.

By standardizing and using open API standards, organizations can ensure consistency, interoperability, and adherence to their scalability standards by designing and implementing APIs. This promotes reuse and better integration and enhances the developer experience.

We looked at different API architectural styles in the previous chapter and compared their advantages and use case scenarios. In this chapter, we will discuss the need for standardization and introduce open specifications shaping the API world. By adopting one of these API standards, developers can design, describe, and collaborate more effectively on API development.

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_133a)

In this chapter, we will look at the following topics:

  * Need for standardization
  * Introduction to API specification
  * OpenAPI
  * RESTful API modeling language
  * API blueprint
  * Web Services Description Language for SOAP
  * JSON schema
  * AsyncAPI for event-driven architectures
  * Postman Collections



[Objectives](contents.xhtml#sc2_134a)

In this chapter, we start with the need for standardization of APIs, then jump into the different API specifications and standards that exist while desiging these APIs. These are a combination of creating specifications, testing them, and documenting them.

By the end of this chapter, the reader should have a good understanding of the need for standardization, the different standards that exist, and the associated tools that facilitate the rapid adoption of their APIs.

[Need for standardization](contents.xhtml#sc2_135a)

Due to the lack of standardization in the API world, APIs are created using different standards. This lack of standardization creates communication barriers and reduces seamless integration. As a result, the time it takes to develop and test APIs increases and is inconsistent across the development of APIs.

There are some challenges created by not following standards. Some of them are:

  * Lack of uniformity: Data formats, authentication methods, and communication protocols can vary vastly across APIs. This incompatibility makes it harder for engineers to write applications or services that interact with each other efficiently and easily. An example of this would be different APIs returning dates in varying formats.

// Response from GET /orders

{

"orderId": 12345,

"orderDate": "2025-01-15T10:30:00Z" // ISO 8601

}

// Response from GET /shipments

{

"shipmentId": 98765,

"shipmentDate": "15/01/2025" // DD/MM/YYYY

}

  * Redundant documentation: Since each API requires separate and good documentation, it sometimes produces redundant effort and inconsistent information. This results in slower development time as developers need to spend time understanding different APIs and then possibly adapting code to incompatible interfaces.

An example would be a company with multiple teams developing APIs for different services, but due to a lack of standardization, each team documents their APIs independently.

Team A:

GET /users/{id}

Description: Retrieves user details.

Response:

{

"id": 1,

"username": "john_doe",

"email": "john.doe@example.com"

}

Team B:

GET /profiles/{id}

Description: Retrieves user profile details.

Response:

{

"userId": 1,

"userName": "john_doe",

"userEmail": "john.doe@example.com"

}

  * Limited collaboration: The lack of common standards makes collaboration between producers and consumers more complex as different APIs have different interaction methods, leading to integration complexity.
  * Inconsistent documentation: When developers do not adhere to common standards, they produce inconsistent or incorrect documentation, making it harder to develop using these APIs.

The previous example of redundant documentation is also relevant to inconsistent documentation.

  * Reusability: The lack of common standards decreases the reusability of code and tools across different APIs. By their very nature, APIs are meant to hide the complexity of the underlying logic. However, in the absence of standards, it falls upon the developer to improve efficiency and scalability, sometimes with limited knowledge.



The above factors make it clear why adopting common specifications through standardization effectively reduces the challenges faced in API development.

Some of the advantages of using common standards or a shared language for describing APIs are described as follows:

  * Seamless integration: By using a common standard or language for describing APIs, the developer only needs to understand the API specs for integrating with their application. They do not need to understand the underlying implementation and this is very useful when planning scaling and performance.
  * Efficient development: The adherence to clear and consistent documentation enables developers to integrate APIs quickly.
  * Enhanced collaboration: Standardized interfaces, specs, and data formats help effectively collaborate among teams and organizations.
  * Innovation: Reducing dependence on ad hoc APIs helps developers focus on their applications and build unique functionalities without reinventing existing and tested services.
  * Interoperability: Standardization leads to systems and applications interacting with each other in a precise and defined manner without any compatibility issues.
  * Clear documentation: A significant advantage is a clear understanding of API functionalities. This makes it easier for developers to integrate and use APIs rapidly.



[Introduction to API specifications](contents.xhtml#sc2_136a)

Open specifications are publicly available documents that define how APIs are designed, engineered, and eventually consumed. They are of use to both producers and consumers of APIs.

These specifications promote interoperability, clarity, and best practices, benefiting API producers and consumers.

Let us explore some of the open specifications used for developing APIs:

  * OpenAPI Specification (OAS): A widely adopted specification for describing RESTful APIs.
  * RESTful API modeling language (RAML): A human-readable and machine-readable language for documenting RESTful APIs.
  * API blueprint: A lightweight and human-readable language for describing RESTful APIs.
  * Web Services Description Language (WSDL): A specification for describing SOAP-based web services.
  * JSON Schema: A language for defining the structure and validation rules of JSON data used in APIs.
  * AsyncAPI for Event-Driven Architectures (EDAs): A specification for describing event-driven APIs.
  * Postman Collections: A format for grouping HTTP requests and responses used in the popular API testing tool, Postman.



We will explore a few specifications, their advantages, and some sample specifications for a payment API. This is meant to be a high-level understanding to enable you to make the initial choice and understand how it empowers developers to create effective and interoperable APIs.

[OpenAPI](contents.xhtml#sc3_137a)

OAS is a widely adopted standard for describing RESTful APIs, formerly known as the Swagger Specification. It enables documentation of API endpoints, operations, parameters, data models, and security requirements that are easily understandable by both machines and developers. This allows for both usage and discovery of these APIs and understanding of their capabilities. Hence, consumers of these APIs do not need to necessarily understand the underlying logic or the code of the consumed service.

[Key features of OAS](contents.xhtml#sc4_138a)

OAS is a popular way to document APIs with some key features, which are as follows:

  * OAS documents are written in YAML or JSON, which makes them readable for humans and easily processable for machines.
  * OAS covers all aspects of an API, including resources, operations, parameters, responses, authentication, and security.
  * It also uses JSON Schema for data modeling, which allows for the definition of the structure and rules of data exchanged through the API.
  * OAS supports a range of authentication and authorization mechanisms, ensuring secure API interactions.
  * It also offers a rich set of tools to work with OAS documents, making API design, documentation, testing, and code generation easier. This is one of the factors leading to its popularity.
  * OAS promotes a design-first approach, encouraging API planning and design at the start of the development process for better API design.
  * Importantly, it supports API evolution with explicit versioning, making maintaining and updating APIs easier.



[Benefits of using OAS](contents.xhtml#sc4_139a)

It is essential to understand the benefits of using OAS. Following are some reasons why using OAS benefits the organization and developer:

  * Having well-defined, clear, and consistent documentation helps developers to understand APIs quickly. This allows APIs to be seamlessly integrated into their applications rapidly, thereby improving developer experience.
  * OAS fosters consistent understanding and communication between API providers and consumers, resulting in better collaboration.
  * By leveraging tools and automation, OAS can significantly reduce the time required to design, document, and test APIs. This leads to an overall reduced development time.
  * Adhering to common standards improves the interoperability of APIs, enabling easier integration across diverse systems and platforms.
  * There is better communication between front-end and back-end teams and with external partners, leading to better collaboration.
  * API documentation allows the defining of security schemes that improve API security. This also will enable improvements in security technology to be incorporated as part of the standards.



[Example](contents.xhtml#sc4_140a)

Consider the following example of an OpenAPI standard for a payment processing API. This is an elementary example of using the specifications to define an API using OAS. It is not comprehensive and is not meant to be exhaustive. It is for reference and understanding of the basic structure. A code snippet for this example is shown as follows:

openapi: 3.0.0

info:

title: Payment Processing API

description: This API allows for payment processing, including creating, retrieving, and listing payments.

version: 1.0.0

servers:

\- url: https://api.paymentprovider.com/v1

paths:

/payments:

post:

summary: Create a payment

operationId: createPayment

tags:

\- Payments

requestBody:

required: true

content:

application/json:

schema:

$ref: '#/components/schemas/PaymentRequest'

responses:

'201':

description: Payment created successfully

content:

application/json:

schema:

$ref: '#/components/schemas/PaymentResponse'

'400':

description: Invalid request

components:

schemas:

PaymentRequest:

type: object

properties:

amount:

type: number

format: double

description: Payment amount

currency:

type: string

description: Payment currency

recipientId:

type: string

description: Identifier for the payment recipient

required:

\- amount

\- currency

\- recipientId

PaymentResponse:

type: object

properties:

paymentId:

type: string

description: Unique identifier for the payment

status:

type: string

description: Current status of the payment

amount:

type: number

format: double

description: Payment amount

currency:

type: string

description: Payment currency

required:

\- paymentId

\- status

\- amount

\- currency

securitySchemes:

ApiKeyAuth:

type: apiKey

in: header

name: X-API-KEY 

security:

\- ApiKeyAuth: []

A brief description of the elements in the OpenAPI example above are as follows:

  * OpenAPI version: Specifies the OAS version used.
  * Info: Provides metadata about the API, such as title, description, and version.
  * Servers: Lists the base URL(s) for the API endpoints.
  * Paths: Defines the available paths/endpoints in the API.
    * /payments: Endpoint for creating a new payment and listing all payments.
    * /payments/{paymentId}: Endpoint for retrieving details of a specific payment.
  * Components: Reusable schemas for request and response bodies.
    * PaymentRequest: Defines the structure for a payment creation request.
    * PaymentResponse: Defines the structure for a payment response.
  * Security: Describes authentication and authorization schemes, in this case, an API key.



Real-world APIs will include more detailed specifications, additional endpoints, parameters, error codes, and security definitions.

The OAS is a powerful tool for designing and documenting APIs. It produces a level of consistency that makes API consumption easier.

[Getting started with OAS](contents.xhtml#sc4_141a)

The best way to get started is to explore the official OAS documentation ([https://swagger.io/specification/](https://swagger.io/specification/)).

You can also find numerous online tutorials, tools, and resources to guide you through creating and utilizing OAS documents for your APIs.

[RESTful API modeling language](contents.xhtml#sc3_142a)

RAML is a YAML-based language that describes RESTful APIs. It features a structured and well-defined format for specifying the API's resources, methods, parameters, responses, and other information.

Objectives of RAML include the simplification of the API documentation process, making it easier for developers to design, develop, and implement APIs. A key feature is that it supports the creation of reusable and modular API definitions, resulting in a significantly speeded-up development process. 

RAML enables the automatic generation of documentation, client libraries, and server stubs. This helps maintain consistency across different stages of the entire API lifecycle. It has a user-friendly syntax and focuses on reusability, making RAML a popular choice among developers for designing and documenting APIs.

OAS dominates the RESTful API world. However, RAML provides an alternative approach.

[Key features of RAML](contents.xhtml#sc4_143a)

RAML has key features that make it important for an architect to understand. These include:

  * RAML prioritizes human readability, making it easy to understand the structure and intent of an API.
  * RAML allows you to define reusable types (reusable data structures that can be referenced across multiple endpoints) and traits (reusable behaviors, such as common headers, query parameters, or responses across multiple endpoints). This helps reduce redundancy and promote consistency across your API design.
  * RAML supports various response formats like JSON and XML, providing flexibility for different clients.
  * RAML integrates validation rules and allows the generation of mock servers for development and testing.
  * Though smaller than OAS, RAML offers tools for documentation generation, code generation, and API validation.



[Benefits of using RAML](contents.xhtml#sc4_144a)

RAML has benefits that should be considered during selection. These include:

  * RAML's focus on readability creates readily understandable API documentation. By having clear and concise documentation, RAML helps both technical and non-technical audiences better understand the API.
  * By leveraging traits and types, RAML promotes design consistency and simplifies API maintenance. This promotes reusability and maintainability.
  * Catering to diverse client needs, RAML's ability to handle different response formats enhances API flexibility. Supporting multiple formats is critical to API development versatility.
  * Validation and mocking capabilities in RAML streamline development. This helps catch errors early, enables rapid prototyping, and improves developer efficiency.



[Example](contents.xhtml#sc4_145a)

Consider the following example of a RAML standard for a payment processing API. This is a simple example of using the specifications to define an API using RAML. It is not comprehensive and is not meant to be exhaustive. It is there only for reference and to understand the basic structure. A code snippet is provided here:

#%RAML 1.0

title: Payment Processing API

version: 1.0.0

baseUri: https://api.paymentprovider.com/v1

mediaType: application/json

types:

PaymentRequest:

type: object

properties:

amount:

type: number

description: Payment amount

currency:

type: string

description: Payment currency

recipientId:

type: string

description: Identifier for the payment recipient

required:

\- amount

\- currency

\- recipientId

PaymentResponse:

type: object

properties:

paymentId:

type: string

description: Unique identifier for the payment

status:

type: string

description: Current status of the payment

amount:

type: number

description: Payment amount

currency:

type: string

description: Payment currency

required:

\- paymentId

\- status

\- amount

\- currency

A brief description of the elements in the RAML example above is as follows:

  * RAML version and metadata: The document starts with the RAML version declaration and includes metadata like the API title, version, and base URI.
  * Types: Defines data types for request and response bodies. PaymentRequest and PaymentResponse are custom types that describe the structure of the JSON payloads for requests and responses.
  * Resources and methods: The /payments and /payments/{paymentId} resources represent the API endpoints. Each resource defines methods (post and get) with descriptions, request bodies (if applicable), and response bodies.
  * URI parameters: For endpoints that require parameters (e.g., /payments/{paymentId}), the parameters are defined under uriParameters.
  * SecuritySchemes: Defines a security scheme named api_key that describes how the API is secured. This case specifies that an API key must be provided in the header X-API-KEY.
  * SecuredBy: Applies the defined security scheme to the API, indicating that the API key secures all endpoints.



[Getting started with RAML](contents.xhtml#sc4_146a)

The official RAML website [https://raml.org/](https://raml.org/) offers comprehensive documentation, tutorials, and a playground for trying out RAML.

[API Blueprint](contents.xhtml#sc3_147a)

API Blueprint is a high-level documentation format that provides a clear and easy-to-read syntax for defining RESTful APIs. It enables effective communication between different stakeholders in the API development process, such as project managers, developers, and clients. It supports automatic documentation generation, API mock servers, and testing frameworks. 

API Blueprint helps teams streamline their API design and documentation processes by prioritizing readability and offering a range of tools. As a result, API Blueprint is popular with beginners and experienced developers.

[Key features](contents.xhtml#sc4_148a)

API Blueprint format offers several features to support the development of APIs, including:

  * One such feature is agile documentation, which enables developers to update the documentation alongside the API development process, ensuring accuracy and completeness. 
  * The tooling ecosystem provides various tools for validation, mocking, and testing based on the API Blueprint format. The documentation uses familiar markdown syntax, making it accessible and easily understood.
  * It organizes documentation around API actions and the resources they interact with, prioritizing clarity to make complex APIs more understandable.
  * The flexible data modeling feature supports different data formats like JSON and YAML for request and response bodies.
  * The integrated testing feature allows developers to write validation rules and integration tests within the blueprint.
  * Additionally, various tools are available for generating interactive documentation, mock servers, and code from API Blueprint specifications.



[Benefits of using API Blueprint](contents.xhtml#sc4_149a)

API Blueprint offers a range of benefits, including:

  * Provides a clear, concise way to communicate API design among team members and stakeholders, resulting in clear communications.
  * Facilitates faster development cycles by enabling early testing and feedback through mock servers.
  * The familiar Markdown syntax makes API Blueprint easy for developers with different experiences to learn and use.
  * Clear and concise documentation fosters better communication between API providers and consumers. This helps in improving collaboration between developers.
  * Simple syntax and integrated testing enable quick API prototyping and validation.
  * Supports various data formats and offers customization options for tailored documentation, resulting in flexible options and better customization support.



[Example](contents.xhtml#sc4_150a)

Consider the following example of an API Blueprint standard for a payment processing API. This is a very simple example of using the specifications to define an API. It is not comprehensive and is not meant to be exhaustive. It is there only for reference and to understand the basic structure.

FORMAT: 1A

HOST: https://api.paymentprovider.com/v1

# Payment Processing API

Payment Processing API allows payment processing, including creating, retrieving, and listing payments.

## Group Payments

### Payments Collection [/payments]

#### Create a Payment [POST]

\+ Request (application/json)

\+ Attributes (object)

\+ amount: 100.00 (number) - Payment amount

\+ currency: `USD` (string) - Payment currency

\+ recipientId: `12345` (string) - Identifier for the payment recipient

\+ Response 201 (application/json)

\+ Attributes (object)

\+ paymentId: `abcde12345` (string) - Unique identifier for the payment

\+ status: `processed` (string) - Current status of the payment

\+ amount: 100.00 (number) - Payment amount

\+ currency: `USD` (string) - Payment currency

#### List All Payments [GET]

\+ Response 200 (application/json)

\+ Attributes (array[PaymentResponse])

### Single Payment [/payments/{paymentId}]

\+ Parameters

\+ paymentId: `abcde12345` (string) - The unique identifier of the payment

#### Retrieve a Payment [GET]

\+ Response 200 (application/json)

\+ Attributes (PaymentResponse)

\+ Response 404 (application/json)

\+ Attributes (object)

\+ error: `Payment not found` (string)

# Data Structures

## PaymentResponse (object)

\+ paymentId: `abcde12345` (string) - Unique identifier for the payment

\+ status: `processed` (string) - Current status of the payment

\+ amount: 100.00 (number) - Payment amount

\+ currency: `USD` (string) - Payment currency

A brief description of the elements in the API Blueprint example above is as follows:

  * Format and host: Specifies the API Blueprint format and the base URL for the API.
  * API name and description: Provides a title and a brief description of what the API does.
  * Group payments: Organizes related resources into a group. In this case, all endpoints related to payments are grouped together.
  * Resources and actions: Defines the endpoints (/payments and /payments/{paymentId}) and HTTP methods (POST for creating a payment and GET for listing and retrieving payments).
  * Request and response: The request attributes (for POST) and the response attributes are detailed for each action, including status codes.
  * Parameters: Specifies parameters for actions, such as paymentId for retrieving a specific payment.
  * Data structures: Defines reusable structures, like PaymentResponse, to ensure consistency in request and response formats.



[Getting started with API Blueprint](contents.xhtml#sc4_151a)

The official API Blueprint website ([https://apiblueprint.org/](https://apiblueprint.org/)) provides in-depth documentation and tutorials. It also has a live editor for experimenting with syntax. Numerous tools and integrations are available to streamline API development workflow.

[Web Services Description Language for SOAP](contents.xhtml#sc3_152a)

WSDL is an XML-based format and describes the functionality of SOAP-based web services. It provides a machine-readable explanation of how to call the service, what parameters it requires, and what data structures it returns.

WSDL serves as a contract between service providers and consumers, enabling automatic generation of client-side code to interact with the service. It specifies the service's location and the operations or methods it exposes. WSDL is an essential component of the SOAP protocol stack but can also be used with other protocols.

By using XML-based language to describe the functionality offered by a web service, it acts as a contract between the client and server and hence promotes interoperability between different software applications running on various platforms and frameworks

[Key features](contents.xhtml#sc4_153a)

The following lists some key features of WSDL:

  * WSDL describes operations and messages in detail, including data types. This results in strong typing, ensuring strict adherence to the API contract.
  * WSDL allows for describing services independent of the underlying platform or language.
  * It utilizes XML to describe web service interfaces, ensuring machine-readable and human-understandable definitions.
  * Defines web services through operations (methods) that clients can invoke.
  * Data modeling uses XML Schema to define the structure, validate the rules, and test data exchanged through the service.
  * Specifies how the web service is accessed (e.g., HTTP, SOAP), promoting platform independence and ensuring binding specifications.
  * Supports various security mechanisms like authentication and authorization, ensuring tight security integration.



[Benefits](contents.xhtml#sc4_154a)

WSDL has benefits to its usage, including:

  * WSDL facilitates the integration of diverse systems, regardless of the implementation language or platform promoting interoperability.
  * It enables automated tools to generate client and server code, reducing manual coding efforts.
  * XML documents provide comprehensive information about the web service's functionality and usage. This clear documentation is invaluable when using SOAP services.
  * Binding specifications allow web service access through different platforms and programming languages, resulting in platform independence.
  * Built-in security support and mechanisms enhance the security of web service interactions. This enforces minimal security standardization.



[Example](contents.xhtml#sc4_155a)

Following is a simplified example of a WSDL document for a basic payment processing web service, including operations for creating a payment, retrieving payment details, and listing all payments.

<?xml version="1.0" encoding="UTF-8"?>

<definitions xmlns="http://schemas.xmlsoap.org/wsdl/"

xmlns:soap="http://schemas.xmlsoap.org/wsdl/soap/"

xmlns:tns="http://www.example.com/paymentapi"

xmlns:xsd="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema"

targetNamespace="http://www.example.com/paymentapi"

name="PaymentService">

<types>

<xsd:schema targetNamespace="http://www.example.com/paymentapi">

<xsd:element name="CreatePaymentRequest">

<xsd:complexType>

<xsd:sequence>

<xsd:element name="amount" type="xsd:double"/>

<xsd:element name="currency" type="xsd:string"/>

<xsd:element name="recipientId" type="xsd:string"/>

</xsd:sequence>

</xsd:complexType>

</xsd:element>

<xsd:element name="CreatePaymentResponse">

<xsd:complexType>

<xsd:sequence>

<xsd:element name="paymentId" type="xsd:string"/>

<xsd:element name="status" type="xsd:string"/>

</xsd:sequence>

</xsd:complexType>

</xsd:element>

<!-- Additional types for other operations -->

</xsd:schema>

</types>

<message name="CreatePaymentRequestMessage">

<part name="parameters" element="tns:CreatePaymentRequest"/>

</message>

<message name="CreatePaymentResponseMessage">

<part name="parameters" element="tns:CreatePaymentResponse"/>

</message>

<!-- Additional messages for other operations -->

<portType name="PaymentPortType">

<operation name="CreatePayment">

<input message="tns:CreatePaymentRequestMessage"/>

<output message="tns:CreatePaymentResponseMessage"/>

</operation>

<!-- Additional operations -->

</portType>

<binding name="PaymentBinding" type="tns:PaymentPortType">

<soap:binding style="document" transport="http://schemas.xmlsoap.org/soap/http"/>

<operation name="CreatePayment">

<soap:operation soapAction="http://www.example.com/paymentapi/CreatePayment"/>

<input>

<soap:body use="literal"/>

</input>

<output>

<soap:body use="literal"/>

</output>

</operation>

<!-- Additional operations -->

</binding>

<service name="PaymentService">

<documentation>This service allows for payment processing, including creating, retrieving, and listing payments.</documentation>

<port name="PaymentPort" binding="tns:PaymentBinding">

<soap:address location="https://api.paymentprovider.com/paymentService"/>

</port>

</service>

</definitions>

The terms used in the WSDL code above are explained as follows:

  * definitions: The WSDL document's root element defines the web service's name and target namespace.
  * types: Contains type definitions used by the web service, defined using XML Schema. This includes complex types of request and response messages.
  * message: Defines the data being communicated during web service operations. Each message consists of one or more parts, which can be associated with specific types.
  * portType: Describes the operations (or methods) provided by the web service. Each operation specifies input and output messages.
  * binding: Specifies the communication protocols and data formats used for each operation.
  * service: Defines the web service, including its available ports (endpoints) and the service URL.



This WSDL document outlines a SOAP-based payment processing service, detailing the operations for creating payments and managing payment information.

[Getting started with WSDL](contents.xhtml#sc4_156a)

Understanding WSDL is crucial to an architect using SOAP. The W3C website ([https://www.w3.org/TR/wsdl/](https://www.w3.org/TR/wsdl/)) offers the official WSDL specification and resources.

However, it should be pointed out that RESTful APIs have become the preferred approach for modern web services due to their simplicity and flexibility. Nevertheless, understanding WSDL is valuable for working with legacy systems or specific integration scenarios.

The next few standards are not necessarily open standards for creating and using APIs. However, they are important to understand from an engineer and architect's perspective because of their importance in creating and using API standards while developing an API.

[JSON schema](contents.xhtml#sc3_157a)

JSON schema is a vocabulary that allows annotation and validation of JSON documents. It defines JSON data structure for validation, documentation, and interaction control.

APIs increasingly rely on JSON for data exchange. Hence, ensuring data integrity and consistency becomes crucial when using JSON. JSON schema provides a standard way to define the structure and validation rules for JSON data used in APIs. This specification helps prevent invalid data entering systems and promotes consistent data handling across applications.

JSON schema plays a crucial role in API documentation, providing a clear and concise way to communicate API data models to developers, which facilitates easier integration and use of APIs.

[Key features](contents.xhtml#sc4_158a)

JSON schemas have some features that are listed as follows:

  * Provides a validation mechanism for JSON data structure, ensuring it meets the defined schema.
  * Describes the structure and constraints of JSON data, making it self-explanatory.
  * Specifies the expected properties, data types, and relationships within JSON objects, clearly defining the data structure.
  * Allows for validation rules by enabling setting constraints on data values, formats, and lengths to ensure data integrity.
  * Supports multiple data types like strings, numbers, arrays, and objects for comprehensive data modeling.
  * Allows defining reusable schemas for commonly used data structures, reducing redundancy.
  * Numerous tools and libraries exist for validating JSON data against defined schemas in different programming languages.



[Benefits](contents.xhtml#sc4_159a)

Benefits of using JSON data formats include:

  * Ensures the accuracy and consistency of JSON data exchanged between systems, leading to improved data integrity.
  * Flexible support for various application requirements, from configuration files to data interchange formats.
  * Enforces validation rules, preventing invalid data from entering systems and improving data quality.
  * Clearly defines expected data formats, promoting better API understanding and usage and leading to good documentation practices.
  * Reusability and validation capabilities streamline development and prevent data-related errors, resulting in faster development.
  * Enables consistent data exchange between diverse applications that adhere to the same schema, leading to good integration practices.
  * Tools can automatically validate JSON data against the schema, automating quality checks.



[Examples](contents.xhtml#sc4_160a)

Creating JSON schema definitions for a payment API involves specifying the structure, required fields, and data types for the JSON objects used in the API requests and responses.

Here are some JSON schema examples for requesting to create a payment and the response that might be received from such an API. These schemas define the expected format of the JSON data for creating a payment API and the structure of the response that the API returns.

JSON schema for creating a payment request

This schema defines the structure for a payment creation request, specifying required fields and data types.

{

"$schema": "http://json-schema.org/draft-07/schema#",

"title": "PaymentRequest",

"type": "object",

"properties": {

"amount": {

"type": "number",

"description": "The amount of the payment"

},

"currency": {

"type": "string",

"description": "The currency of the payment"

},

"recipientId": {

"type": "string",

"description": "The identifier for the payment recipient"

}

},

"required": ["amount", "currency", "recipientId"]

}

JSON schema for payment response

This schema outlines the structure of a response from the payment API after a payment creation request, including the payment's status and details.

{

"$schema": "http://json-schema.org/draft-07/schema#",

"title": "PaymentResponse",

"type": "object",

"properties": {

"paymentId": {

"type": "string",

"description": "Unique identifier for the payment"

},

"status": {

"type": "string",

"description": "Current status of the payment"

},

"amount": {

"type": "number",

"description": "The amount of the payment"

},

"currency": {

"type": "string",

"description": "The currency of the payment"

}

},

"required": ["paymentId", "status", "amount", "currency"]

}

The key highlights of the JSON specs are as follows:

  * $schema: Declares the JSON schema version being used.
  * title: Provides a name for the schema, which helps identify it.
  * type: Specifies the type of data structure, in this case, an "object".
  * properties: Defines the object's properties (or fields), including their data types and descriptions.
  * The PaymentRequest schema's properties are amount, currency, and recipientId.
  * The PaymentResponse schema's properties include paymentId, status, amount, and currency.
  * required: Lists the properties that must be present in the object for it to be considered valid.



These JSON schema definitions provide a clear and enforceable structure for the JSON data exchanged in a payment API, ensuring that requests and responses conform to expected formats.

[Getting started with JSON schema](contents.xhtml#sc4_161a)

The official JSON schema website ([https://json-schema.org/](https://json-schema.org/)) provides detailed documentation, tutorials, and a playground for trying out schemas.

[AsyncAPI for event-driven architectures](contents.xhtml#sc3_162a)

EDA is a software design pattern where events determine the flow of the application. An event is defined as a significant change in state or an occurrence happening within an application. For example, a user clicking on a button, a sensor detecting temperature changes, a file being uploaded could all constitute events. In this architecture, services communicate by producing, detecting, or responding to these events.

AsyncAPI is an open-source specification standard for defining asynchronous APIs and supports EDAs, enabling the documentation and management of APIs that use protocols like MQTT, WebSocket, and Kafka.

EDAs rely on asynchronous event communication, enabling loosely coupled and scalable systems. AsyncAPI emerges as a crucial specification to standardize and document APIs within EDAs.

[Key features](contents.xhtml#sc4_163a)

AsyncAPIs key features include:

  * This is a protocol-agnostic standard supporting multiple messaging protocols. This means that APIs can be described and built on a variety of communication protocols without being tied to a specific one. IoT devices might use MQTT for lightweight messaging, while real-time communications might use WebSockets, and EDA architectures may use WebSockets. AsyncAPI ensures that all these systems can be documented in a unified way regardless of the underlying protocol.
  * Enables the generation of code, documentation, and even infrastructure as code (IaC), streamlining development processes.
  * It is based on event-centric description and helps define events, their payload formats, and how different components subscribe to and react to them.
  * Describes event producers (servers) and consumers, providing a complete overview of interactions in a server and consumer model.
  * Leverages JSON schema for defining event payload structures and validation rules.
  * Supports standard security mechanisms like OAuth and API keys for secure event exchange.
  * Tools exist for generating documentation, code, and test cases from AsyncAPI specifications.



[Benefits](contents.xhtml#sc4_164a)

Event-driven APIs have benefits, some of which are listed as follows:

  * Provides a clear, structured way to document event-driven APIs, improving understanding and collaboration.
  * Facilitates the design of scalable and efficient event-driven systems, essential for modern application architectures.
  * Standardizes event definitions and interactions, fostering clear communication between developers and teams.
  * Enables comprehensive documentation of event-driven APIs, enhancing understanding and collaboration.
  * Faster development: Tools and automation based on AsyncAPI accelerate the development and testing of event-driven systems.
  * Protocol agnostic specifications allow choosing the most suitable messaging protocol for your architecture.



Creating an AsyncAPI specification for a payment API involves defining the channels, messages, and components for asynchronous communication, typically over WebSocket, MQTT, or other real-time messaging protocols. This example will illustrate how an AsyncAPI document might be structured to describe a payment processing system that uses event-driven architecture for real-time notifications about payment status updates.

AsyncAPI Specification Example for a Payment API

asyncapi: '2.0.0'

info:

title: Payment Processing API

version: '1.0.0'

description: This API allows for real-time notification of payment status updates.

servers:

production:

url: wss://api.paymentprovider.com/ws

protocol: wss

description: Secured production server

channels:

paymentStatus:

subscribe:

summary: Receive updates about payment status

operationId: receivePaymentStatusUpdate

message:

$ref: '#/components/messages/PaymentStatusUpdate'

components:

messages:

PaymentStatusUpdate:

contentType: application/json

payload:

type: object

properties:

paymentId:

type: string

description: Unique identifier for the payment

status:

type: string

enum: [processed, failed, pending]

description: Current status of the payment

amount:

type: number

description: Payment amount

currency:

type: string

description: Payment currency

required:

\- paymentId

\- status

\- amount

\- currency

Some of the key terms in the code are explained here:

  * asyncapi: Specifies the AsyncAPI specification version being used.
  * info: Provides metadata about the API, including the title, version, and a brief description.
  * servers: Lists the servers (or brokers) where the API is available, including the URL and protocol used for connections. In this case, a WebSocket Secure (wss) protocol is used.
  * channels: Defines the channels through which clients can communicate with the API. Here, a paymentStatus channel is defined for subscribing to payment status updates.
    * subscribe: Indicates that clients can subscribe to messages on this channel.
    * message: References a message object that describes the data's structure.
  * components/messages: Defines reusable components, such as message payloads. The PaymentStatusUpdate message includes details about the payment's unique identifier, status, amount, and currency.
    * contentType: Specifies the message's content type, typically application/json.
    * payload: Describes the structure and content of the message payload, including required fields and their data types.



This AsyncAPI document outlines how a payment processing system can notify clients of real-time payment status updates using an event-driven architecture. By defining the channels, messages, and data structures, the specification ensures that publishers and subscribers can effectively communicate and understand the data being exchanged.

[Getting started with AsyncAPI](contents.xhtml#sc4_165a)

The official AsyncAPI website ([https://www.asyncapi.com/](https://www.asyncapi.com/)) offers comprehensive documentation, tutorials, and an online editor for experimenting with specifications.

Understanding AsyncAPIs is important if you're working with or adopting EDAs. While other specifications like OAS primarily focus on RESTful APIs, AsyncAPI empowers effective communication and development in asynchronous event-driven systems.

[Chapter 12](ch12.xhtml), Real-time, Asynchronous, and Streaming APIs on advanced topics, will describe these in greater detail.

[Postman Collections](contents.xhtml#sc3_166a)

While not strictly an open specification like the previous ones, Postman Collections plays a vital role in the API development and testing process.

Postman is a comprehensive platform streamlining the creation, testing, documentation, and maintenance of APIs. It provides developers with a user-friendly interface to send requests to APIs, view responses, and debug, thereby significantly enhancing the efficiency of the development process.

Postman supports various types of HTTP requests and can be used to test RESTful, SOAP, GraphQL, WebSocket APIs, and others. Its ability to create and organize collections of requests, automate tests, and simulate endpoints through mock servers makes it an invaluable tool for individual developers and teams.

Postman facilitates collaboration by allowing teams to share collections and environments, ensuring consistency and speeding up the API development lifecycle. Additionally, its features for documenting APIs and generating code snippets in multiple languages help maintain up-to-date documentation and ease the integration process for developers. Postman's extensive functionality and integrations with other tools make it a central hub for managing the entire API lifecycle, from design to deprecation.

[Key features](contents.xhtml#sc4_167a)

Using Postman Collections can utilize these key features:

  * Supports sharing of collections among team members, enhancing collaboration across teams and improving consistency. Collections are organized sets of API requests (including headers, parameters, body. Authentication details, etc.) and serve as a central repository for documenting, testing, and automating API interactions.
  * Enables automated testing of API endpoints, ensuring reliability and performance and seamless integration testing.
  * Collections organize related API requests and corresponding responses for clarity and easy access by grouping the requests and responses.
  * Allows storage and management of different API environments (e.g., development, staging, production) within the collection.
  * Enables writing JavaScript code to manipulate data or headers before sending requests for execution. These code snippets are called pre-request scripts and allow you to dynamically manipulate the request, set variables, perform calculations, and prepare the data programmatically. These scripts add flexibility and automation to the API testing workflows.
  * Support writing assertions and validation scripts to test and verify API responses.
  * Provides a seamlessenvironment for efficient testing and workflow management.



[Benefits](contents.xhtml#sc4_168a)

Some of the benefits of utilizing Postman Collections include:

  * Offers an intuitive interface for creating, organizing, and executing API requests.
  * Facilitates comprehensive testing of APIs, including functional tests, security tests, and performance tests.
  * Organize and reuse testing scenarios, saving time and effort, thereby improving testing efficiency.
  * Comprehensive documentation: Document API endpoints and behavior with request-response pairs in the collection.



[Example](contents.xhtml#sc4_169a)

Creating a Postman Collection for a payment API involves setting up a collection of API requests in Postman. This collection can include various operations such as creating a payment, retrieving payment details, and listing all payments. Below is a conceptual overview of how these requests might be structured within a Postman Collection. Since Postman Collections are JSON files, this example will outline the structure and content of such a file for a basic payment processing API.

{

"info": {

"_postman_id": "unique-id-for-collection",

"name": "Payment Processing API",

"description": "This collection allows for payment processing, including creating, retrieving, and listing payments.",

"schema": "https://schema.getpostman.com/json/collection/v2.1.0/collection.json"

},

"item": [

{

"name": "Create a Payment",

"request": {

"method": "POST",

"header": [],

"body": {

"mode": "raw",

"raw": "{\n \"amount\": 100.00,\n \"currency\": \"USD\",\n \"recipientId\": \"12345\"\n}",

"options": {

"raw": {

"language": "json"

}

}

},

"url": {

"raw": "{{base_url}}/payments",

"host": [

"{{base_url}}"

],

"path": [

"payments"

]

}

},

"response": []

},

{

"name": "List All Payments",

"request": {

"method": "GET",

"header": [],

"url": {

"raw": "{{base_url}}/payments",

"host": [

"{{base_url}}"

],

"path": [

"payments"

]

}

},

"response": []

},

{

"name": "Retrieve a Payment",

"request": {

"method": "GET",

"header": [],

"url": {

"raw": "{{base_url}}/payments/:paymentId",

"host": [

"{{base_url}}"

],

"path": [

"payments",

":paymentId"

],

"variable": [

{

"id": "paymentId",

"key": "paymentId",

"value": "abcde12345"

}

]

}

},

"response": []

}

],

"variable": [

{

"id": "base_url",

"key": "base_url",

"value": "https://api.paymentprovider.com/v1",

"type": "string"

}

]

}

A brief description of the elements in the example above are as follows:

  * info: Contains metadata about the collection, including a unique ID, name, and description of the API, along with the schema version of the collection format.
  * item: An array of requested items in the collection. Each item represents an API endpoint with a name, the HTTP method, request headers (if any), the request body (for POST requests), and the request URL.
  * variable: Defines collection-wide variables, such as base_url, which can be used in request URLs to avoid hardcoding values.



This JSON structure represents a Postman Collection for a payment API, allowing developers to import this collection into Postman for testing and collaboration. The collection includes examples of structuring requests for creating, listing, and retrieving payments, making API testing more efficient and standardized.

[Getting started with Postman Collections](contents.xhtml#sc4_170a)

To get started, download and install the free Postman application ([https://www.postman.com/](https://www.postman.com/)).

A first step is to create individual API requests and group them under a meaningful name to form a collection. For more complex structures, you can organize collections into folders.

Remember, while Postman Collections are valuable for internal teams or specific use cases, they do not necessarily adhere to a standardized format like other open specifications mentioned earlier.

[Conclusion](contents.xhtml#sc2_171a)

In this chapter, we explored different open API standards with examples of specifications for an API payment API.

Each standard has its benefits for describing and documenting APIs. By embracing these open standards, developers can enjoy several advantages, such as straightforward and seamless integration across various platforms and tools, faster development through standardized documentation and tooling, better collaboration between API providers and consumers, and more focus on unique functionalities and innovations rather than duplicating work.

However, it is essential to remember that selecting the appropriate standard depends on your specific requirements and preferences. Factors to consider include API complexity, team familiarity, desired level of detail, and integration with existing tools.

Regardless, adopting open standards is an investment in the future of your APIs, ensuring clarity, interoperability, and, ultimately, a more connected and innovative digital ecosystem.

The next chapter deals with API security, one of the most important aspects of API development. We will understand some of the methods of attacks by hackers. We will then describe some methods for mitigating these threats by following key architectural principles while developing the APIs.

[Key terms](contents.xhtml#sc2_172a)
