• Deprecation notice:

o Endpoint /v1/users will be deprecated on 2025-12-31. Please migrate to /v2/users.

o Include a migration guide

 Change `v1` to `v2` in your endpoint URLs.

 Update request payloads to include the `status` field.

• Increases transparency: Clear and open expectations for API consumers increases transparency. These could include Service Level Agreements (SLAs) that the API guarantees. Documentation can serve as a platform for providing feedback, leading to community-driven improvements and innovation.

The following figure summarizes the above:

Figure 9.1: Attributes that make for good documentation

[Types of API documentation](contents.xhtml#sc2_263a)

AI is transforming how API documentation is created, managed, and consumed. Using Large Language Models (LLM) to automate complex tasks, generate human-readable content, and provide intelligent insights helps API developers create comprehensive and user-friendly documentation.

API documentation can cover different topics; each type serves a specific purpose. Some examples of the topics covered in the API documentation include an emphasis on how AI can help create richer and more accurate documentation:

• Overview documentation: It describes what the API does, its target audience, and key features. AI is now simplifying the process of generating high-level summaries of APIs by analyzing the code base, endpoints, and functionality. Natural Language Processing (NLP) using LLMs is particularly helpful for this. ChatGPT, NotebookLM, etc, are good examples of tools that can be used.

• Getting started guides: These guides are aimed at new users and provide a step-by-step approach to implementing the API for the first time. AI can generate onboarding guides based on API definitions (e.g., OpenAPI specifications). Dynamic and personalized instructions for specific programming languages can be quickly and easily built using AI.

• Authentication guides: Since many APIs require authentication, these guides explain how to obtain and use API keys or other authentication mechanisms.

• Endpoint descriptions: Detailed explanations of each endpoint, including the URL, method (GET, POST, etc.), required parameters, and the expected response. AI helps in extracting endpoint details and generates structured, human-readable documentation.

• Code examples: Practical examples in various programming languages show how to make API calls and handle responses. AI shines here, making it easier to create practical and language-specific code examples (Python, Java, JavaScript, Rust, etc) that show developers how to interact with an API. In addition, AI development tools such as Cursor and GitHub CoPilot can easily write code examples directly from API descriptions.

• Error codes: Document potential errors that can occur and how to resolve them.

• SDK and library documentation: If an API has an associated SDK or library, this documentation will detail how to use it effectively.

• Interactive API explorers: Some documentation includes tools that allow developers to test API calls directly from the browser.

• FAQs: Frequently asked questions that provide quick answers to common issues developers may face.

• Changelog: A record of all changes made to the API, including new features, bug fixes, and deprecations.

[Designing effective API documentation and developer persona](contents.xhtml#sc2_264a)

An API documentation framework needs to consider the developer persona or the target audience for the documentation. The API may cater to a specific group of developers, such as those working in a particular programming language, framework, or domain. Understanding the background, skills, and experience level of the API target audience will help introduce the proper documentation for their requirements.

These are some factors to consider when identifying the target audience:

• Programming language: The languages commonly used by the target developers need to be determined. This will help the API producer to provide code examples and snippets in the relevant language(s).

• Skill level: Assess the skill level of the target developers across programming languages, frameworks, and business functionality. This will determine and influence the depth and complexity of the documentation. In many cases (especially where APIs are created for monetization), this might not be applicable or may be unknown, so it is best to plan the documentation for the largest audience and write it for a beginning developer.

• Domain or industry knowledge: APIs can be domain-neutral or specific to an industry or domain. Different domains have particular terminology, conventions, or regulatory requirements that should be addressed in the documentation. Since this is a specific kind of persona requirement, here are a few examples that illustrate this point:

o Healthcare: APIs in this domain deal with sensitive patient data. They must comply with Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act (HIPAA) regulations in the US. The documentation should outline the security measures implemented (e.g., encryption, access controls, audit logging) and the methods to utilize those.

In addition, it should document the usage of healthcare-specific terminologies such as Health Level 7 (HL 7) standards for data exchange, International Classification of Diseases (ICD) codes, and Fast Healthcare Interoperability Resources (FHIR) specifications.

o Finance: APIs in this domain handle sensitive financial transactions and account information, including sensitive customer data. The documentation should address security standards, such as the Payment Card Industry Data Security Standard (PCI DSS) for handling and storing credit card information.

 Financial terminology for money transfers includes Society Worldwide Interbank Financial Telecommunications (SWIFT) codes for bank identification and ISO 20022 standards for financial messaging.

 In banking and capital markets, terminologies using FIX codes and specific terms like settlement, reconciliation, chargebacks, etc., are used and should be explained clearly in their usage.

o Aviation: APIs in this domain include flight data, booking systems, and airport operations. Aviation-specific terminologies using International Air Transport Association (IATA) codes for airports and airlines, International Civil Aviation Organization (ICAO) standards, and standard terms like boarding pass, baggage allowance, and flight status should be marked out, especially for developers who are unfamiliar with these terms.

o E-commerce: This domain includes information like products, shopping carts, and payment transactions. The documentation should clarify the use of e-commerce-specific terminologies like Stock Keeping Unit (SKU), shopping cart, and checkouts, amongst other domain-specific terms.

• Use cases: Identifying everyday use cases for the API under design consideration is essential for personal development as it lays the groundwork for developers to integrate this API into their applications. Use cases will help the documentation prioritize the information based on them and include examples and code snippets specific to sample use cases.

Identifying the target audience and developing a persona for them helps create the right level of documentation for APIs and goes a long way toward fostering adoptability.

[Best practices for API documentation design](contents.xhtml#sc2_265a)

We laid out the reasons for identifying the developer's needs and creating a persona around them, as well as the types of documentation that could form the backbone of the API documentation system. The documentation created for APIs and other systems has led to some general best practices most suited for documentation purposes. Some of these are listed as follows:

• Provide clarity with consistency: Ensure that the language, terminology, and structure used throughout the documentation are concise, clear, and, importantly, consistent.

• Maintain a consistent format and structure: This allows users to quickly discover and navigate to the information about the APIs that they seek.

• Provide accurate and up-to-date information: Any changes, updates, or upcoming deprecations should be identified and labeled. This should also be regularly reviewed to validate the status of APIs and update as required.

• Include practical examples and code snippets: This helps users utilize the API rapidly and effectively.

• Showcase sample requests and responses: This demonstrates the endpoints' expected inputs (request parameters) and responses (messages, payload, error codes). Include error handling and troubleshooting information.

• Adopt and follow industry standards and conventions: Standards such as OpenAPI specification or API Blueprint are well established and documented. Please refer to [Chapter 5](ch05.xhtml), Embracing Open API Standards, as we talk extensively about different standards in the industry today.

• Ensure easy accessibility and usability: Developers develop using a variety of devices, and this helps the development process across various devices and platforms.

• Continuously update and improve documentation: This should be based on community and user feedback. Encourage user feedback and constantly incorporate it to improve and refine the API documentation.

• Organize the documentation by using cases and workflows: This should be the case for the specific service that the API is providing. This helps users understand how to use and combine a particular API with other APIs to achieve specific business functionality.

These best practices guide the next section, Structuring user API documentation. By following these practices, the documentation will follow modern standards, be updated with current API information, and make it easy for developers to adopt an API.

[Structuring API documentation](contents.xhtml#sc2_266a)

API documentation is a vast subject. In a large organization with hundreds or thousands of APIs, it is important to have a formal structure on the structure and the information it should contain. To help get this started, we have laid out some minimal documentation structures that should be included:

• Overview section: This is a concise summary that explains the need for the API, what it does, key features, and potential use cases. At a glance, it should give a developer an immediate understanding of the API's purpose.

• Quick guides: These are usually step-by-step tutorials to get developers up and running quickly. They should include installation instructions (docker environments, for example), basic setup, and a simple hello world code snippet to test the API minimally.

• Detailed endpoint documentation: Every endpoint should be listed, cross-referenced, and thoroughly documented with at least the following sections listed:

o Purpose

o Request parameters

o Request samples with required and optional parameters

o Success and error response with all permutations and combinations

o Response examples

o Use case examples with real-world use cases

• Authentication and authorization: Secure APIs require authentication and authorization methods to use the APIs. Explicit instructions on how to use these methods and sample requests, including access tokens, API keys, or other security mechanisms, should be mentioned.

• Error codes and messages: The detailed endpoint documentation includes errors and the messages for those endpoints. However, a section to document common error responses and their meaning helps developers get information on diagnosing and debugging issues when they arise.

• Versioning and backward compatibility: APIs will change, replace, or remove entirely during their lifecycle. Documenting the API versioning strategy with detailed logs explaining the methodology should form part of the comprehensive documentation.

• Interactive documentation: Many tools exist in the API lifecycle management framework that allows interactive documentation elements. These could include an efficient API discovery process and live API calls within the documentation to test the API's basic request and response and enhance the API's understanding and testing.

• Rate limiting and throttling: APIs could have rate limits or throttling mechanisms for various reasons, including security. This should be clearly documented. In addition, the number of requests allowed per time period, applicable limits, how to handle exceeded errors, and the retry mechanism should also be documented. This enables developers to put in checks and balances when using these APIs and to inform their users of potential errors with clear messages for debugging.

The best practices dictate that documentation should be updated in real-time as APIs evolve. The documentation should be logical and easily navigable. These best practices give you a practical approach to structuring API documentation effectively to improve the DX and reduce the learning curve. API documentation from companies like Stripe ([https://docs.stripe.com/api](https://docs.stripe.com/api)) are good reference points for the developer to consider. In addition, Swagger has an example documentation and testing hypothetical pet store (https://petstore.swagger.io/#/) that not only showcases documentation for the pet store but also allows for authentication and testing of the APIs from that demo API site.

[Localization and internationalization](contents.xhtml#sc2_267a)

Today, organizations worldwide use APIs to fulfill several business services. These APIs could be internal or external to the organization, including paid APIs. They are also used across different countries and regions. To serve this diverse user base effectively, APIs must support various languages, currencies, and other localization requirements.

Multi-language support is crucial for APIs that need to work around the world. This includes documentation, error messages, and user interfaces. Multi-language support in an API makes it easier for developers worldwide to integrate with it.

Localization and internationalization (L10n and I18n) are crucial parts of API documentation that enhance the global DX. These concepts should be incorporated into the documentation to ensure proper usage and understanding by developers with different linguistic and cultural backgrounds.

Internationalization is the process of designing and developing an API and its documentation to enable easy adaption to different languages and regions without requiring engineering changes.

Some best practices to ensure that include:

• Unicode support: This ensures that the API and its documentation can handle different character sets and scripts used in languages used worldwide.

• Avoid hard-coded strings: Use placeholders, message keys, or variables instead of hard-coded text for easy translation and localization.

• Provide language-neutral examples: In API documentation, examples should not be specific to any particular language. This will make it easy to understand universally.

• Use frameworks and libraries for consistency: There are internationalization frameworks and libraries that should be used. We will give some examples of these later in this section.

• Region-specific testing: Conduct testing with users from different regions to identify and address any localization issues.

Localization involves adapting the API documentation to a specific language and region. It considers cultural nuances, idioms, and conventions. Some key considerations for localization include:

• Accurate translation: Translated documentation should convey the same meaning as the original. Sometimes, literal translations may confuse, so care must be taken to translate using appropriate technical terminology.

• Cultural sensitivity: Different parts of the world have different cultures, which should be factored in while creating documentation. For instance, examples, icons, and images should suit the target countries or regions.

• Date, time, and currency formats: Use localized formats for dates (e.g., MM/DD/YYYY vs. DD/MM/YYYY), times, and currencies in examples and sample code snippets.

• Units of measurement: Some regions use imperial units, and others use metric units. The API and its documentation should be able to handle and convert between these units as required.

• Address and phone number formats: The structure of addresses and phone numbers are different in different parts of the world. The API should accommodate these differences.

Implementing I18n and L10n in API portals, documentation, and other media has several benefits, which are as follows:

• Worldwide audience: Localized documentation makes the API more accessible to developers in other regions, leading to broader adoption and a more extensive consumer base.

• Improved user experience: Developers find it easier to use when documentation is in a language they understand and is tailored to their cultural context.

• Reduced support costs: Clear, localized documentation and self-service portals help reduce the support queries from developers, saving time and resources for the API producer.

• Competitive advantage: Localized support could set an organization apart from its competition, which might not have invested in I18n and L10n, attracting more consumers.

[Internationalization and localization frameworks and libraries](contents.xhtml#sc3_268a)

Implementing I18n and L10n in API documentation requires additional support from translators, localization experts, and developers familiar with the target languages. One option that is quite useful and helpful is to use i18n and l10n frameworks and libraries for different programming languages. Some examples of the different languages are:

• JavaScript:

o I18next: This is a powerful internationalization framework for JavaScript with a simple and flexible API.

o React-intl: An internationalizing library for React applications. It provides components and APIs for formatting dates, numbers, and strings.

• Java:

o Java resource bundles: This is a built-in mechanism in Java for internationalizing applications using resource bundles.

o ICU4J: These are a set of Java libraries that provide Unicode and globalization support, including message formatting, date-time parsing, etc.

o GNU gettext: This internationalization and localization system is used with Java through libraries such as gettext-commons.

• Python:

o This internationalization and localization system is available for Python and uses message catalogs for translations.

o Babel: This is a set of tools for Python applications. It includes message extraction, catalog management, and number/date formatting.

These are a few examples of internationalization and localization frameworks; many more are available. Libraries are also available for other programming languages, such as Ruby, PHP, C#/.NET, Swift (for iOS), and Android (using Java).

Support for different languages, currencies, date/time formats, and other localization requirements is essential for today’s global API-first economy. This expands the potential user base and corresponding monetization value.

[Interactive API explorers](contents.xhtml#sc2_269a)

Interactive API explorers have emerged as powerful tools to enhance the DX with hands-on tools. These explorers support a hands-on, interactive environment to explore and test API endpoints, including actual requests and responses and providing error messages. This makes it easy for developers to integrate APIs into their applications, sometimes even when the API is not entirely implemented and deployed.

In this section, we will discuss the features and benefits of these interactive API explorers and provide a sample of the available tools.

The features that API explorers provide include:

• Instant API exploration: It allows developers to explore and interact with API endpoints without writing code or setting up a development environment. It usually takes a few clicks to input parameters, execute requests, and view the responses. This immediate feedback lets the developer quickly grasp the API's functionality and capabilities.

• Request and response visualization: API explorers visually represent API requests and responses. This helps developers understand the structure and format of the data being exchanged. This includes required headers, request parameters, and response payloads in a visual interface.

• Authentication and authorization: Many of these tools include built-in support for authentication and authorization methods. Developers can include their API keys, tokens, or other credentials within the explorer interface, thus automating the process. This allows developers to focus on exploring and testing the API endpoints.

• Request customization: API requests allow request parameters, headers, and request bodies to be customized. API explorers allow modification of these parameters to test different scenarios and edge cases. This capability is useful in testing different input combinations and observing the corresponding API responses. Using this feature, developers can thoroughly test the functionality and other aspects of the API and determine its robustness and fit for purpose.

• Response inspection and debugging: This powerful feature allows API responses to be inspected and debugged. Developers can view the response status codes, headers, and payload in a formatted and readable output. Additionally, there are usually options for filtering, searching, and navigating the response data. This helps troubleshoot issues that might be present on the API producer side. For example, Postman allows you to do the following:

o Send a GET request to an API endpoint.

o Inspect the response in the “Body” tab, which has the response data

o Use the “Tests” tab to write and run tests on the response, checking status codes or verifying the specific data in the response.

• Code generation and SDK integration: Some advanced API explorers can generate code snippets in different programming languages based on the API requests made through the explorer. These are ready-to-use code samples that developers can easily integrate into their applications. Some API explorers integrate with popular SDKs, allowing the developers to transition from exploration to implementation seamlessly. For example, Postman integrates with popular frameworks like Express.js (Node.js), Flask (Python), and Spring Boot (Java).

• Collaboration: This is an important feature in enhancing the DX. API explorers can create and share API collections, making working on API integration and troubleshooting easier. It improves productivity and consistency across the development team.

• Integration with API documentation: One handy feature is the ability of the API explorer to integrate with documentation tools. This allows for a seamless experience of exploring and testing API endpoints within the documentation, which is highly efficient and makes the entire process streamlined.

The above points highlight how API explorers have transformed developers' interactions with APIs. Providing this visual and interactive environment allows them the freedom to explore, understand, and test APIs quickly. API explorers will continue to be an effective tool for developers, helping them rapidly build robust and reliable applications.

[Tools for documentation and interactive API exploration](contents.xhtml#sc3_270a)

This section will look at tools to generate and host API documentation. These tools also allow API exploration, forming a highly integrated documentation portal.

• Swagger (OpenAPI): This is a widely used open-source framework for designing, building, documenting, and consuming APIs. It provides tools including Swagger Editor, Swagger UI, and Swagger Codegen. This set of tools allows API documentation creation, visualization, and production.

• Postman: This popular API development and testing tool offers API documentation features. It allows API documentation creation and publication, including various authentication methods, descriptions, request/response examples, and code snippets. It has an interactive API console where developers send requests and can then view the response received. It supports different authentication mechanisms like OAuth, API keys, and JWT. However, it is not a dedicated documentation tool like Swagger.

• Apigee API console: Apigee is a complete API management platform with an interactive API console that allows developers to explore and test APIs. This has a user-friendly interface for making API requests and viewing responses. It is tightly integrated with Apigee’s API management capabilities, providing a comprehensive API explorer tool.

• ReadMe: This is a platform for creating and hosting API documentation with a user-friendly interface for writing and organizing API documentation, including endpoints, parameters, and code samples. It also allows interactive API exploration, allowing API calls directly from the documentation. It has other features, including API analytics and changelog management.

• Apiary: Besides designing, documenting, and testing APIs, Apiary offers a collaborative environment for creating API blueprints using API Blueprint language and supports OpenAPI. It generates interactive documentation and allows developers to explore and test endpoints.

• Slate: This is an open-source tool for creating responsive API documentation. It uses a simple markdown-based language to define the API documentation structure and content. It generates static HTML pages that can be easily hosted and shared. 

Additional information can be found at: [https://github.com/slatedocs/slate](https://github.com/slatedocs/slate)

• ReDoc: This is an open-source tool for generating documentation from OpenAPI specifications. It creates a responsive and interactive documentation site with a modern design. ReDoc supports API exploration, allowing engineers to try out API endpoints. GitHub repository: [https://github.com/Redocly/redoc](https://github.com/Redocly/redoc)

• Stoplight: Besides designing, documenting, and testing APIs, Stoplight has a visual editor for creating API specifications using various API formats (OpenAPI, JSON schema, and API Blueprint). It also has a user-friendly interface for exploring and testing endpoints. Website: [https://stoplight.io/](https://stoplight.io/)

• RESTer: This browser extension is available for Chrome and Firefox browsers and allows for interactive API exploration. It has a simple interface for making API requests, viewing responses, and organizing API collections. It supports different authentication methods and allows developers to customize headers and parameters.

• Insomnia: A cross-platform API client and explorer with an intuitive interface for interacting with APIs. It features request chaining, environment variables, and code generation. It supports different authentication methods and allows organization and sharing of API requests.

• ReadTheDocs: This open-source documentation hosting platform supports Sphinx docs written in reStructuredText and Markdown documents. It features version control, a powerful search engine, and supports multiple languages.

• Docusaurus: This was developed as a project for easily and quickly building, deploying, and maintaining open-source project websites. It supports Markdown, documentation versioning, localization, and many other features.

The above list is a summary of the many tools available for generating, hosting, and, in many cases, exploring the APIs with a lot of customizable options while exploring these APIs. Each tool has its features, customization options, and integration capabilities, and the selection of a tool will depend on many factors, including enterprise standards, support options, features, and cost.

A summary (non-exhaustive) table that details the different tools are shown as follows:

Figure 9.2: Tools for documentation

Interactive API explorers have transformed the API adoption process and offer multiple options to enhance the DX.

[Developer portals and dashboards](contents.xhtml#sc2_271a)

Developer portals and dashboards are essential tools for enhancing the DX. These platforms act as the interface between API producers and consumers, offering numerous resources, tools, and information to facilitate the integration and usage of APIs.

A well-designed developer portal should be a one-stop solution for developers, providing them with everything they need to get started with an API quickly. This should include comprehensive documentation, code samples, tutorials, and guides to help developers understand the API. Developer portals reduce the learning curve and help develop and integrate APIs rapidly.

The following are some essential components of a developer portal that should be part of any tool or gateway being introduced. Many of these developer portals would include the best practices of API development portals introduced earlier in this chapter. These include:

• API documentation:

o Detailed documentation for each API, including endpoints, request/response formats, authentication methods, and example code snippets.

o Clear explanations of API functionality, parameters, and error handling.

o Changelog and version history to keep developers informed of updates and changes.

• Getting started guide:

o Step-by-step tutorials and guides to help developers quickly set up and start using the APIs.

o Code samples and examples in popular programming languages.

o Instructions on how to obtain API keys, access tokens, or other authentication credentials.

• API reference:

o Comprehensive reference documentation for all API endpoints, including request methods, headers, parameters, and response formats.

o Detailed descriptions of each API resource, along with their properties and relationships.

o Information on rate limits, usage quotas, and any applicable constraints.

• Interactive API console:

o An interactive tool that allows developers to test API requests and view responses directly within the developer portal.

o Ability to input parameters and headers and request bodies to simulate API calls.

o Immediate feedback on API responses, including status codes and response data.

• SDKs and libraries:

o Downloadable Software Development Kits (SDKs) and libraries in various programming languages to simplify API integration.

o Pre-built client libraries that abstract the low-level details of making API requests.

o Documentation and examples specific to each SDK or library.

• Authentication and security:

o Clear instructions on how to authenticate and secure API requests.

o Information on supported authentication methods, such as API keys, OAuth, or JWT tokens.

o Guidelines on best practices for handling sensitive data and ensuring secure API usage.

• Support and community:

o Forums or discussion boards where developers can ask questions, report issues, and engage with the API provider's support team and other developers. Examples include GitHub and StackOverflow.

o FAQs section addressing common queries and concerns.

o Contact information for reaching out to the API provider's support team.

• API status and monitoring:

o Real-time information on the current status and availability of the APIs.

o Uptime metrics and historical data on API performance and reliability.

o Notifications and alerts for planned maintenance, updates, or any service disruptions.

• Billing and usage:

o Details on pricing plans, billing metrics, and usage limits associated with the APIs.

o Tools for monitoring API usage, tracking consumption, and generating usage reports.

o Billing and payment information for subscribing to API plans or managing account billing.

• Developer onboarding:

o A streamlined process for developers to sign up and create an account on the developer portal.

o Profile management features allow developers to update their information and preferences.

o Mechanisms for generating and managing API keys or access tokens.

• Tutorials:

o Walk developers through the process of using the API in a structured and beginner-friendly manner.

o A hands-on learning experience for developers through specific tasks or building applications.

o Well organized, well written, and includes code snippets with explanations in a step-by-step manner.

• Guides:

o It is complete and more comprehensive than tutorials covering broader topics.

o In-depth explanations, best practices, and recommendations on specific topics are essential for guides.

o Guides can also cover specific topics like authentication, error handling, rate limiting, and other features of the API ecosystem.

• Use cases:

o Demonstrate how the API can be applied to solve real-world problems.

o Highlight the API’s functionality by showcasing practical examples and scenarios.

o Help developers understand the API's potential applications and allow them to apply them to similar scenarios they face in their applications.

The components described above are the gateway to exploring the potential of the APIs. Providing a comprehensive and user-friendly experience to the organization’s catalog of APIs improves developer productivity and encourages the adoption of existing APIs.

Developer dashboards give developers a personalized view of their API usage and performance. Developers typically need to log into their accounts, granting them access to the metrics related to their API consumption, track usage, requests, response time, and error rates. This helps developers optimize their applications and resolve issues the tracked metrics highlight.

[Performance metrics and monitoring for developer experience](contents.xhtml#sc2_272a)

In the lifecycle of an API, enhancing the DX is critical to fostering adoption and improving productivity and consumer satisfaction among the developer and architect community. One method used to achieve this is to implement performance metrics and monitoring of the APIs and their usage to ensure that the API documentation meets the needs and expectations of its users. Most API lifecycle management tools already have some form of these metrics available and need to be configured.

Here are some helpful performance metrics that help track the DX:

• Page load time: This monitors the time it takes for API documentation pages to load completely. Tools like Google PageSpeed Insights or Lighthouse measure are used to measure and improve page performance.

• Search functionality: This metric helps track the usage and effectiveness of the search feature by monitoring parameters such as search query volume, search result relevance, and time taken to display search results.

• Navigation and discoverability: This measures and analyzes the user navigation patterns and click paths within the API documentation. It helps identify popular sections and frequently accessed pages that can be optimized.

• Code sample usage: Monitoring the code sample views, copies, and successful executions provides feedback on the relevance of the code samples.

• API playground and interactive tools: This measures the usage of interactive tools like API playgrounds and sandboxes by tracking the number of API requests made through these tools.

• Documentation feedback and analysis: These metrics need to monitor developers' sentiment and satisfaction levels using the documentation. Metrics like unique visitors, page views, and time spent are other metrics that can be tracked. They can be analyzed to identify common pain points or feature requests.

• API key and token management: API keys or other access tokens management are part of the developer portal. These can be API key creations, revocation, and usage patterns.

Collecting, monitoring, and analyzing API documentation system metrics helps identify issues and opportunities for improvement and allows data-driven optimizations. A sound documentation subsystem of the API development life cycle contributes to an optimized DX.

[Conclusion](contents.xhtml#sc2_273a)

The DX is integral to the adoption and developer satisfaction using an API. This chapter focuses on the significance of comprehensive and well-designed API documentation. We talked about the need for designing comprehensive documentation. As part of this section, we discussed the developer persona that most benefits from having proper, complete documentation, different types of API documentation, design principles, and best practices. We laid out some best practices and ended that section, covering the importance of structuring API documentation logically and intuitively to facilitate developer understanding and usage.

Interactive API explorers are highlighted as valuable tools to enhance the DX. This section explains the different aspects of interaction with APIs (test endpoints, view requests, and responses). It then gives some examples of tools, such as Swagger, Postman, and Apiary, that support documentation and API exploration. 

Developer portals and dashboards are discussed in centralizing API documentation, resources, and support, as well as the essential components of these portals and dashboards, and offer best practices for designing, structuring, tools, and delivering effective API documentation.

The next chapter talks about the operational aspect of developing APIs. Scaling and performance are key to allowing users to utilize APIs without worrying about the ability to scale gracefully. We discuss issues such as load balancing, caching strategies, rate limiting, and throttling. In conjunction with the other chapters, this chapter gives the API architect and developer the mindset to focus on scalability from the design to implementation phases.

[Key terms](contents.xhtml#sc2_274a)

• Documentation: Written materials about a product, service, or system.

• Developer experience: The overall experience of a developer when working with a particular tool, platform, or ecosystem.

• OpenAPI Specification: A standard, language-agnostic interface for describing, producing, consuming, and visualizing RESTful web services.

• Postman: A popular API development and testing tool that helps developers create, share, test, and document APIs.

• API reference: A comprehensive guide that provides detailed information about an API's endpoints, parameters, and responses.

• Code samples: Examples of code snippets demonstrating how to use an API in various programming languages.

• API Sandbox: An environment that allows developers to test and experiment with an API without affecting real-world data or systems.


# [CHAPTER 10API Performance and Scaling](contents.xhtml#ch10a)

[Introduction](contents.xhtml#sc2_275a)

This chapter will explore the requirements and associated challenges when designing and implementing APIs. Scaling and performance for APIs are not limited to design and implementation. Monitoring APIs in production and upgrading based on these observations is part of the life cycle of the API. An API designed and optimized for performance can handle large traffic volumes, even during burst activity periods, providing consumers with a smooth and responsive experience.

Some fundamental scalability principles and specific factors, such as vertical and horizontal infrastructure scaling, impact API performance. Caching, in-memory caches, and content delivery networks all play an important part in improving API response times and reducing server load.

Selecting the appropriate architectural patterns and design practices promotes scalability and performance during API development. An important API architecture used widely to help scalability and performance is asynchronous processing APIs, message queues, and event-driven architectures. These architectures can handle high requests and ensure smooth operation under heavy loads. We discuss this architectural pattern in [Chapter 12](ch12.xhtml), Real-time, Asynchronous, and Streaming APIs. Additionally, API gateways are fundamental to distributing loads across servers and managing API requests efficiently. We discuss the idea of API gateways as part of [Chapter 13](ch13.xhtml), API Management Platforms and Gateways.

While we talk about the design elements to improve performance and scalability, post-deployment operational monitoring and logging are critical, and observability is highlighted as a key principle for ensuring the reliability, performance, and scalability of APIs.

Performance optimization is a continuous process. It involves efficient design of the API, implementation, and continuous monitoring of the different parameters.

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_276a)

We will cover the following topics in this chapter:

  * Designing APIs for performance and scalability
  * Performance testing and monitoring APIs



[Objectives](contents.xhtml#sc2_277a)

By the end of the chapter, you should comprehensively understand APIs' scaling and performance considerations. This should provide you with the knowledge and tools to design, implement, and optimize APIs that handle high traffic volumes while delivering exceptional performance.

[Designing APIs for performance and scalability](contents.xhtml#sc2_278a)

A few key considerations and best practices exist when designing APIs for performance and scalability. We have discussed these in each chapter, but in this chapter, we will discuss all the dimensions listed as follows:

  * Choosing the right architecture:
    * We have previously discussed choosing the right API architecture style (REST, GraphQL, gRPC, SOAP, etc.). A more detailed examination of this is in [Chapter 2](ch02.xhtml), Beyond REST, and [Chapter 4](ch04.xhtml), Crafting APIs.
    * Sometimes, using a microservices architecture to break down the API into smaller, independent deployable services makes it much more efficient and scalable.

An example of this could be a large e-commerce platform that is a monolithic service that provides different functionalities such as user authentication, product catalog, inventory management, etc.

In a microservices architecture, the above components or services would only handle its functionality. So, there could be user authentication microservices (handling user registration, user profiles, authentication, etc.) and product catalog microservices (managing product catalogs, including product details, categories, and attributes). The e-commerce application could be a massive conglomeration of microservices, each performing a business service. Since each microservice is granular and small in its code base and infrastructure requirements, it can be independently scaled for performance.

    * We have discussed stateless design (refer to [Chapter 2](ch02.xhtml), Beyond REST, and [Chapter 3](ch03.xhtml), Fundamentals of API Design). This ensures scalability and easier horizontal scaling.
    * Implement asynchronous processing and message queues for resource-hungry tasks. We discuss this further in [Chapter 12](ch12.xhtml), Real-time, Asynchronous and Streaming API's.
  * Optimize data transfer
    * Efficient data formats like JSON or protocol buffers minimize payload size and network transfers.

To illustrate this, let us look at a scenario where a mobile application fetches user profiles. A straightforward implementation in XML and JSON is shown below, along with a comparison of sizes.

XML:

<user>

<id>123</id>

<name>John Doe</name>

</user>

Payload size: ~ 54

JSON:

{"id":123,"name":"John Doe"}

Payload size: ~27 bytes

    * Pagination and filtering mechanisms limit the amount of data transferred in a response.
    * In certain circumstances, compression techniques (e.g., gzip) can be leveraged to reduce network bandwidth.
  * Caching implementation
    * Caching implemented at different levels (e.g., application, client-side, database) reduces the load on the backend systems.
    * API services can implement caching headers to enable client-side caching and reduce unnecessary requests.
    * Distributed caching systems (e.g., Redis, Memcached) improve performance and scalability.
  * Database optimization
    * Design efficient database schemas and indexes to optimize query performance.
    * Use database connection pooling to minimize the overhead of establishing new connections.
    * Implement database sharding or partitioning techniques to distribute load across multiple database instances.
  * Rate limiting and throttling
    * Implement a rate-limiting mechanism to prevent abuse and excessive requests.
    * Throttle API requests to control the rate and ensure fair usage of resources.
    * Different rate limits are available for different API tiers or user roles.
  * Auto load balancing and auto-scaling
    * Load balancers should be used to distribute incoming requests across multiple API instances. Balancing the workload across servers prevents any single server from being overwhelmed.
    * The number of API instances (servers running the application) should be automatically adjusted based on incoming traffic.
    * Containerization (e.g., Docker) and orchestration platforms like Kubernetes can be used for efficient scaling and management. Containers allow scaling of application instances. For example, if the website gets more traffic, additional containerized instances of the application can be quickly spun up to handle the increased load. Similarly, containers can be stopped when demand decreases, saving resources and costs.
  * Monitoring and logging
    * Comprehensive monitoring and logging mechanisms should be in place to track API performance and other patterns.
    * Observability should be implemented as part of monitoring to gain insights into a system's internal state and behavior.
    * Alerts and notifications should be set up to identify and address performance issues protectively.
  * API testing and optimization
    * Testing before deployment is key to identifying bottlenecks and optimizing API performance.
    * Tools such as Apache JMeter can simulate high-load scenarios and measure response times.
    * Continuous monitoring and optimization of the API should be performed by analyzing how it is used in the real-world, and these metrics can be used to improve an API's performance, functionality, and usability.



[Load balancing and traffic distribution](contents.xhtml#sc3_279a)

Load balancers are an important aspect of scaling APIs effectively. It is the process that distributes incoming network traffic across multiple servers to optimize their utilization and reduce response times. API requests are distributed according to specific algorithms among numerous instances of the API service. This helps prevent any one service from becoming overloaded.

These concepts can also be applied to stateful APIs. However, they require additional strategies and configurations due to the need to manage and maintain user session data or application data. One such strategy that some load balancers support is session persistence (sticky sessions), where all requests from a particular client are routed to the same server where their session data exists. For example, Nginx, HAProxy, and AWS Elastic Load Balancers (ELB) support sticky sessions.

There are other methods for stateful applications, including distributed session management, where the session data is stored in a shared centralized storage system (database of in-memory stores). Any server in the cluster can then access the session state without having to be routed to a particular server.

The following figure depicts a scenario where multiple servers service requests from different clients, with the load balancer determining which server gets the request:

Figure 10.1: Load balancer in action

Benefits include improved scalability and an increase in reliability. In addition, the load balancer provides fault tolerance by distributing traffic across multiple instances. If one instance fails, the load balancer can detect that and redirect traffic, minimizing downtime.

Several load-balancing strategies are available. Strategy selection is done based on specific requirements and characteristics of the application. Some of these load-balancing strategies include:

  * Round robin: The load balancer distributes incoming requests sequentially across the available API instances (possibly in a pool of servers) in a cyclic way. Each instance receives an equal number of requests, resulting in a fair load distribution. It is the easiest to implement but does not consider the different capacities of the various servers.
  * Least connections: The load balancer assigns incoming requests to the API server with the least active connections. This balances the load and the current workload of each instance. This method assumes that servers with fewer connections have spare capacity compared to their peers.
  * IP hash: The client IP address determines which API instance should receive the request. This strategy consistently routes requests from the same client to the same server instance. This sometimes helps in maintaining session affinity.


  * Weighted distribution: Each API instance is assigned a weight based on the server's capacity or performance metric. The load balancer then assigns traffic to the servers and is proportional to the assigned weights. This allows the requests to be distributed based on an algorithm with the server having the capacity to allocate the API request at any given time. This allows more powerful instances to receive more traffic. The weighted distribution method reduces the challenges faced by the round-robin method.



In addition to load balancing, traffic distribution techniques route API traffic based on specific criteria or policies.

Some of these traffic distribution techniques include:

  * Path-based routing: API requests can be routed based on the URL path of the request. Different paths can be mapped to specific API services or instances, allowing for granular control over request handling.
  * Header-based routing: The load balancer examines the headers of incoming requests and then, based on predefined rules, routes them to specific locations. This method enables routing based on factors like user authentication, application version, or device types.
  * API gateway routing: In many organizations, the API gateway is the single entry point for API requests and provides advanced traffic distribution capabilities. It handles various tasks, including request and response routing, authentication, rate limiting, etc. In [Chapter 13](ch13.xhtml), API Management Platforms and Gateways, we discuss API gateways in greater detail due to their importance in the API lifecycle.



[Implementation of load balancers](contents.xhtml#sc4_280a)

Load balancers are implemented in a variety of ways; two common ones are described as follows:

  * Software load balancer: These are implemented by running specialized software on commodity hardware or virtual machines. They handle different protocols and offer flexibility, cost-effectiveness, and multiple hardware options, including on-premises or cloud environments. Examples include HAProxy, Nginx, and Apache HTTP server with the mod_proxy module.
  * Hardware load balancers: These are dedicated physical machines designed especially for load balancing. They offer hardware-based scalability and advanced features not typically found in software balancers. They are more expensive than software because they usually have specially implemented hardware accelerators built into them. Examples of vendor-based products include F5 Big-IP, Citrix ADC, and Kemp LoadMaster.



The load balancers usually support the various algorithms for traffic distribution and have the capacity for health checks on servers based on different criteria. Many offer session persistence capabilities, SSL/TLS offloading (where SSL/TLS encryption and decryption are offloaded from backend servers), and robust monitoring and logging features as part of their capabilities.

[Caching and content delivery](contents.xhtml#sc3_281a)

Caching improves performance, reduces latency, and optimizes resource usage. This section discusses the concepts of caching and content delivery for APIs and implementing them effectively.

Caching is a method used to store frequently accessed data for rapid access. It is stored in memory or dedicated cache servers. Caching API responses help return subsequent requests for the same data from the cache instead of being processed by the API server. This reduces the server load while improving response times.

The following figure shows how a consumer app works with and without a cache:

Figure 10.2: Caching API requests

There are different types of caching. Standard caching methods include:

  * Client-side caching: In this method, the client devices (web browser, mobile apps, etc.) store the API responses for later use. This can be achieved using specific HTTP headers such as Cache-Control and Expires to specific caching policies.
  * Server-side caching: In this case, API responses are stored server-side or on a dedicated cache server. Caching solutions include Redis, Memcached, and Varnish. Server-side caching can significantly reduce loads on API servers, improving overall performance.



There are a few caching strategies that are in use to ensure cached data is valid:

  * Time-based caching: This sets an expiration time for cached responses. At the expiry of the specified time, the cache data is considered invalid and is refreshed from the API server.
  * Invalidation-based caching: This is a more efficient and complex caching strategy where the cached data is invalidated when the underlying data changes, ensuring clients always receive valid data.
  * Conditional caching: HTTP headers such as ETag and Last-Modified are used to determine if the cached data is still valid. The API server can return a 304 Not Modified response if the data has not changed. This indicates that the data is valid and usable.



Caching is a complex subject, and there are a few considerations to consider when ensuring the data is valid when being served to clients. These include:

  * Cache consistency: Cached data must be consistent with the actual data in the server, and an appropriate cache invalidation mechanism must be implemented to update or remove stale data.
  * Cache eviction policies: These policies must be defined during design to determine when and how the cached data must be removed. Some policies include Least Recently Used (LRU) and Least Frequently Used (LFU).
  * Cache size and scalability: The cost of caching is mainly determined by the size of the cache, which is dependent on the scalability requirements. Expected data volumes and access patterns help define these parameters.



[Content delivery](contents.xhtml#sc4_282a)

Content delivery is the efficient distribution of data to clients across different geographies. It involves using content delivery networks (CDNs) to cache and serve responses from servers closer to the clients. This reduces latency and improves performance.

CDNs are a distributed network of servers that cache and serve content. They are usually located closer to the end user. By leveraging CDNs, API responses can be delivered from nearby servers closer to the end-users. Popular CDN providers include Akamai, Cloudflare, and Amazon CloudFront.

API response can be cached on CDN servers by configuring the appropriate caching headers and policies. It can be integrated with APIs by updating DNS records to route API responses through CDN edge servers.

[Scalability techniques](contents.xhtml#sc3_283a)

When designing API, scalability considerations should be considered at the outset and refined during implementation and continuous monitoring. Scalability considerations involve software development and other aspects, including infrastructure, data storage, caching, load balancing, and more. We have considered load distribution and caching techniques in previous sections. Here, we will focus on other methods, including horizontal and vertical scaling considerations.

[Horizontal scaling techniques](contents.xhtml#sc4_284a)

This is also known as scaling out, and it is a technique used to improve scalability and performance by adding more instances or servers to handle the increasing workloads. This method does not rely on one powerful machine but instead distributes the load across multiple servers, allowing for more concurrent requests. One significant benefit here is that this technique allows for growth. Some commonly used horizontal scaling methods include load balancing, caching, stateless architectures, and asynchronous processing (discussed in [Chapter 12](ch12.xhtml), Real time, Asynchronous and Streaming APIs). Other techniques that we have not addressed in the book are described as follows:

  * Database sharding: The database is partitioned horizontally by splitting the data across multiple instances. Each database instance handles a subset of the data, allowing for parallel processing and improved performance. This technique enables horizontal scaling of the data layer.
  * Auto-scaling: Auto-scaling mechanisms that automatically adjust the number of server instances based on incoming requests are standard, especially in virtual instances on the cloud. Defining scaling policies and thresholds to add or remove server instances ensures that the API can handle sudden spikes in traffic.
  * Microservices architecture: This style breaks down the API into small and loosely coupled microservices that are deployed and scaled independently. Each microservice focuses on a specific functionality and allows for fine-grained scaling of individual components.
  * Containerization and orchestration: Containerization technologies like Docker can package the API and its dependencies into a lightweight and portable container for scalable deployment. Orchestration platforms like Kubernetes manage and scale the containerized API instances automatically.



When implementing horizontal scaling techniques, it is important to consider factors such as data consistency, state management, and inter-service communications. Monitoring and automation are key to establishing and maintaining a horizontally scaled API ecosystem.

[Vertical scaling techniques](contents.xhtml#sc4_285a)

Vertical scaling is also known as scaling up and involves increasing the capacity and resources of a single server to handle higher loads. This section will explore certain vertical scaling techniques that can be applied to APIs. Some vertical scaling techniques include upgrading hardware (e.g., increase CPU cores, add RAM, upgrade storage), optimizing server configuration (e.g., operating system configuration, fine-tuning web server and application server), and implementing caching (e.g., in-memory caching, application-level caching). In addition, vertical autoscaling can be implemented by leveraging capabilities provided by cloud platforms such as AWS and Azure. These platforms automatically adjust server capacity using predefined metrics and thresholds.

While vertical scaling techniques provide significant improvements, there are limitations to how much a single server can be scaled up. A combination of vertical and horizontal scaling, along with continuous monitoring, is required to optimize API infrastructure.

[Edge computing and serverless caching](contents.xhtml#sc3_286a)

Edge computing has emerged as a powerful method for enhancing scalability and improving performance. It is a distributed computing paradigm that brings computation and data storage closer to the sources of data, especially IoT devices or end-user devices (e.g., mobile and POS devices). Processing data at the network's edge, closer to where it is generated or consumed, has quite a few benefits. The processing of requests at the network's edge and closer to the end users decreases the round-trip time of API calls, which is beneficial, especially for applications requiring real-time or near real-time responses, such as video streaming or IoT applications. Since the number of consuming devices is essentially the edge devices, this architecture distribution enables the offloading of the workload from central servers, reducing the burden on the servers. The decrease in traffic to central servers for processing also reduces the dependency on the network and improves the overall reliability and availability of the APIs.

[Serverless caching](contents.xhtml#sc4_287a)

With the advent of serverless computing, available in AWS (Lambda), Google, and Azure (Functions), serverless caching is a new way of implementing caching without the need for dedicated caching infrastructure. These cloud providers offer corresponding caching services that work well with serverless functions. Amazon ElastiCache and Azure Cache for Redis are two examples that provide high-performance and in-memory caching capabilities. These services are easily integrated with serverless functions to store and retrieve cached data. Serverless platforms provide cache invalidation settings, including time-to-live (TTL), to ensure that the cache provides valid data. The advantages of serverless caching include dynamic scaling and provisioning more instances to handle sudden spikes in traffic without manual intervention. Serverless computing is based on paying for actual computation and storage resources consumed during the execution of caching functions. Hence, it is more cost-efficient than setting up a dedicated caching infrastructure. Additionally, this method simplifies and reduces the complexity of underlying infrastructure management by abstracting caching as a virtual function within the cloud provider. For advanced caching applications, serverless functions can implement fine-grained control over caching behavior, depending on functionality, architecture, and data access needs.

The following figure depicts the flow of a simple serverless cache implementation using a cloud provider.

Figure 10.3: Simple serverless cache flow

Either of the above-advanced techniques, while powerful, do add a level of complexity that needs to be architected early in the design to ensure data consistency and cache coherence. The same principles that apply to setting up a cache infrastructure should be used, and a cost-to-benefit analysis will also ensure that the benefits outweigh the complexities of implementation and infrastructure management.

[Performance testing and monitoring of APIs](contents.xhtml#sc2_288a)

We have discussed the need, advantages, and design considerations for creating a robust, scalable, and performance-oriented API. However, the benefits will not be visible without continuous monitoring and testing.

As part of the API development process, we have discussed key metrics that determine not only the health of an API but also its usefulness to its consumers. This includes response times, throughput, error rates, resource utilization, stability, and scalability under different load conditions. In [Chapter 8](ch08.xhtml), API Testing , we have described various testing methods that apply to APIs to ensure that the API code and the infrastructure can be constantly optimized to fine-tune the API's performance. Understanding the API's performance under testing and real-world situations is critical to achieving a solid API ecosystem.

In [Chapter 2](ch02.xhtml), Beyond REST, we laid out best practices for developing APIs, including pagination, data serialization, and load balancing (described further in this chapter). Creating efficient and scalable APIs combines all these factors and starts right at the requirements analysis phase, culminating in the deployment and monitoring of an API. Hence, performance and scaling should not be isolated after development. It should be part of the API development lifecycle. [Chapter 13](ch13.xhtml), API Management Platforms and Gateways, will look at API gateways that help monitor and automate many scaling and reliability processes for APIs.

[Handling spikes and sudden traffic surge](contents.xhtml#sc3_289a)

When designing and scaling APIs, it is important to remember that API traffic does not necessarily have to be constant throughout the day. There will be times when there is a traffic surge, and a good API and its ecosystem need to be prepared to handle such scenarios gracefully. While in some cases, traffic surges can be planned (viral content, marketing campaigns), there are unexpected events that could drive unexpected spikes. Some methods to effectively handle spikes and sudden traffic surges in the API infrastructure are mentioned as follows:

  * Elastic scaling:
    * Here, an elastic scaling mechanism that automatically adjusts the number of server instances based on incoming traffic is implemented.
    * Cloud platforms like Amazon Web Services (AWS), Azure, or Google Cloud Platform (GCP) provide auto-scaling capabilities that can be incorporated into the API ecosystem.
    * Define, manage, and monitor scaling policies and thresholds, which monitor metrics (CPU utilization, request rate, or response times) to determine scaling needs.
    * Applications should be designed to be stateless and handle horizontal scaling seamlessly.
  * Utilize load balancers and appropriate caching as part of the API ecosystem.
  * Implement rate limiting and throttling mechanisms to protect the APIs from surges and potential abuses.
  * Implement different API architectures, including asynchronous processing.
  * Capacity planning and testing exercises should be performed regularly to estimate traffic and plan for future scalability.
  * Robust and continuous monitoring and alerting should be implemented to respond to spikes promptly. Some parameters that should be monitored are response times, throughput, error rates, and latency.



These measures will better equip the API ecosystem to handle sudden spikes and traffic surges. It is important to continuously monitor, test, and optimize the system to ensure that the APIs can handle any load on the system.

[Observability](contents.xhtml#sc3_290a)

Observability is the ability of the monitoring system to gain insights into the system's different internal states and behaviors based on its external outputs. By tracing each state of the API request and its responses, we can identify issues or bottlenecks at a micro level.

Observability, in addition to regular monitoring and logging, helps in the scaling and performance improvements for APIs in the following ways:

  * Troubleshooting and root cause analysis: Observability tools enable faster troubleshooting and root cause analysis. They can access detailed logs, traces, and metrics set up as part of the observability service. The source of the problems could be a specific API endpoint, downstream service, or an infrastructure issue.
  * Continuous improvement: Observability tools monitor and analyze data across different processes within an API. These tools provide visibility into the internal workings of APIs by collecting and analyzing data from various processes like performance metrics, logs, traces, and user behavior. Hence, it helps us make data-driven decisions on which part of the API needs to be improved to enhance overall performance and scalability.

Example: Travel site using a booking API

    * Observability tools track metrics like request latency, response times, throughput (requests per second), and error rates.
    * If response times exceed 500ms for specific endpoints (e.g., "search flights"), alerts are triggered, prompting the team to investigate.
    * Tools that can be used for this scenario include Prometheus, Grafana, and AWS CloudWatch to track performance metrics for APIs hosted on AWS infrastructure.
  * Capacity planning: Observability data can be analyzed for historical data and trends, forecast future API usage, and plan for any necessary infrastructure and resource requirements to support the expected growth. From our previous travel site example, capacity planning involves analyzing observability data such as historical metrics, usage trends, and traffic patterns to predict future API usage. Analyzing historical data would include metrics such as Requests Per Second (RPS) for each API endpoint, latency for a request, and infrastructure (CPU, memory, disk usage, etc.) utilization. It could highlight that the API served an average of 1000 RPS for flight searches and then peaked at 6000 RPS during Christmas flight dates. Data such as this enables capacity planning around foreseen peak demand periods.
  * Identifying bottlenecks: Observability tools help collect data and can help pinpoint specific components or pieces of functional logic that are causing slowdowns or impacting the overall performance of the API.
  * Service level indicators (SLIs) and objectives (SLOs): SLIs and SLOs help define an organization's tolerance to API specifications and performance parameters. SLIs and SLOs can be determined based on observability data. This helps to align the API's performance with the business objectives and user expectations and helps create Service Level Agreements (SLAs) between the producer and consumer of an API.
  * Dependency analysis: Modern applications rely on microservices and external APIs and depend on their availability and performance expectations. Observability can help understand and monitor these dependencies, as the bottleneck can impact overall performance.
  * Cost management: Scaling to improve performance can rapidly increase costs. By using observability parameters, we can see which parts of the API are resource-hungry, allowing for targeted optimization to reduce costs.
  * Change management: The micro visibility that observability provides into an API's operations helps us understand the impact of updates and upgrades to the API infrastructure. Comparing the before and after observations helps us identify any adverse consequences of the changes.



Implementation of observability can be done using different tools and techniques:

  * API gateways provide built-in monitoring and logging capabilities.
  * Distributed tracing frameworks like OpenTelemetry or Jaeger help trace API requests across multiple services. Distributed tracing includes:
    * Recording each step of the request.
    * Collecting metadata like timestamps, latencies, errors, and resource utilization at each service or process.
    * Visualizing the entire path of the request makes debugging complex issues easier.
  * Logging frameworks and centralized log management solutions.
  * Metrics collection and visualization tools like Prometheus and Grafana.
  * Application Performance Monitoring (APM) products that provide end-to-end visibility into API performance. Examples of such products include Datadog APM, Dynatrace, New Relic APM, and App Dynamics. The following figure is a summary table of APM tools. APM tools are complex, and this table simplifies things. For example, Elastic APM is open-source and integrates with the ELK stack, offering many more features that are presented on each company’s website.



Figure 10.4: Comparison of some monitoring tools

By incorporating observability practices, frameworks, and tools into the API development and operational part of the API lifecycle, we can gain valuable insights and proactively identify and resolve performance issues.

[Conclusion](contents.xhtml#sc2_291a)

API scaling and performance optimization are critical to building and maintaining successful APIs. APIs should inherently be able to handle increasing demands and deliver optimal user experiences. Performance and scalability are complex subjects that encompass the lifecycle of the API development process. At the beginning of the chapter, we laid a foundation for understanding the important considerations in designing an API for performance and scalability.

Load balancing is a complex subject because it is not only part of the development process but also an integral part of how the API is deployed on the underlying infrastructure. Load balancing can be handled by different mechanisms, including API gateways, discussed in [Chapter 13](ch13.xhtml), API Management Platforms and Gateways.

Caching is another important feature in an API's design and infrastructure implementation, and a newer way of using cloud-based serverless caching is discussed in this chapter. It offers the advantage of not dealing with the underlying complexity of implementing our caching infrastructure.

One last takeaway from this chapter should be not to underestimate the business implications of API performance. A poorly performing API can lead to a loss of revenue, competitive advantage, and customer trust. On the other hand, competitive advantages can be gained by delivering high-performance APIs.

In the next chapter, we will discuss API governance, which is a framework that brings together all aspects of API design, development, and monitoring and enables a level of consistency in the entire process and the means to identify, track, and rectify any deviations from standards whether those standards are performance related, or design related.

[Key terms](contents.xhtml#sc2_292a)

  * Throughput: The number of requests an API can handle within a specific time frame, usually measured in requests per second (RPS).
  * Latency: The time it takes for an API to respond to a request, typically measured in milliseconds (ms).
  * Concurrency: The ability of an API to handle multiple requests simultaneously without affecting performance.
  * Load balancing: Distributing incoming API requests across multiple servers to optimize resource utilization and improve performance.
  * Caching: The technique of storing frequently accessed data in a fast-access memory to reduce the number of requests to the backend server and improve response times.
  * Rate limiting: A technique used to control the number of requests a client can make to an API within a specific time frame to prevent abuse and protect server resources.
  * Horizontal scaling: Adding more servers to handle increased API traffic allows the system to scale out.
  * Vertical scaling: The process of increasing the resources (e.g., CPU, RAM) of existing servers to handle increased API traffic, allowing the system to scale up.
  * Auto-scaling: The ability of a system to automatically adjust the number of servers or resources based on the incoming API traffic to maintain optimal performance.
  * Asynchronous processing: A method of handling API requests where the server doesn't wait for a request to complete before accepting new requests, improving overall performance.
  * API gateway: It is a server that acts as a single entry point for APIs, handling tasks like request routing, composition, and protocol translation.
  * Microservices: An architectural approach where an application is broken down into smaller, loosely coupled services that communicate via APIs, allowing for better scalability and maintainability.
  * API throttling: A technique to limit a client's requests to an API within a specific time frame to prevent server overload.
  * Connection pooling: A technique used to maintain a pool of database connections, reducing the overhead of establishing new connections for each API request.
  * API monitoring: The process of tracking and analyzing API performance metrics, such as response times, error rates, and throughput, to identify and resolve performance issues.
