# [CHAPTER 11API Governance](contents.xhtml#ch11a)

[Introduction](contents.xhtml#sc2_293a)

Developing an enterprise-wide API ecosystem requires more than technical knowledge to build APIs. It requires a strategic approach that includes establishing an API governance process that establishes clear guidelines, standards, and best practices that align with the organization’s overall business objectives and technical requirements.

API governance encompasses everything from defining design principles to secure access, documentation of the functionality, and monitoring usage. APIs allow companies to expose their functionality to a broader developer community.

Effective API governance should not burden the developers; it should help organizations ensure consistency and quality across all API development.

API governance is a framework that introduces a set of practices, standards, and processes established in an organization to ensure the proper development, management, and publishing of secure and reliable APIs. It is a crucial aspect of API management to ensure APIs' consistency, reliability, security, and scalability throughout their lifecycle. In a world where APIs are being built at scale, uncontrolled API proliferation can lead to confusion about functionality, duplication, security vulnerabilities, and possibly a poor developer experience. This is where API governance steps in.

Throughout this chapter, we will explore the different dimensions of API governance and provide guidance on building and maintaining a practical API governance framework.

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_294a)

In this chapter, we will discuss the following topics:

  * Standardization and best practices
  * Building a string API governance foundation
  * Operational governance
  * Future trends and innovations in API governance



[Objectives](contents.xhtml#sc2_295a)

The primary objective of API governance is to align the APIs with an organization’s overall business strategy while maintaining a balance between the needs of the API consumers and the requirements of the API producers. It involves establishing clear guidelines and best practices for API design, documentation, version, access control, testing, and monitoring.

Effective API Governance helps achieve API consistency and standardization by ensuring that APIs are designed and developed using a consistent set of standards and guidelines, with an emphasis on security and compliance standards. In addition to detailing these two aspects and establishing some guidelines, we will discuss collaboration, reusability, and innovation. These are clear guidelines and tools that help to foster collaboration within an organization. A critical aspect of the API development cycle is the method of versioning and deprecating APIs. Another element of API governance is to evaluate the documentation and its adherence to the standards. API governance is part of the lifecycle but needs other components of the lifecycle to be successful. This chapter will show how it acts as a guardrail to ensure API development meets enterprise standards.

[Standardization and best practices](contents.xhtml#sc2_296a)

A practical API governance framework will help an organization align its API strategy with the business goals to improve the developer experience and the overall quality and reliability of the API offerings. A well-defined and managed governance framework will streamline and facilitate the API development process. Since API governance is critical to managing and maintaining a robust API ecosystem, it involves establishing standards, guidelines, and best practices to ensure consistency, security, and efficiency during operational APIs' design, implementation, and management.

The API lifecycle comprises several steps, as discussed in Chapter 4, Crafting APIs

Governance should be enforced at every stage with minimal disruption to the development. However, some stages require attention to ensure adherence to governance processes. These stages are described in the next section.

[API design and development governance](contents.xhtml#sc3_297a)

A proper governance team must establish the standards and procedures and ensure its enforcement by monitoring its adherence to those standards.

Some common governance objectives are detailed as follows:

  * The governance process should establish clear guidelines and standards for API design, including naming conventions, resource modeling, versioning, and deprecation.
  * The governance specifications should define an enterprise-wide API specification format, such as OpenAPI specifications.
  * The governance process should ensure adherence to the guideline standards and API contract formats throughout the life cycle of the API design stage.
  * API governance includes standardizing API design tools, frameworks, and libraries to maintain consistency. The API governance process needs to monitor metrics to ensure adherence to these standards.



[Documentation and discoverability](contents.xhtml#sc3_298a)

One of the most critical aspects that determine the mass adoption of reusable APIs in an organization is the ability of developers to discover these APIs quickly and be provided clear and concise documentation for incorporating them into their applications. Some important governance aspects include:

  * The governance process mandates creating (and formatting) comprehensive and up-to-date documentation for APIs. This can include API reference guides, tutorials, and code samples.
  * Establish the enterprise centralized API catalog (possibly through a developer portal) to provide API discovery and be a single point of reference for developers.
  * Use documentation tools and platforms that automate the generation of API documentation and review the API documentation to ensure accuracy and relevance.
  * Assign clear ownership of each API entry within the catalog for support and adherence to governance standards. This should indicate the status of APIs (active, deprecated, retired) within the catalog.



[Security protocols and compliance](contents.xhtml#sc3_299a)

Security is a crucial component that must be standardized in the API governance process. Some key aspects that API governance on security and compliance need to establish and enforce include:

  * Ensure the use of robust security protocols for API authentication, authorization, and data protection.
  * Enforce industry-standard security measures, including OAuth 2.0, JSON Web Tokens (JWT), and SSL/TLS encryption.
  * Organizations operate under different compliance regulations and standards, such as GDPR, HIPAA, and PCI-DSS. APIs need to be monitored for their adherence to these standards.



[Lifecycle management and deprecation](contents.xhtml#sc3_300a)

API governance begins with a concept and follows the API right up to retirement. Embedding API governance within the API lifecycle reduces the burden on developers to spend time specifically on governance activities. Some key activities to establish this as part of the lifecycle management include:

  * Defining a straightforward API lifecycle management process and communicating this and any deprecation schedules per a clearly defined governance process.
  * Establishing a feedback loop with API consumers to gather insights and make informed decisions about API lifecycle management.



[Monitoring and analytics](contents.xhtml#sc3_301a)

This is the heart of establishing a practical and data-driven governance approach. Some key definitions and processes that need to be established include:

  * Implementing a comprehensive monitoring and analytics solution to track API performance, usage, and health.
  * Establishing KPIs and SLAs to measure API success.
  * Utilize API analytics data as part of the governance process to gain and provide insights to teams on usage patterns to identify bottlenecks and any variations from established standards.



These key areas can help establish a robust API governance framework that promotes consistency. These areas can be crystallized into some best practices. The following are some of the best practices that help create practical API governance in an organization:

  * Define API governance policies:
    * Establish clear policies and guidelines for API design, development, documentation, and usage.
    * Define standards for API versioning, naming conventions, error handling, and security protocols.
    * Communicate these policies to all stakeholders involved in the API lifecycle.
  * Create an API governance team:
    * Assemble a dedicated team responsible for overseeing and enforcing API governance policies.
    * Include representatives from various departments, such as IT, security, legal, and business units.
    * Assign roles and responsibilities to team members to ensure effective governance.
  * Implement API design and development standards:
    * Enforce consistent API design principles, such as RESTful architecture and industry best practices.
    * Provide guidelines for API documentation, including format, content, and maintenance.
    * Establish coding standards and guidelines for API development to ensure code quality and maintainability.
  * Establish API security measures:
    * Implement robust security mechanisms, such as authentication, authorization, and encryption.
    * Define security policies for API access control, data protection, and threat prevention.
    * Regularly review and update security measures to address emerging threats and vulnerabilities.
  * Monitor and audit API usage:
    * Implement monitoring and logging mechanisms to track API usage, errors, and performance.
    * Regularly audit API usage to ensure compliance with governance policies and detect any anomalies or misuse.
    * Use analytics and reporting tools to gain insights into API usage patterns and identify areas for improvement.
  * Manage API lifecycle:
    * Define a clear process for API lifecycle management, including versioning, deprecation, and retirement.
    * Communicate API changes and updates to consumers and provide adequate notice for breaking changes.
    * Maintain a centralized API catalog or developer portal to provide easy access to API documentation and resources.
  * Foster collaboration and communication:
    * Encourage collaboration and knowledge sharing among API developers, consumers, and stakeholders.
    * Establish communication channels like forums or mailing lists to facilitate discussions and feedback.
    * Provide training and support to ensure that all stakeholders understand and adhere to API governance policies.
  * Regularly review and update governance policies:
    * Continuously review and update API governance policies to adapt to changing requirements, technology advancements, and industry best practices.
    * Maintain up-to-date and comprehensive API documentation that includes details on endpoints, requests and response formats, authentication methods, and examples of usage.
    * Gather feedback from API consumers and stakeholders to identify areas for improvement.



[Building a strong API governance foundation](contents.xhtml#sc2_302a)

For any API governance initiative to succeed, it requires a solid and practical foundation built on collaboration and clear communications. A sound governance framework needs a few components to support the implementation and management of architecture in an organization.

The following are the components:

  * Key stakeholders in API governance: Identify the key individuals, teams, and departments with a vested interest in the success of the API governance framework, such as IT, business units, security, and compliance.
  * Roles and responsibilities of stakeholders: Clearly define the roles and responsibilities of each stakeholder group, outlining their specific tasks, decision-making authority, and accountability in the API governance process.
  * Define and automate processes: Establish well-defined processes for API design, development, testing, deployment, versioning, and retirement, ensuring consistency and adherence to best practices across the organization.
  * Establish documentation standards and guidelines: Create and maintain comprehensive documentation that outlines the standards, guidelines, and best practices for API design, security, performance, and other critical aspects of API development and management.
  * Monitoring and metrics: Implement monitoring systems and define relevant metrics to track API performance, usage, and compliance with governance policies, enabling continuous improvement and informed decision-making.
  * Tools: Identify and utilize appropriate tools to support API governance, such as API management platforms, security tools, testing frameworks, and documentation generators, streamlining the implementation and enforcement of governance policies.



[Key stakeholders and responsibilities in API governance](contents.xhtml#sc3_303a)

Every organization is in a different stage of maturity and development cycle. Hence, while we explain some key constituents of the API governance framework, it needs to be adapted to each organization’s needs and requirements, keeping in mind that governance should not be a burden or a tax that reduces the effectiveness of development.

Some of the key stakeholders are listed as followss:

  * Product teams: These are developers, product owners/managers, designers, and testers actively involved in building, maintaining, and evolving APIs. They are responsible for making sure that APIs meet business needs and meet governance standards.
  * API governance team: Different enterprises have different names. Examples include the API governance committee, Center of Excellence (COE for governance), Architecture Review Board (ARB). This cross-functional group oversees the API strategy, documentation standards, and governance framework. It usually includes representatives from infrastructure, security, business units, and, in many cases, legal departments. This critical group defines the policies, reviews API designs, and ensures consistency across the ecosystem.
  * API platform team: This team is usually responsible for managing the API infrastructure (cloud, data centers) and the tools that support the API lifecycle. This includes API gateways, developer portals, monitoring, logging, and analytics platforms.
  * Security teams: Security teams, as part of the governing body, define access control mechanisms, conduct security assessments, and measure adherence to these standards by the APIs.
  * Business leaders: They set the strategic direction for API initiatives. This ensures alignment across the organization with the business goals and drives adoption.
  * Legal teams: APIs encompass a broad spectrum of internal and external users. These teams establish and review service terms, evaluate data privacy considerations, and broadly ensure compliance with relevant industry regulations.



Establishing the right team structure and defining their roles and responsibilities is one of the most important aspects of establishing a robust API governance framework.

[Technology and tooling for API governance](contents.xhtml#sc3_304a)

Organizations must leverage the right technologies and tooling to implement API governance effectively. There is no one tool for API governance; instead, it uses different tools for the various stages in the API lifecycle that need to come together to create a comprehensive API governance dashboard. The following are some key technologies and tools that support API governance:

  * API management platforms: These provide a centralized hub for designing, publishing, documenting, and monitoring APIs. These platforms offer features such as versioning, access control, rate limiting, and analytics. These platforms all facilitate the establishment of API governance practices by helping enforce governance policies, ensure API consistency, and provide API visibility into API usage and performance. We will discuss API platforms in Chapter 12, Real-time, Asynchronous, and Streaming APIs.
  * API design and documentation tools: Tools like OpenAPI and RAML provide standardized formats for describing API specifications. These tools enforce API governance by ensuring consistent API design and documentation and the means to track them for non-compliance.
  * API testing and validation tools: An essential aspect of API governance is the testing and monitoring of APIs to ensure their quality and reliability. Tools like Postman SoapUI allow consistent creation and execution of API tests to validate the responses.
  * API monitoring and analytics: Monitoring and analyzing API performance and usage is essential for effective API governance. Tools like Splunk, Elasticsearch, and Kibana provide real-time monitoring, logging, and analytics capabilities. These tools help track API metrics, identify performance bottlenecks, and gain insights into usage patterns.
  * Governance and compliance tools: These tools provide policy management, access control, audit trails, and compliance reporting. They ensure the APIs are developed, published, and consumed following defined standards and regulations. Tools include Axway API gateway and WSO2 API manager. Most API gateways offer some variation of governance-specific tools.



Organizations can establish an automated and robust API governance framework by leveraging these technologies and frameworks. Dashboards can be either part of these tools or custom-built within an API gateway in many cases. API governance is very organization-specific, and the organization's needs should be balanced with the additional burden of having a governance layer as part of the API development lifecycle.

[Measuring API governance success](contents.xhtml#sc3_305a)

Establishing an API governance framework and process needs a method to measure the success of this body. This is usually done by measuring key metrics and monitoring them across different development groups to form a long-term enterprise-wise metrics standard.

Some metrics and KPIs that are tracked to measure governance across the enterprise are mentioned as follows:

  * Identify key metrics and KPIs aligned with business objectives
  * Establish baseline measurements for current API performance
  * Set realistic and achievable targets for improvement
  * Examples of metrics and KPIs:
    * Number of APIs published and consumed
    * API adoption rate and growth
    * API response time and availability
    * Number of API-related incidents and resolution time



We monitor API adoption and usage in the following ways:

  * Implement tools and processes to track API usage and adoption
  * Analyze API traffic patterns and trends
  * Monitor API consumption by internal and external developers
  * Identify popular and underutilized APIs
  * Gather feedback from API consumers to understand their needs and experiences



The following ways is how we assess API quality and performance:

  * Regularly review API documentation and ensure it is up-to-date and comprehensive
  * Conduct API testing to identify and address performance issues
  * Monitor API error rates and response times
  * Perform security assessments to identify and mitigate vulnerabilities
  * Gather user feedback on API usability and developer experience



We continuously improve API governance processes in the following ways:

  * Regularly review and update API governance policies and procedures
  * Incorporate input from stakeholders to refine governance processes
  * Conduct post-mortem analysis of API-related incidents to identify areas for improvement
  * Foster a culture of continuous improvement and learning within the organization



Reporting and communication for API governance is achieved in the following ways:

  * Generate regular reports on API governance metrics and KPIs
  * Communicate the value and impact of API governance to stakeholders
  * Share success stories and lessons learned with the broader organization
  * Collaborate with cross-functional teams to address challenges and opportunities
  * Celebrate achievements and recognize individuals and teams contributing to API governance success



Governance maturity assessment is achieved in the following ways:

  * Conduct periodic assessments of the organization's API governance maturity
  * Identify strengths, weaknesses, and areas for improvement
  * Benchmark against industry standards and best practices
  * Develop a roadmap for advancing API governance maturity over time
  * Regularly review and update the maturity assessment to track progress



While quantitative data, as defined above, is valuable, other qualitative aspects help determine the effectiveness of API governance. Some of these include:

  * Developer community growth: A thriving and engaged developer community indicates a solid and active API ecosystem.
  * Innovation driven by APIs: The emergence of innovative applications around the APIs is a key indicator of value creation in an organization.
  * Industry recognition: Industry recognition and positive media coverage attract new developers and enhances the API program's reputation.



API governance is an activity that should be measured over time as governance takes time to be established, followed, and then enhanced. Regularly evaluating the impact of the API governance efforts over time helps refine the governance efforts. Some of the practical ways to do this are:

  * Compare KPIs over time: Some KPIs are long-term trend indicators and should be compared over time. These include KPIs like developer adoption or API response times to assess the effectiveness of implemented initiatives.
  * Cost-benefit analysis: API governance programs have a cost associated with them. Evaluate an API program's return on investment (ROI) by considering costs related to the development and management of the program vs value generated by API-driven innovation and the reduced development cost.
  * Feedback analysis: Gather and analyze feedback from developers and internal stakeholders to identify areas for improvement within the API governance framework.



Regular monitoring, assessment, and reporting will help the API governance efforts be practical and aligned with business objectives.

[Operational governance](contents.xhtml#sc2_306a)

The development of an API is only the first step in their lifecycle. It involves designing and writing code for the API. Subsequent steps include deployment and the runtime component of the API. Governance during these phases provides an additional level of information that is necessary for a complete API lifecycle governance. In this section, we define an application's runtime and deployment aspects and then discuss how operational governance fits into the picture.

[Deployment](contents.xhtml#sc3_307a)

Deployment is the process of installing and releasing applications or updates into a production environment. It includes packaging application code and its dependencies and configurations and making it available for users or other systems. This complex task needs proper processes and auditable data to ensure effective governance.

API governance for deployments includes setting up deployment strategies, providing guidelines on infrastructure provisioning, automating any tools for DevOps, and testing and validating deployed applications.

[Runtime](contents.xhtml#sc3_308a)

Runtime is when a deployed application is actively running and serving users. During this period, the application executes its code, interacts with other systems or services, and responds to system events.

Governance during runtime provides many metrics to measure an API's effectiveness and adherence to the standards and KPIs (governance). Governance during runtime includes monitoring performance and gathering data, validating the scalability and elasticity of applications, identifying resiliency challenges, and measuring security incidents as they occur. Since the applications are designed with the API governance framework, measuring some parameters helps determine and improve any shortcomings in the API governance process.

Operational governance guides the management and control of deployed applications and their associated infrastructure. It includes the definition and enforcement of processes, policies, and practices. Operational governance typically involves defining the broad set of deployment and runtime aspects.

The key aspects of operation governance include:

  * Configuration management: Defines, establishes, and maintains a consistent and controlled environment by managing application configurations, dependencies, and infrastructure settings.
  * Change management: Implements a structured process for managing and approving application changes, configuration, or underlying infrastructure.
  * Incident management: Establish procedures for detecting, responding to, and resolving incidents that may arise during application runtime, including incident classification, escalation, and post-mortem analysis.
  * Compliance and auditing: Ensures that the application and its operations adhere to the relevant industry standards, regulations, and any internal policies that have been formulated. Additionally, this establishes a process to conduct regular audits to validate compliance.



By enforcing operation governance, organizations can measure their application's effectiveness during runtime and gain insights into the applications.

[Future trends and innovations in API governance](contents.xhtml#sc2_309a)

API governance is being shaped by different trends that determine API lifecycle management. The proliferation of APIs needs a sharper focus on security. API governance should incorporate advancements in API threat detection tools.

The rise of microservices and serverless architectures marks a shift in how APIs are deployed, and API governance must be kept track of this. Service mesh technologies such as Istio or Linkerd are used for API management, security, and observability. These should be incorporated as part of the API governance process.

APIs are increasingly becoming a core business asset for an organization. Hence, as different models and API marketplaces become common, they include subscription models or usage-based pricing API management and governance platforms to support these monetization tools.

AI-driven API management and governance is leveraging AI and ML for automated API discovery, classification, and documentation. In addition, AI is increasingly used to implement intelligent API security measures, including anomaly detection and real-time threat analysis using deep learning. AI is also used to gain advanced analytical insights into API usage, performance, user behavior, and automated downtime remediation.

[Conclusion](contents.xhtml#sc2_310a)

Effective API Governance is a strategic imperative in today's digital age. API governance is a framework that outlines the policies, processes, and best practices for managing the lifecycle of APIs. It ensures that APIs are designed, developed, and maintained consistently, securely, and efficiently. API governance practices are set up to meet the organization's overall business objectives. API governance helps standardize the creation of APIs, and we discussed some best practices for creating an effective API governance organization in an enterprise. Some key aspects promoting API governance include API design and development governance, documentation standards, and discoverability. In addition, operational governance, which includes deployment and runtime considerations, is discussed. We round off this chapter with a discussion of different tools and platforms that help in the API governance process and a brief discussion of future trends in API governance.

In the next chapter, we will discuss other types of APIs, including real-time APIs and asynchronous and streaming APIs. We will explain the need for real-time APIs and its implications while designing a modern API-based platform.

[Key terms](contents.xhtml#sc2_311a)

  * API governance: The policies, processes, and guidelines that ensure APIs are designed, developed, and managed consistently, securely, and aligned with the enterprise goals.
  * Operational governance: The framework of policies, processes, and controls that ensure the efficiency and compliance of day-to-day business and technology operations.
  * API lifecycle management: The process of managing an API from its design and development through deployment, maintenance, and eventual retirement.
  * API versioning: The practice of assigning version numbers to API releases to allow for backward compatibility, enabling developers to make changes and improvements without breaking existing integrations.
  * API contract: A formal agreement between the API provider and consumer that defines the API's functionality, performance, and usage terms.
  * API monetization: The process of generating revenue from an API through various business models, such as subscriptions, pay-per-use, or revenue sharing.
  * API portal: A web-based interface that provides documentation, tools, and resources for developers to discover, learn about, and consume APIs.
  * Architecture review board: A cross-functional team responsible for defining, implementing, and enforcing API governance policies and best practices within an organization.

# [CHAPTER 12Real-time, Asynchronous, and Streaming APIs](contents.xhtml#ch12a)

[Introduction](contents.xhtml#sc2_312a)

In the modern digital economy, users demand instant, real-time, updated data as it is produced. This chapter aims to discuss a few of these technologies and patterns prioritizing real-time data using a variety of architectures. REST APIs are popular, but a disadvantage is that they are sluggish when it comes to real-time data exchange. By walking through some of the options available for real-time APIs, the architect will better appreciate when and how to choose some of the other options to produce instantaneous data.

Asynchronous and streaming APIs are required to build responsive and efficient applications that can handle high volumes of data with minimal latency and allow operations to proceed without waiting for the response, thus facilitating a non-blocking interaction model.

Later in the chapter, we explore the role of message brokers and queues, such as Kafka and RabbitMQ, in facilitating real-time data exchange and are critical in managing data flow across distributed systems, ensuring scalability and reliability in processing and delivering messages.

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_313a)

In this chapter, we will cover the following topics:

  * Defining real-time APIs
  * Understanding asynchronous and streaming APIs
  * Different methods for real-time APIs
  * Real-time API design principles and guidelines
  * Real-time API design patterns
  * Message brokers and queues
  * Event-driven architectures
  * Harnessing the power of real-time data
  * Additional enabling technologies
  * Future of real-time data APIs



[Objectives](contents.xhtml#sc2_314a)

We will begin by defining real-time APIs and why they are crucial in today's interconnected world. This chapter will examine real-time APIs and their characteristics and advantages compared to conventional REST APIs.

We will explore asynchronous and streaming APIS and understand their underlying mechanisms. A deep dive into the WebSocket protocol and Server-Sent Events (SSE) follows, providing insights into the mechanisms that power real-time communication over the web. WebSocket offers a full-duplex communication channel over a single, long-lived connection, making it ideal for interactive applications. SSE, while simpler, enables servers to push updates to clients, perfect for scenarios where data flows in one direction.

We will continue exploring event-driven architectures for building responsive and reactive applications. By the end of this chapter, readers will be equipped with an understanding of real-time, asynchronous, and streaming APIs. They will be well-versed in the technologies and patterns that enable instant data exchange, empowering them to harness the power of real-time data for their applications.

[Defining real-time APIs](contents.xhtml#sc2_315a)

Real-time APIs aim to deliver data with minimal latency. The goal is to provide data as soon as it is generated or requested. This differs from traditional REST APIs, where clients make requests and wait for responses, leading to possible delays.

An example of the need for a real-time API is watching a live sports event that refreshes the score periodically rather than as it happens. REST APIs would not be acceptable for instantaneous score updates. Hence, real-time APIs are used where data updates are often measured in milliseconds.

There are a few characteristics of real-time APIs that differentiate them from other styles of APIs:

  * Data has to be delivered in real-time or near real-time, typically within milliseconds or less. This means there has to be extremely low latency in the response times of the APIs.
  * There has to be low latency full duplex communications (bi-directional communications). This means clients and servers can exchange data simultaneously, unlike the request-response style of REST.
  * Another critical requirement is persistent connections between the clients and servers. This helps enable continuous data flow without exchanging handshakes every time.
  * Real-time APIs must be inherently scalable because they are required to efficiently handle dynamic workloads, which often include unpredictable spikes in demand and large number of concurrent connections. These APIs enable instant data exchange and communications in modern applications.



The publishing and consuming of real-time APIs has some benefits for applications, such as:

  * In today's digital world, real-time updates create a more responsive and interactive user experience.
  * Since data is delivered in real-time, data accuracy is higher as the data reflects the latest state of the system.
  * An additional advantage for systems that require real-time is the ability to react instantaneously to changing conditions and then optimize the responses. This increases operational efficiency. An example would be reacting to sensors in an IoT environment.



The use cases for real-time APIs include:

  * Messaging applications: Chat, social media, and collaboration platforms rely heavily on real-time communication.
  * IoT/sensor data streaming: Real-time data from IoT devices requires low-latency delivery for analysis and control.
  * Financial trading platforms: Real-time market updates are crucial for informed trading decisions.
  * Live gaming applications: Real-time synchronization and updates are essential for seamless gaming experiences.
  * Live chat applications: Messages appear instantly, fostering seamless communication.
  * Social media feeds: Updates appear as they happen, keeping users engaged in real-time conversations.



Real-time APIs usually leverage two key styles for data exchange: asynchronous APIs and streaming APIs. While their main objective is to reduce latency and produce data with minimal delay, they approach it with different methodologies.

[Understanding asynchronous and streaming APIs](contents.xhtml#sc2_316a)

In today’s digital world, applications need different efficient communication methods to deliver a smooth user experience in real-time. Asynchronous and streaming APIs are two methods designed for non-blocking real-time communications between a client and a server are required. Understanding these APIs is essential for building modern, high-performance applications that can scale effectively. Below, we discuss each of these in greater detail.

[Asynchronous APIs](contents.xhtml#sc3_317a)

Asynchronous APIs involve decoupled requests and responses (often with callbacks) that allow the client to continue processing other work.

A good way to understand asynchronous APIs is when we order food online. The customer (client) makes a request, gets an acknowledgment of the request, and then continues to do other work. The restaurant (server) works in the background and then fulfills the request, notifying the customer (client) only when it is completed.

The asynchronous APIs have a few characteristics, such as:

  * Requests and responses are decoupled. This means that clients, after getting an ack of the request, do not necessarily wait for an immediate response when they send requests to a server before performing other activities. Usually, they continue their logic flow and perform other tasks. The server handles requests based on certain logic (priority, data availability, service tiers, etc.) and eventually sends the response.
  * Since we are not waiting for server responses before sending a new request, it is also called non-blocking communication.
  * Since the client is not blocked while waiting for a request, applications have improved responsiveness and feel faster and more interactive.
  * The inherent multi-tasking nature of this style makes for efficient resource utilization, as servers can handle multiple requests concurrently with the right infrastructure design.



Asynchronous APIs can use different protocols and methods. Popular protocols include WebSockets, SSE, and message queues.

Take a look at the following diagram to depict the sequence of operations in a synchronous vs async call:

Figure 12.1: Synchronous API call

The following diagram depicts an asynchronous call:

Figure 12.2: Asynchronous API call

[Callbacks in asynchronous APIs](contents.xhtml#sc4_318a)

One method of utilizing asynchronous APIs is the method of callbacks. They allow applications to perform tasks without blocking the flow within that application. We will discuss what they are, how they function, and their advantages and best practices.

A callback is a function or URL passed to an API invoked when some process or action is completed. Once the operation is complete, the callback function is called with the result, allowing the application to proceed with that data.

A depiction of a callback function is shown in the figure:

Figure 12.3: Using callbacks

The following is a simple explanation of how callbacks work in asynchronous APIs:

  * An asynchronous API call is made by a consumer application to a producer, and a callback function/URL is provided as an argument.
  * The consumer application continues to execute its other code without waiting for the results.
  * Once the producer finishes the operation, the callback function is called with the results. The result could be the data, messages for error, etc.



There are some advantages to using callbacks, such as:

  * Since callbacks are non-blocking, it improves responsiveness as the calling application processes its workload.
  * There is a degree of simplicity, flexibility, and adaptability in the development cycle as this is a straightforward way to segment the piece of code dealing with the asynchronous results. It allows for better exception handling.



However, we should be careful while using callbacks for the following reasons:

  * When multiple asynchronous tasks depend on each other and are written using nested callbacks, the code becomes harder to understand and debug. Let us look at an example of an app wanting to fetch user data, then fetch their posts, and then fetch comments on these posts. Using nested callbacks, it would look like this (in JavaScript):

fetchUser(userId, (err, user) => {

if (err) return console.error("Error fetching user:", err);

fetchPosts(user.id, (err, posts) => {

if (err) return console.error("Error fetching posts:", err);

fetchComments(posts[0].id, (err, comments) => {

if (err) return console.error("Error fetching comments:", err);

console.log("Comments:", comments);

});

});

});

This is hard to follow, and error handling can be quite complex since error handling is scattered at each level of the callback. If we forget to handle an error in one callback, it might lead to unhandled exceptions or bugs.

  * However, some of the above challenges can be alleviated by modularizing callback functions into smaller and reusable functions. This improves the readability of the code.
  * In addition, a conscious choice should be made to avoid deep nesting. This can be done using control flow libraries async/await syntax (where supported).
  * To improve maintainability, documentation standards should be clear, for example, using clear and descriptive names for callback functions.



[Characteristics of streaming APIs](contents.xhtml#sc3_319a)

Streaming APIs expand the concept of real-time data exchange by enabling a continuous data flow between the client and server. Streaming APIs keep the connections open between the client and server constantly. This allows data to be sent in real-time as soon as it is available. This is particularly useful for live video streaming and real-time analytics.

The key characteristics for streaming APIs include:

  * Data is delivered continuously as a stream of elements, primarily in real-time. Clients can process each element as it arrives without waiting for all the data to be available.
  * By maintaining an open connection for the duration of the session, there is no overhead of repeatedly opening and closing connections for each piece of data.
  * WebSockets are one of the most commonly used protocols for implementing streaming APIs, especially when real-time, bidirectional communications are required; however, specialized streaming protocols like gRPC and Kafka are also used.
  * One significant advantage that streaming APIs offer is the ability to adapt to varying data rates. This allows for bursts of data or, on the flip side, periods of inactivity without compromising performance.



They are well-defined use cases for streaming APIs. One good example is live video streams. In addition, financial market updates and continuous sensor data are also good examples of using streaming APIs.

A depiction of a streaming API is shown as follows:

Figure 12.4: Streaming API call

Here is a table depicting key differences:

Feature |  Asynchronous |  Streaming  
---|---|---  
Data delivery |  Discrete responses after requests |  A continuous flow of elements  
Latency |  Reduced compared to REST, but not real-time guaranteed |  Real-time delivery possible  
Use cases |  Background tasks |  Live feeds, continuous updates  
  
Table 12.1: Key differences between asynchronous and streaming APIs

The preceding table is not comprehensive but more of a guideline to understand the differences. This will help you choose the appropriate API type based on your needs and application requirements.

[Different methods for real-time APIs](contents.xhtml#sc2_320a)

Many different protocols support real-time communications. A brief description of the common ones is mentioned in the following sections.

[WebSocket protocol](contents.xhtml#sc3_321a)

One commonly used protocol is Websockets. This protocol is a fundamental technology for both asynchronous and streaming APIs.

We have described this in [Chapter 4](ch04.xhtml), Crafting APIs, so we will not spend too much time on that here.

The following is the summary of the WebSocket protocol:

  * It enables persistent two-way full duplex connections between servers and clients. This does not incur the HTTP overheads of a request and response protocol.
  * It supports low-latency data exchange.
  * It offers the ability to send data as text or binary.
  * However, it requires more complete implementations and additional server-side infrastructure compared to REST APIs.
  * The WebSocket handshake is relatively simple (a diagrammatic example was given in [Chapter 4](ch04.xhtml), Crafting APIs).
    * The client initiates connection using a special HTTP request for a WebSocket upgrade
    * The server verifies and accepts the upgrade from HTTP to WebSocket
    * Both client and server at this time can send and receive messages in a bi-directional manner



[Server-Sent Events](contents.xhtml#sc3_322a)

There are times when data needs to move from server to client only. SSE offers a simpler alternative to WebSockets by providing a streamlined method for servers to send real-time client updates. SSE, primarily, pushes data updates from the server to clients over a single long-lived HTTP connection. This is good for applications like newsfeeds and social media updates where the complexity of implementing handshakes for full duplex communications is not required.

Simply put, SSE enables a web application to receive automatic updates from a server using an HTTP connection.

SSE has some key features and advantages that are important to the architect. The features are as follows:

  * SSE is easy to implement on the client and server side. This is because it works over standard HTTP without requiring an upgrade like WebSocket.
  * An open connection allows SSE servers to push updates with minimal latency.
  * It has a robust reconnection feature; SSE automatically tries to reconnect if the connection is lost. This simplifies error handling and improves the connection reliability.
  * Furthermore, SSE messages are text-based, making it easy for developers to create, debug, and manage these messages.



SSE is well suited for some applications, such as:

  * Live news feeds require automatic updates with the latest news without manual refreshes for clients.
  * Sports score updates for delivering real-time scores and status to fans.
  * Financial tickers for streaming stock or other financial data to clients on changes.
  * Dashboard updates are used to display metrics or analytics as they change.



While SSE simplifies the development life cycle, it poses a few challenges that must be evaluated before use. Some are listed as follows:

  * SSE is primarily unidirectional. It only supports server-to-client data flow. Applications requiring some kind of response will need to evaluate other options, including WebSockets.
  * Most modern browsers support SSE, but there are exceptions and limitations, especially on mobile browsers. These need to be factored in before deciding to use SSE.
  * An important consideration is that browsers typically limit concurrent connections to a domain. This could be a constraint as the number of simultaneous SSE connections could be reduced.



The following figure represents how an SSE client-server interaction happens:

Figure 12.5: SSE client and server example interaction

The following explains the SSE mechanics and the preceding figure:

  * Client opens connection: To initiate an SSE connection, the client sends a standard HTTP request to the server with the Accept: text/event-stream header content type.
  * Server sends initial response: The server responds by opening the connection, keeping it open, and transmitting data in a specific format whenever updates are available. The above diagram depicts the data format as JSON.
  * Server sends data messages: Each message sent to the client is preceded by a data: line, and multiple messages can be batched together.
  * Client processes events: The client receives the incoming data based on the event type and the processing logic.



[SSE summary](contents.xhtml#sc4_323a)

SSE provides a powerful but straightforward mechanism for implementing real-time server-to-server communications. By leveraging SSE, developers can efficiently push updates to clients, enhancing the user experience with less overhead. However, SSE cannot replace WebSockets for all use cases. A careful analysis of the use case and its tradeoffs will help determine which protocol would be more appropriate.

The choice between WebSockets and SSE is easy to understand. If you require two-way, low-latency communications, WebSockets have the advantage. However, if a more straightforward unidirectional update is appropriate for your application, SSE offers a more lightweight solution.

[Message queuing telemetry transport](contents.xhtml#sc3_324a)

This lightweight messaging protocol is primarily used for IoT devices, mobile devices, and getting messages from small sensors. Unlike the other protocols we have discussed till now, Message Queuing Telemetry Transport (MQTT) is unique and fulfills a need for a highly suitable protocol for remote communications where network bandwidth and power resources are limited. MQTT operates over the Transmission Control Protocol/Internet Protocol (TCP/IP) protocol and is known for its simplicity. It enables the efficient distribution of information to multiple receivers (consumers) using minimal network traffic.

The following are the key features:

  * Lightweight protocol: MQTT is designed to be compact and has a small code base, making it ideal for embedded devices with limited resources.
  * Pub/sub model: Unlike the other protocols and the request-response model, MQTT uses the pub-sub model. As discussed, this model decouples message publishers from consumers. This makes the message distribution scalable and efficient.
  * Multiple Quality of Service (QoS) levels: Depending on resources and needs, Embedded devices sometimes need different QoS levels for message delivery. MQTT supports the following three levels:
    * QoS 0 (At most once): The message is delivered at most one time. There is no confirmation that the message was delivered or received.
    * QoS 1 (At least once): The message is delivered at least once to the receiver.
    * QoS 2 (Exactly once): This guarantees that the intended receiver receives each message only once.
  * Retained messages: MQTT has a unique property that can be set to allow it to retain the last message sent on a topic. This feature lets new subscribers immediately receive the most current information once subscribed.
  * Last Will and Testament (LWT): MQTT clients can specify a last-will message. This is published if the client unexpectedly disconnects. This is published on a specified topic.



MQTT has several very unique use cases, such as:

  * IoT devices: IoT devices have limited resources. MQTT offers a method of sending data to an IoT gateway in a compact manner, facilitating efficient communications.
  * Telemetry: MQTT is widely used for telemetry applications like sensor networks. The application collects data from various sensors and sends it to an IoT gateway for further storage and analysis.
  * Smart home: In smart homes with different types of sensors, MQTT monitors and controls devices offering real-time updates and controls.
  * Efficient messaging and notifications: MQTT's message distribution model makes it very useful where compact messaging notifications are needed across different platforms.



The main component in the MQTT protocol is the MQTT broker. It is responsible for managing the network connections, getting the messages, and distributing them to the clients.

Clients connect to the broker, subscribe to topics, or publish messages to topics. The broker ensures the messages are delivered based on the specified QoS levels.

The MQTT broker manages message filtering by distributing messages only to clients who have explicitly subscribed to the relevant topics.

The following figure shows an example MQTT publish/subscribe architecture:

Figure 12.6: MQTT publish/subscribe architecture   
Source: [https://mqtt.org](https://mqtt.org)

[Security considerations](contents.xhtml#sc4_325a)

Even though MQTT is a lightweight protocol, it incorporates certain features to ensure secure communications, such as:

  * Transport Layer Security (TLS): MQTT can use TLS to encrypt data transmitted over the network.
  * Authentication: MQTT supports simple username/password authentication methods. If more robust authentication methods are needed, they can be implemented on top of MQTT. This ensures that only authorized users or devices can publish or subscribe to topics.
  * Authorization: The broker can be used to control access to different topics. This ensures clients can only interact with the topics for which they have been authorized.



MQTT's lightweight, compact implementation with an efficient pub/sub communication model makes it ideal for a variety of devices in IoT and mobile devices. As the IoT ecosystem expands, MQTT will continue to be used extensively.

A non-exhaustive and simple comparison of different streaming protocols is presented as follows:

Figure 12.7: Streaming protocol characteristics

[Real-time API design principles and guidelines](contents.xhtml#sc2_326a)

The following are a few fundamental design principles and guidelines that are suggested while evaluating what patterns help to create real-time APIs that are efficient and perform well:

  * Asynchronous communication:
    * Event-driven architecture: Design your system around events and reactions, enabling efficient response to data changes.
    * Resource optimization: Avoid blocking calls and leverage non-blocking I/O to handle multiple requests concurrently.
    * Background processing: Offload long-running tasks to dedicated workers, enhancing responsiveness.
  * Streaming for continuous data:
    * Chunking: Break down large data payloads into smaller chunks for efficient transmission and processing.
    * Backpressure: Implement mechanisms to regulate data flow based on consumer capacity, preventing overload.
    * Fan-out: Distribute data streams to multiple consumers for parallel processing and scalability.
  * Leverage robust communication channels:
    * WebSockets over SSE: Choose WebSockets for true two-way communication and low latency while considering SSE for simple unidirectional updates.
    * Authentication and authorization: Secure your API endpoints with mechanisms like tokens or OAuth.
    * Error handling and retries: Implement strategies to handle errors and ensure reliable message delivery gracefully.
  * Optimize data delivery:
    * Serialization formats: Choose efficient formats like JSON for text-based data and Protocol Buffers for complex structured data.
    * Data compression: Reduce data size to minimize bandwidth usage and improve transmission speed.
    * Caching: Store frequently accessed data locally on clients or servers for faster retrieval.
  * Scalability:
    * Horizontal scaling: Distribute loads across multiple servers to handle increasing load.
    * Vertical scaling: Upgrade server resources like CPU and memory for improved performance.
    * Monitoring and observability: Track key metrics and proactively identify bottlenecks to address performance issues.



Design patterns help the architect in many ways. It reduces development time since the architect leverages best practices instead of reinventing the wheel. Reusable code, a structured and maintainable code architect, also helps improve code quality and maintenance. In addition, design patterns have best practices built for handling high volumes and demanding workloads. This satisfies a key requirement for real-time APIs, performance, and scalability.

[Real-time API design patterns](contents.xhtml#sc2_327a)

Designing APIs for real-time communications involves various factors, including scalability, efficiency, concurrent connection handling, durability, reliability, etc. This section explores key design patterns that should be carefully considered before building effective real-time APIs. These patterns help address common challenges and ensure that the APIs are robust, responsive, and capable of providing a reliable real-time data exchange platform.

We shall look at some common patterns in the following sections.

[Publish-subscribe](contents.xhtml#sc3_328a)

The publish/subscribe (pub/sub) pattern is a widely used model for real-time API design. It decouples the producers of messages (publishers) from the consumers of messages (subscribers). Publishers send messages on a topic without needing to or caring about who will consume them. Applications subscribe to topics they are interested in (subscribers), receiving messages as they are published.

The following figure depicts a pub-sub model:

Figure 12.8: Example of a publish-subscribe model

The diagram represents a simple example of how the pub-sub model works. The publisher sends out several topics. Subscribers then subscribe to these topics and get the data as it is produced.

The following are the benefits of using a Pub/Sub model:

  * Scalable: The system can easily scale by adding more publishers or subscribers. They are independent and do not affect each other.
  * Flexible: New subscribers can be added to topics without requiring any changes on the publishing side.
  * Efficiency: Reduces the bandwidth and processing power needed as clients only receive messages they have expressed an interest in by subscribing to that topic.



[Request-response over WebSocket](contents.xhtml#sc3_329a)

While WebSockets are inherently bi-directional and event-driven, they are adaptable to support a request-response pattern. This is done by sending a request over a WebSocket connection but receiving a response asynchronously. This pattern is helpful for scenarios where an immediate response is required from the server without closing the connection.

The following are the benefits of using a Request_response over WebSocket pattern:

  * Low latency: Since an open connection is maintained for real-time, bi-directional communication, there is low latency and minimal overhead.
  * Resource efficiency: Since there is no need for multiple HTTP connections, there is lower server load and less network traffic.



[Fan-out and Fan-in architecture for event APIs](contents.xhtml#sc3_330a)

This is a powerful architecture pattern for building event-driven APIs. It enables efficient communication across different services.

Fan-out is used when a single event needs to be broadcast to multiple clients or services. It has specific characteristics, such as:

  * An event is triggered from a single source, e.g., user actions, an external data feed that has come in, or any system change to the infrastructure.
  * This event is then fanned-out to multiple consumers. These can be individual services, microservices, or even other external systems.
  * This distribution of events usually happens asynchronously using messaging queues or pub-sub systems. This allows consumers to process the events at their discretion and does not block the event's producer.



Figure 12.9: Fan-out illustration

The fan-in pattern is when data is aggregated from multiple sources before being delivered to a single recipient. A good example is when a system has numerous sensors (e.g., temperature, pressure, failure switches, etc.), and all data needs to be collected before processing. In this example, the results from these sensors might need to be optionally aggregated or calculated based on specific algorithms to produce a final response. The way aggregation happens can be different and depend on particular use cases.

Figure 12.10: Fan-In illustration

The Fan-in/Fan-out pattern is compelling for the following reasons: 

  * Since this allows for parallel processing, high volumes of events can be handled efficiently and is limited only by the number of consumers.
  * The consumers are loosely coupled. They only need to understand their event format and not the logic of other consumers. This allows for modularity and expandability.
  * The entire pattern is resilient. Failure of one consumer does not affect other consumers or the overall flow. Retries can be parameterized using the messaging system to guarantee eventual delivery.



Messaging systems, pub/sub systems, and event streaming platforms can be used to implement the fan-in/fan-out pattern. Factors like message volume, latency requirements, and integration with existing infrastructure are important in choosing the right platform.

[Command Query Responsibility Segregation](contents.xhtml#sc3_331a)

Command Query Responsibility Segregation (CQRS) is a pattern that separates data reading (query) from the modification of data (command). This segregation allows for more efficient handling and scaling of read and write operations. In real-time APIs, CQRS can handle high volumes of read requests by separating them from write operations, potentially using different modes or databases.

Since this pattern allows for independently optimizing read and write operations, overall improvements to system performance can be made at a more granular level.

Another benefit is that complex operations can be simplified by separating them into commands and queries. This helps in understanding and maintaining the system more easily.

[Backpressure management](contents.xhtml#sc4_332a)

Backpressure refers to the buildup of data at some stage in the system that cannot be processed in real-time. This is of critical concern in real-time systems. Managing backpressure involves implementing mechanisms to control the data flow. This will ensure the system handles high volumes without losing data or degrading performance.

Incorporating backpressure management in a system prevents system overload and ensures stability in operations. In addition, it can improve resource utilization by matching the processing rate to the incoming data rate by scaling resources. This also helps improve the quality of service.

Having looked at some design patterns and guidelines, the following section looks at out-of-the-box technology platforms that facilitate the creation of real-time APIs and handle the underlying mechanism of transferring data. One such approach is to use message brokers and the concept of queues.

[Message brokers and queues](contents.xhtml#sc2_333a)

Real-time applications span multiple services and micro-services. This needs robust and guaranteed communications. In this section, we will explore the role of message brokers and queues and the role they play in a modern API-first ecosystem.

Millions of data pieces (messages) can be produced and consumed in a digital ecosystem. These could be in real-time or slightly delayed data. We need robust infrastructure to ensure these messages are delivered efficiently and smoothly. Message brokers and queues are the background pieces of the puzzle that work in a real-time data environment. While they are usually in the background and are heavily coupled with infrastructure, their importance in a modern API economy cannot be understated.

[Traffic controllers in message brokers](contents.xhtml#sc3_334a)

Think of message brokers as traffic controllers in a busy traffic intersection. Their prime responsibility is to act as an intermediary between message producers (applications) and consumers. They decouple the producers and the consumers and enable scalable and asynchronous communication.

The key characteristics include:

  * Receive messages from producers (applications).
  * Route messages based on defined rules and then direct them to the appropriate consumers (applications/services).
  * Ensure and guarantee message delivery even if consumers are unavailable or experiencing issues.
  * Ensure smooth traffic flow by scaling to handle high volumes.



The following are the benefits of having a message broker:

  * Load balancing and message distribution across multiple consumers.
  * Decoupling services for improved scalability and fault tolerance.
  * Enabling flexible routing and message filtering.
  * Supporting various message protocols and message types



Some popular message brokers include:

  * Apache Kafka: Highly scalable and fault-tolerant, ideal for large-scale streaming applications.
  * RabbitMQ: Lightweight and open-source, suitable for diverse real-time needs.
  * ActiveMQ: Versatile and feature-rich, supporting various messaging protocols.
  * Apache Pulsar: All-in-one messaging and streaming platform.



[Queues](contents.xhtml#sc3_335a)

Queues act as temporary holding lanes for messages, ensuring order and reliability. Messages are added to the back of the queue and processed by consumers in the front, one at a time. Essentially, queues store messages till the consuming application can process them. This enables asynchronous communications and ensures the messages are not lost even under heavy load.

Queues provide the following advantages:

  * Orderly processing: Guarantee messages are processed in the intended sequence.
  * Decoupling producers and consumers: Enables independent scaling and resilience.
  * Load balancing: Distributes workload across multiple consumers.



Applications have different requirements for the transmission of queue messages. Different queueing strategies allow additional flexibility.

Some examples of queues include:

  * First-in-first-out (FIFO): In this strategy, messages are processed in the order they arrive, which is ideal for time-sensitive tasks.
  * Last-in-first-out (LIFO): Newer messages are processed first, which helps handle urgent updates.
  * Priority queues: Messages with higher priority are processed first, ensuring critical tasks are addressed promptly.



Message brokers and queues are the backbone of scalable, reliable, real-time data flow. By leveraging both of these capabilities, applications can handle demanding data volumes with the ability to scale as required.

The use cases include:

  * Task processing and job queues.
  * Event-driven microservice communication.
  * Asynchronous data processing pipelines.



To handle messages, platforms like Apache Kafka and RabbitMQ provide robust solutions for managing high volumes of data with reliability and efficiency.

We will look into some platforms like Apache Kafka and RabbitMQ and provide a high-level comparison of more platforms.

[Apache Kafka](contents.xhtml#sc4_336a)

This widely used distributed event streaming platform is geared to handle high volumes of data in real time. It follows a subscribe-publish paradigm and can process large multiple streams of data. Kafka allows for massive parallel processing and is built around the concept of topics.

Kafka has some important features to be considered while designing around this technology stack, such as:

  * Since Kafka is designed to handle millions of messages per second, it is suitable for large-scale message-processing applications.
  * Kafka replicates data across multiple nodes. This ensures that messages are not lost even if one node has a failure.
  * More nodes can be added for horizontal scalability. This has the advantage of adding capacity without any downtime.
  * Since messages in Kafka can be persisted on disk, it ensures that data is not lost even during system crashes.



More information can be found here: [https://kafka.apache.org/](https://kafka.apache.org/)

[RabbitMQ](contents.xhtml#sc4_337a)

Another alternative to Kafka is RabbitMQ, an open-source messaging broker that implements the Advanced Message Queueing Protocol (AMQP). It is flexible, reliable, and easily integrates with different programming languages and platforms. It supports multiple message patterns, including routing, topics, and Remote Procedure Calls (RPC).

The key features to be considered while designing a system around this stack include:

  * RabbitMQ allows for complex routing and message-handling scenarios. This makes it versatile for a variety of use cases.
  * It has features like message acknowledgment, durable queues, and persistent messages to ensure data is never lost.
  * It has a user-friendly web interface for monitoring and managing the platform.
  * It supports clusters for high availability and scalability.



More information on RabbitMQ can be found here: [https://www.rabbitmq.com/](https://www.rabbitmq.com/)

The use case examples are as follows:

  * Real-time analytics: Kafka is often used in applications requiring real-time analytics, as it can ingest and process large data streams from different sources.
  * Event sourcing: Kafka can be the backbone for event-sourced systems. This means it tracks every change to the application state (think of different statuses within an application) as an event for further analysis, logging, monitoring, etc.
  * RabbitMQ is commonly used to distribute tasks across worker nodes, enabling scalable and efficient processing.
  * Both RabbitMQ and Kafka support inter-service communication between microservices in a distributed architecture. This helps in decoupling services and improves fault tolerance.



A sample comparison table of features and capabilities one should look for before selecting a platform is depicted as follows. The following is a sample representation of features that interest an engineer and architect. The list of platforms is also not exhaustive but should serve as a starter set for comparison.
