* requires additional configuration or application-level handling

[Summary on queues and message brokers](contents.xhtml#sc4_338a)

Message brokers and queues are essential elements of the architecture for modern applications requiring real-time data processing and communication. They provide scalable and efficient mechanisms for message exchange and enable developers to build complex and distributed systems capable of handling high data volumes. A practical consideration while choosing a platform will depend on the complexity of the integration required in an enterprise's existing stack.

[Event-driven architectures](contents.xhtml#sc2_339a)

Event-driven architecture (EDA) is a design paradigm that revolves around reacting to events as they occur. This is different from the traditional request-response interaction. EDA revolves around the production, consumption, and reaction to events generated by applications. We briefly introduced this architecture in [Chapter 5](ch05.xhtml), Embracing Open API Standards, when we described AsyncAPI, an open-source specification standard for EDAs.

[Core concepts of EDA](contents.xhtml#sc3_340a)

The following are the core concepts of EDA:

  * Events: They are the primary source of communication between different components of an application. An event is defined as a state change or an update in an application.
  * Event producers: Source that generates the events. It could be anything from a user action, system alert, or IoT sensor output.
  * Event consumers: They are the consumers that listen to and react to events. They can then perform actions in response to the events they get.
  * Event channels: These are the pipelines through which events are communicated from the producer to the consumer. Their prime responsibility is to ensure events reach the appropriate consumer.
  * Event processing: Handling and reacting to events that could be trivial, like logging or more complex operations, including data transformations, initiating other sequences, etc.



Using EDAs has some benefits that need to be examined before implementation. The key benefits include:

  * Scalability: EDA allows systems to scale more easily by decoupling event producers from consumers. This decoupling enables independent scaling of different components as needed.
  * Loose coupling: Services only have to react to events relevant to them without depending on specific patterns.
  * Flexibility: Easy adapting to changing requirements by adding or modifying event handling logic.
  * Flexibility: New consumers can subscribe to events without requiring changes to the producers, making the system adaptable to changes and new requirements.
  * Responsiveness: By reacting to real-time events, systems can provide immediate feedback or take action, enhancing the user experience and operational efficiency.
  * Resilience: EDA can improve system resilience and fault tolerance. If one component fails, it does not necessarily bring down the entire system, as other components can continue to operate independently.



[Implementing EDA](contents.xhtml#sc3_341a)

There are several key considerations:

  * Event design: Designing events properly is critical to the success of the architecture. Events should be well-defined and contain all the necessary information for consumers to react appropriately.
  * Event sourcing: This is the source of the actual events being generated. It could be changes to the application state stored as a sequence of events. This allows for robust auditing, debugging, and replaying of events to restore or understand the system's current state.
  * Underlying technologies: Platforms like Kafka and RabbitMQ are often used to implement the event channels. This then brings these platforms' advantages, including durability, scalability, and efficient routing of events between producers and consumers.



Some examples of using EDAs include alerting systems, real-time analytics, and monitoring dashboards. In a microservices architecture, EDA's loose coupling and asynchronous communication between services enhance the effectiveness of using EDAs. One major area of use is when systems must analyze and respond to complex patterns in real time. Examples include fraud detection systems, trading platforms, etc.

Common EDA patterns include the pub-sub model and the CQRS model we discussed before. In addition, the Saga pattern is also used. This pattern is vital in managing long-lived business transactions spanning multiple services. Since EDA is based on the principle of decoupled services, the saga pattern complements this by orchestrating transactions across these services without requiring them to invoke each other directly. The saga pattern coordinates across multiple services using either:

  * Choreography: Here, services listen for events and react accordingly, implicitly coordinating the transaction.
  * Orchestration: A central service (orchestrator) explicitly manages the sequence of events and service interactions.



[Harnessing the power of real-time data](contents.xhtml#sc2_342a)

In the digital era, where data is produced in real-time and consumption and demand for real-time data continue to accelerate, it has become a critical competitive edge for businesses to adapt to real-time data.

From a business perspective, some key needs include:

  * Real-time analytics: Implementing real-time analytics enhances an organization's ability to monitor and analyze data as it flows in the system. This capability is important for identifying trends and making informed decisions quickly. An example could be sales data that could help identify early market trends.
  * Event-driven decision-making: Decisions in organizations are made based on many factors, including financial, statistical, etc. By adopting an event-driven approach, businesses can automate decision-making processes based on real-time events. This enhances responsiveness and agility, allowing quick reaction times to specific conditions.
  * Personalization and increasing user engagement: Real-time data in conjunction with instantaneous analytics can be used to tailor content to individual users. This increases user engagement and satisfaction. For example, recommending products and content of websites based on real-time user action events.
  * Predictive maintenance: The explosion of real-time data from sensors and IoT devices has led to the availability of data on equipment status. This helps in applications that can predict equipment failures before they occur and reduce downtime.



[Additional enabling technologies](contents.xhtml#sc2_343a)

We have discussed several enabling technologies, such as streaming data platforms like Apache Kafka, which support ingesting, processing, and analyzing streaming data. WebSockets and real-time APIs are foundational for developing applications requiring instant data transfer and processing.

There are other technologies, such as Redis and Apache ignite, that offer high-speed data storage and retrieval as well. These are in-memory data stores. They have different approaches to consuming, storing, and processing data.

Complex Event Processing (CEP) engines that analyze multiple event streams to identify meaningful patterns are available. They can recognize and immediately respond to complete event sequences.

While we have discussed the benefits of real-time data, there are some challenges we should consider as well that have an impact on other aspects of the ecosystem, such as:

  * Data volumes and velocity: Real-time systems can sometimes produce overwhelming amounts of data. Careful planning for a robust infrastructure and efficient data processing pipelines is needed.
  * Data quality: The sheer amount of real-time data necessitates a robust data pipeline that can check and ensure the accuracy of the data produced. Any decision that is based on flawed data can lead to unforeseen outcomes.
  * Security and privacy: Like other kinds of data, real-time data needs stringent security measures to protect sensitive information and comply with regulations.



[Future of real-time data APIs](contents.xhtml#sc2_344a)

Real-time, asynchronous, and streaming APIs have redefined and revolutionized the technology and business landscape. They are enhancing current applications and offering opportunities for innovations. This final section explores future directions, emerging trends, and potential challenges.

[Emerging trends](contents.xhtml#sc3_345a)

The following are the emerging trends:

  * AI and Machine learning integration: ML and AI become more powerful using real-time data. This combination makes the ML models more intelligent and allows adaptive applications to be built in real time to predict user behavior and automate decision-making.
  * Edge computing: We have talked about IoT devices previously. The number of these devices is multiplying rapidly. Moving the data source closer to the data processing will enhance real-time access to data by reducing latency and efficiently using bandwidth. This will impact asynchronous and streaming APIs.
  * 5G networks: These networks are being rapidly developed and offer unprecedented speeds for data transfer and the volume of data being transferred. This enables the building of real-time applications that combine 5G networks and IoT devices. Examples include autonomous vehicles and augmented/spatial reality experiences.
  * Serverless architecture: Many applications today have the concept of a server in a data center or the cloud. However, serverless computing, which eliminates the need for dedicated processing power, has become more prevalent with architectures that support it efficiently across many different end devices. This model is particularly suited for real-time applications and uses event-driven architecture.



[Addressing challenges](contents.xhtml#sc3_346a)

Real-time APIs have to address some inherent challenges; some of them are included as follows:

  * Data privacy and security: As real-time data becomes more common, the architect might need to navigate complex regulatory landscapes and implement robust security measures as part of the initial design.
  * Resource management: Increasing volumes of data means that the infrastructure needs efficient resource management. Balancing performance with cost will become a key driver during the adoption of these platforms.
  * Standardization: Real-time technology platforms and ecosystems continue to grow. Standardization must be used from the ground up to ensure interoperability between different systems and standards. This means using open standards might help in the integration of disparate systems.
  * Complexity management: Real-time data from multiple sources and different formats will make systems more complex. It will become important to simplify and standardize these complexities without reducing functionality.



[Conclusion](contents.xhtml#sc2_347a)

Designing real-time APIs involves more than just enabling immediate data exchange; it requires a thoughtful application of design patterns that address scalability, efficiency, and reliability. By leveraging patterns such as publish-subscribe, request-response over WebSocket, event-driven architecture, CQRS, and backpressure management, developers can create robust real-time APIs that meet the demands of modern applications. These patterns provide a foundation for building systems that are not only capable of real-time communication but also scalable, maintainable, and efficient.

In the next chapter, we will discuss an important aspect of API management- API gateways and management platforms. An API gateway performs multiple functions, including security enforcement, monitoring, and developer portals.

[Key terms](contents.xhtml#sc2_348a)

  * Event-driven architecture: A design pattern where components communicate by producing and reacting to events asynchronously.
  * Real-time APIs: APIs that enable instantaneous data exchange between systems and clients with minimal latency.
  * Streaming APIs: APIs that provide a continuous flow of data to clients, typically in real-time, as it becomes available.
  * Asynchronous APIs: APIs that allow non-blocking communication, where a response can be received at a later time after the request.
  * Callbacks: Functions passed as arguments to other functions, executed later when an asynchronous task is complete.
  * WebSocket protocol: A communication protocol enabling full-duplex, real-time interaction between client and server over a single TCP connection.
  * Server-Sent Events (SSE): A protocol for unidirectional, real-time server-to-client communication over HTTP.
  * MQTT: A lightweight messaging protocol for publish-subscribe communication, ideal for IoT and low-bandwidth networks.
  * Fan-in: A pattern where multiple event sources funnel events to a single channel or consumer.
  * Fan-out: A pattern where a single event is sent to multiple consumers or subscribers.
  * Publish-subscribe: A messaging pattern where publishers send messages to a broker, and subscribers receive messages for topics of interest.
  * Command Query Responsibility Segregation (CQRS): A pattern that separates the operations for modifying data (commands) from those for querying data.
  * Message brokers: Middleware systems that route, store, and manage messages between distributed systems (e.g., RabbitMQ, Kafka).
  * Message queues: Systems that store and manage messages temporarily, ensuring asynchronous communication between producers and consumers.
  * Apache Kafka: A distributed event-streaming platform designed for high-throughput, fault-tolerant messaging and log processing.
  * RabbitMQ: A lightweight message broker that supports multiple messaging patterns, widely used for simple and flexible message routing.

# [CHAPTER 13API Management Platforms and Gateways](contents.xhtml#ch13a)

[Introduction](contents.xhtml#sc2_349a)

As the number of APIs has increased exponentially, having an explicit platform geared to managing them during their life has become important from an architect's perspective. Managing the complexity, routing, and interactions has become vital to architecting robust API management platforms.

An API management platform essentially helps design, create, test, analyze, and manage APIs through their lifecycle in a secure and scalable environment. It involves various processes, tools, and best practices that enable organizations to produce and consume services. This facilitates seamless integration between different systems, applications, and platforms.

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_350a)

In this chapter, we will discuss the following topics:

  * API management
  * Understanding API gateways
  * Popular API management and gateway solutions
  * Future trends in API management



[Objectives](contents.xhtml#sc2_351a)

The rise of the API economy has led to a massive increase in the number of APIs that companies produce for internal and external consumption. This has led to complexity and brought forward the need for gateways for routing traffic and management tools to manage commercial API integration and publishing. This chapter discusses an API gateway's various roles, including monitoring, security enforcement, portals for self-management, and some developer documentation.

This chapter dives into API management, the platforms, and their roles in routing traffic, enforcing security, and establishing developer portals to help engineers produce and consume APIs. The first section will discuss the critical components of API management and their benefits.

[API management](contents.xhtml#sc2_352a)

An API management framework encompasses strategy, processes, and tools that enable organizations to produce, consume, manage, monitor, and secure APIs throughout their lifecycle. It usually consists of a suite of tools (separate or an integrated all-in solution) that assists organizations in managing their API universe throughout their lifecycle. By using an API management framework, we can ensure that API design and development are consistent, repeatable, scalable, secure, and, most importantly, fulfill the needs of the consumer and the developer (producer) of these APIs.

Tools within this framework usually provide functionality for designing, creating, documenting, deploying, and versioning APIs. They include features for monitoring APIs, performance, usage patterns, and enforcing security.

There are several API management frameworks available, both open-source and commercial.

Popular API management tools include Apigee, Kong, Salesforce Anypoint, AWS API Gateway, Azure API Management, Gravitee.io, etc. They offer comprehensive solutions supporting API management activities in an integrated and seamless platform.

The following figure shows the magic quadrant for API management:

Figure 13.1: Gartner magic quadrant for API management

Gartner defines the API management market as software for managing, governing, and securing APIs. The Gartner magic quadrant is a research methodology developed by Gartner to evaluate and categorize vendors in the API management market based on a set of criteria. The diagram is broken into four quadrants and, based on their features, are placed in the Leaders, Challengers, Niche Players, or Visionaries categories. The diagram above is presented with no commentary, simply to showcase the number of players in this arena.

API gateways have begun straddling the boundaries of the logical design and how they are deployed in the environment, including monitoring, rate limiting, etc. They are part of the modern API management framework.

API management comprises several logical components that make up the process of API management. A logical component diagram of API management is depicted in the following figure:

Figure 13.2: Key logical components for API management

Let us examine some key logical components to get an understanding of the nature of each component.

[API design and definition](contents.xhtml#sc3_353a)

This is a foundational part of the API development process, focusing on the structure of the API, resources, and behavior of the APIs to meet the needs of the producer and consumer of the API. Part of this phase is to define interfaces, document the APIs, and develop a versioning strategy.

This stage also involves outlining the API endpoints, request/response formats, and the underlying data models to ensure they adhere to the best industry standards.

An Open standard such as OpenAPI specifications may also be evaluated and utilized. This framework is important because it helps create user-friendly documentation, proper API testing, and mock server generation. This was discussed in [Chapter 5](ch05.xhtml), Embracing Open API Standards.

This definition phase is crucial to creating a flexible and robust API ecosystem. The foundation defined here helps create a positive developer experience, which helps adopt these APIs. The key here is that thoughtful design and clear definitions will enable the creation of easy-to-use interfaces and loosely coupled architectures.

[Developer portal](contents.xhtml#sc3_354a)

The developer portal enables the onboarding of developers to discover, request access, try them out, and then eventually use them fully. It is usually a fully customizable website automatically generated with complete API documentation. It includes resources like API documentation, sample code, usage guidelines, sandbox environments, and an effective discovery tool.

[API gateway](contents.xhtml#sc3_355a)

API gateways serve as the critical entry point for all API traffic. It is a central platform for managing and routing API calls. They help simplify the backend service complexities and provide a unified interface to clients.

While the primary focus is routing and request handling, API gateways enforce security policies, offer rate-limiting management and quotas, and load balancing and failovers. They also provide features, including transformations and aggregations. They play a significant role in monitoring, logging, and analytics.

In the section on understanding API gateways, we will discuss this in greater detail.

While API gateways are a common solution for managing APIs, other possibilities exist. Choosing different options would depend on the requirements and architecture of the system.

Some complementary and alternate tools to API gateways are described as follows:

  * Service mesh: This dedicated infrastructure layer manages service-to-service communications and is primarily used in microservices architectures. The main difference between this and an API gateway is that a service mesh focuses more on internal communications between microservices.
  * Reverse proxies: A reverse proxy acts as an intermediary server that forwards client requests to the backend servers. A reverse proxy focuses on request forwarding and load balancing, while API gateways offer other features, including rate limiting and authentication.
  * Direct API exposure: Here, APIs are exposed directly to clients without using intermediaries like an API gateway. However, this approach directly places the burden of authentication, rate limiting, etc., on each service. This could complicate development management and pose a considerable security risk for the organization.



[API lifecycle management](contents.xhtml#sc3_356a)

We have discussed API lifecycle management as part of [Chapter 7](ch07.xhtml), API Development Lifecycle, but we discuss the topic as part of the API management tools. API lifecycle management is the concept of overseeing an API from concept to retirement. It includes all the stages between planning, design, mockup, development, testing, deployment, versioning, deprecation, and eventual retirement. Along the way, it provides a way to monitor each aspect of the API lifecycle stages, including statistics to help refine the API journey.

With effective API lifecycle management, APIs are tracked across their existence and remain relevant during their lifecycle. Following the management process, clear communication with developers about changes to APIs, deprecation policies, and backward compatibility standards makes it easy for consumers of these APIs to be knowledgeable about the lifecycle of an API before adoption, and it also offers a mechanism for feedback. This API lifecycle management adoption ensures value to both the producer (efficient API creation) and the consumer (using APIs for various business services).

[API dashboards/monitoring and analytics](contents.xhtml#sc3_357a)

An API management dashboard visually represents analytics and reporting tools within the API ecosystem. For example, continuous monitoring and logging are key aspects of an API dashboard. It helps continuously observe API health performance metrics and usage patterns. As a result, API usage data, performance metrics, errors, logging data, etc., that help identify issues and optimize performance can be displayed in a real-time dashboard. The use of these dashboards helps the organization make data-driven decisions and supports better monetization and effective cost control.

The management platform's analytics and reporting tools are pivotal in understanding and optimizing API usage. These tools can collect detailed data on API requests and responses, usage patterns, system events, and error rates and utilize advanced analytics to identify consumer behavior. This is essential information for API management administrators and product owners to make informed decisions by identifying trends and proactively uncovering issues.

These tools can help reveal insights about the popularity of endpoints, peak usage, and even the geographical distribution of API consumers. With vast amounts of data collected throughout the API lifecycle, detailed reports and dashboards provide a concise view of the API's health. This supports planning with an eye on continuous improvements to API in different aspects.

[Security in API management](contents.xhtml#sc3_358a)

In the context of API Management, security encompasses a wide range of practices and associated technologies dealing with access and threats to the smooth functioning of APIs. This includes implementing authentication and authorization protocols, ensuring data encryption at rest and motion, applying API rate limits to prevent abuse, and regular vulnerability assessments.

The following are the advantages of using an API management platform:

  * Robust security: As part of the process and with standards defined, the API management platform helps implement security features that protect sensitive data. They also need to ensure compliance with regulatory standards.

In addition, API gateways have centralized security and access control mechanisms, which reduce the risk of security vulnerabilities by having security policies applied centrally, tested, and managed efficiently.

  * Scalability: Having all the components in an API management platform and using a versatile API gateway helps organizations transparently manage the ever-expanding number of APIs.
  * Enhanced developer experience: The availability of an API discovery engine, portal-based documentation, usage patterns, and the way to integrate with the APIs accelerates the development cycle.
  * Operational efficiency: An API management platform automates many aspects of the entire API lifecycle. This reduces manual intervention and increases organizational productivity.
  * Platform insights: Having insights into API performance and usage with machine learning using these data points helps in strategic decision-making.
  * Improved governance: Implementing consistent policies and processes and enforcing best practices ensure adherence to enterprise standards. This enables oversight of the entire lifecycle and improves API governance.



[Challenges of using API management platforms](contents.xhtml#sc3_359a)

For an organization to introduce API management as part of its technology stack, commitment and a proper roadmap are required to ensure its success. This is due to today's complex IT environment, which makes integrating API management software with existing systems challenging. Organizations must examine the benefits and the challenges of incorporating these platforms and their differences. However, if the organization is looking at an API-first philosophy, it is a matter of when they need to start using an API management platform. The benefits will outweigh the short-term implementation challenges.

We have given an overview of API management in the preceding section. API management tools are indispensable to businesses and help them maximize the value of their APIs. As technology evolves, API management platforms continue to adapt and offer more advanced and integrated features to meet the growing demands of an API-first economy.

In today's API economy, API management is essential for organizations looking to produce APIs for consumption efficiently and securely. It enables seamless integration between disparate applications, systems, and platforms. It is business agnostic, and by leveraging best practices and the right tools and frameworks, organizations can foster collaboration, accelerate development, and have a data-driven approach to decision-making.

The rest of the chapter will discuss a key component of API management, the API gateway. API gateways have emerged as essential components of the API management platform.

[Understanding API gateways](contents.xhtml#sc2_360a)

An API gateway is the front door to APIs in an organization. Think of it as an abstraction layer for consumers to access backend services (the producers). It acts as a single entry and exit point for all API requests. It routes API requests to the correct backend service, takes the response from these services, and then communicates it back to the consuming API. It enforces security policies, rate limiting quotas, etc., as part of many customizable parameters.

A representation of where the API gateway fits within an end-to-end API service call is shown as follows with a few key API gateway components:

Figure 13.3: End-to-end application call using API gateway

An API gateway organizes the management and control of API interactions through a simple-to-use centralized portal by acting as a central hub.

It offers the following advantages:

  * Simplified access: Consumers of APIs do not need to connect to multiple backend systems; instead, they interact solely with the gateway. This simplifies access and reduces development complexity.
  * Enhanced security: We discussed the importance of security as part of the API management platform. Commercial gateways enforce security policies like authentication, authorization, throttling, and generally safeguarding APIs from malicious attacks and unauthorized requests.
  * Improved traffic management: A crucial role of the gateway is to intelligently route API requests, manage load distribution across backend services, and implement failover mechanisms. This improves availability and performance.
  * Increased insights: Gateways provide centralized logging and monitoring, which helps developers gain insights into API usage and identify potential issues.



The essential components of an API gateway are shown in the following figure:

Figure 13.4: API gateway essential components

Modern gateways perform a large number of functions to facilitate API management. Some key functionalities are discussed in the following sections.

[Routing and request handling](contents.xhtml#sc3_361a)

API gateways efficiently route incoming requests to the correct service endpoint. This is based on the request path, method, and other attributes. The gateway also has a responsibility to optimize performance while routing. This section explores the routing and request-handling mechanisms between clients and services.

[Routing mechanism](contents.xhtml#sc4_362a)

Every request coming to an API gateway involves determining the destination service for the request based on various criteria, including the path, HTTP method, headers, and query parameters. This process is almost the first step and is crucial to ensure that the right service can fulfill the request. There are various methods of routing. A few common ones are as follows:

  * Path-based routing: It is the most common routing mechanism. The request URL determines the target service. For example, if a request starts with /account, then the request will be routed to the account service. Similarly, a /payment request would lead to the payment service.
  * Header-based routing: This routes requests based on specific HTTP header values. This method benefits A/B testing or canary deployments where traffic is split between different service versions based on the header values.
  * Weighted routing: It distributes incoming requests across multiple services based on predefined weights. It's helpful for gradually shifting traffic from an old version of a service to a new one.
  * Content-based routing: It is a complex and resource-intensive method to route traffic. It analyzes the request body to route the request based on certain logic.



[Request handling](contents.xhtml#sc4_363a)

As the gateway routes a request to the appropriate service, it must also handle it with other considerations. As part of request handling, it can perform the following functions:

  * Protocol translation: API gateways often translate between different protocols. For example, it can convert from HTTP/REST to an internal service protocol like gRPC for communication with microservices.
  * Request transformation: An API gateway can transform the request format to match the format the backend service expects. This can include modifying headers, query parameters, or even the body of the request.
  * Authentication and authorization: The gateway performs a critical function to identify the requestor and if they have permission to access the requested services. Many gateways provide the ability to integrate with external identity providers.
  * Rate limiting: Certain APIs might need to limit the number of consumer requests a consumer can make within a specific time period. API gateways have the parameters to set this up to prevent service overload.
  * Caching: Since API gateways service millions of requests, they have the ability to cache frequently requested resources. By serving cached responses, the gateway doesn't need to contact the backend service, reducing latency and load on the backend systems.



The routing and request handling aspect of API gateways has a few key advantages that enhance the API consumption and production experience, such as: 

  * They enhance the end-user experience as they minimize latency, and the requests are processed securely.
  * Introducing new services or modifying an existing one can be done seamlessly without disrupting the consumer's interaction with the backend services. This leads to runtime flexibility.
  * The API gateway facilitates the scaling of applications by distributing the request among services based on parameters that have been set up.



Routing and request handling are essential to the functionality of API gateways and are the first step in the API journey. It enables the gateway to manage the flow of data across applications. However, this function does not exist in isolation in the API gateway. A few other components make up the entire API gateway ecosystem.

[Security enforcement at the gateway](contents.xhtml#sc3_364a)

In a digital world, APIs and their associated services are constantly under threat. API gateways have mechanisms to enforce security measures. This ensures that authorized users can access the correct API resources. They implement security measures such as encryption, authorization, and authentication to protect APIs from threats. They can integrate with identity providers and support security standards like OAuth, JWT, and SAML.

[Authentication and authorization](contents.xhtml#sc4_365a)

While the API gateway allows for authentication and authorization, there are certain open APIs which do not require authentication; for example

[https://api.open-meteo.com/v1/forecast?latitude=40.7128&longitude=-74.0060&daily=temperature_2m_max,temperature_2m_min&timezone=America%2FNew_York](https://api.open-meteo.com/v1/forecast?latitude=40.7128&longitude=-74.0060&daily=temperature_2m_max,temperature_2m_min&timezone=America%2FNew_York)

This API fetches weather information and does not require API keys or any other form of authentication. It produces the following JSON response that the consumer application can use as it deems fit:

{

"latitude": 40.710335,

"longitude": -73.99307,

"generationtime_ms": 0.03898143768310547,

"utc_offset_seconds": -18000,

"timezone": "America/New_York",

"timezone_abbreviation": "EST",

"elevation": 32,

"daily_units": {

"time": "iso8601",

“temperature_2m_max”: “°C”,

“temperature_2m_min”: “°C”

},

"daily": {

"time": [

"2024-03-01",

"2024-03-02"

],

"temperature_2m_max": [

6.7,

10.2

],

"temperature_2m_min": [

-2.6,

1.6

]

}

}

The API gateway works in these scenarios as well, where while the API gateway can be responsible for security, it can also allow passthrough, even though it is not advisable.

However, these types of APIs are becoming less common as services want some basic security or wish to monetize their APIs.

Most APIs will have some kind of authentication to verify the identity of a user or a service making an API request. Authorization, conversely, determines if the authenticated entity has permission to perform the requested action. 

API gateways often integrate with identity providers and support various authentication and authorization protocols, including OAuth 2.0, JWT, API Keys, SAML, etc.

[TLS/SSL encryption](contents.xhtml#sc4_366a)

Transport Layer Security (TLS) and Secure Sockets Layer (SSL) are cryptology protocols designed to provide communications security over a network. API gateways can enforce the encryption of all data transmitted from the client to the gateway, ensuring data integrity.

[IP whitelisting and blacklisting](contents.xhtml#sc4_367a)

API Gateways can allow clients access based on IP addresses. This is called whitelisting and permits access to IP addresses explicitly entered into the gateway parameters. It will block all other IP addresses. It also has blacklisting, which blocks specific IP addresses. These could have been marked and are known as malicious.

[Rate-limiting, throttling, and quotas](contents.xhtml#sc4_368a)

Denial-of-Service (DoS) and Distributed Denial-of-Service (DDoS) attacks are common among many well-known sites. API Gateways can enforce rate limiting and throttling on API requests coming to the gateway. It can be based on timeframes or other alternate options.

Quotas are linked to rate limiting but focus more on the long-term usage of APIs. While rate limiting deals with the frequency of requests over a short period (seconds, minutes), quotas can limit the total number of requests a user can make over a day or month. Quotas help enforce fair usage policies so that no API user can monopolize the API resources.

In addition, quotas are very useful when monetizing APIs. Rate limiting and quotas help control costs by preventing excessive use and could be determined by SLA and volume contracts. It also prevents software bugs from bringing a system to its knees and running into massive cost overruns of resources.

API gateways have different methods for implementing rate limiting and quotas. Some common ways include:

  * Fixed window: This is a simple method where the rate limit is reset at fixed intervals, such as every minute or every hour. This is easy to implement and allows bursts of traffic at the end of the reset period.
  * Sliding log: Here, each request is logged and checked against the limit by considering the timestamp of each request. The term sliding refers to the time window that is used to measure the rate of request. The API gateway keeps track of the number of requests each client makes within a moving time window, such as the last minute, last hour, or 10 seconds. As new requests come in, the gateway removes the oldest requests from the window and adds new requests to the window.
  * Token bucket: This algorithm allows a certain number of requests in a given time frame, adding new tokens at regular intervals. This smooths out the burst of traffic and provides a fair balance between limiting and allowing requests.
  * Leaky bucket: Similar to the token bucket but with a fixed output rate. This ensures that the processing of requests is smooth over time and is helpful to clients who cannot handle sudden bursts of traffic.



While rate limits and quotas are beneficial, their implementation must be mindful of determining appropriate limits and managing exceptions for users needing higher limits. This is often done during API monitoring when software is being tested.

[Content-based security policies](contents.xhtml#sc3_369a)

API gateways have the ability to inspect the content of messages to enforce security policies. For example, it could validate input data to prevent SQL injection or other injection attacks. By allowing this level of inspection, gateways proactively help identify and block malicious requests before they reach the backend services.

Security enforcement is a critical function of API gateways, providing tools and protocols to protect the API ecosystem from various threats. Leveraging these tools as part of the API gateway ensures the organization can take proactive steps to safeguard its consumers and producers of APIs.

[Load balancing and failover](contents.xhtml#sc3_370a)

Modern applications have users worldwide, and loads are never linear. They could vary by many factors, including time of day. Modern applications expect high availability of their APIs at all times. Load balancing and failover mechanisms are part of the API gateway configuration. This is achieved by efficiently distributing incoming API requests across multiple backend services and handling disruptions gracefully.

Load balancing distributes traffic across services to ensure no single server is overloaded, leading to slow response times. API gateways can act as traffic controllers and continue to route requests to the most appropriate service based on some algorithms. These algorithms include round robin, least connections, IP hash, and weighted algorithms. We discussed this in [Chapter 10](ch10.xhtml), API Performance and Scaling.

[Failover](contents.xhtml#sc4_371a)

The API gateway can automatically switch to a standby server or system upon the failure of an active server. API gateways monitor the health of the backend service and reroute the traffic automatically, reducing downtime and enhancing availability.

Two standard methods for failover are:

  * Active-passive: A secondary (passive) server takes over the responsibility of the primary server in the event of a failure. This switching can be manual or automatic based on the system parameters.
  * Active-active: Multiple servers perform the same task simultaneously, distributing traffic across them. Upon failure of a server, the load is automatically redistributed amongst the other servers, providing a seamless failover mechanism.



To implement the failover strategies, API gateways perform different types of health checks periodically to monitor the status of backend services. These could be simple pings or more sophisticated systems to indicate operational readiness.

In addition to high availability, enhanced performance, and increased reliability, API gateways ensure that resources are used efficiently at any given time.

[Transformation and aggregation](contents.xhtml#sc3_372a)

API gateways offer two key functionalities that are useful in the API world: transformation and aggregation. This targets the data flow between the consumer and the backend services (producers). This section describes how API gateways perform these critical functions and help streamline communications between producers and consumers.

[Transformation](contents.xhtml#sc4_373a)

Data transformation involves converting or modifying the data format or content of a request or response. It can be considered an adapter that makes the data suitable for different systems.

Transformation in an API gateway can include:

  * Protocol translation: Converts data between communication protocols such as REST to SOAP or gRPC. A client might send data in JSON format to a backend service that exects an XML format. The gateway can transform the JSON to XML format before sending it to the service and vice versa.
  * Data enrichment: Additional information can be added to requests or responses that might be required by the client or service.
  * Data filtering: A backend system might be written to produce unnecessary data in their response, which is not helpful to the client. The gateway can filter this additional data before sending it back to the client, making it more efficient.
  * Data encryption/Decryption: The API gateway can encrypt data in the request before sending it to the backend service and vice versa. This might be a requirement in some instances and is a good feature in a robust API gateway.



[Data aggregation](contents.xhtml#sc4_374a)

Data aggregation combines data from multiple sources. This is very useful when dealing with microservices architecture when a single API call might require data from several backend services. 

For aggregation, the key benefit is combining responses. For example, the API gateway might send multiple requests to various backend services, aggregate the individual responses into a unified response, and then send it back to the client. In addition, the API gateway can combine the data from these sources and create a richer and more concise view of the data for the client.

For example, a product information request might get data from the product details service and the product review service, aggregate the data, and send it back to the client.

This simplifies the client-side logic as the data is in one response. In addition, by reducing client server trips, network performance can be optimized.

Implementing transformation and aggregation, while a powerful mechanism, requires proper planning and implementation.

  * Transformation rules: These must be clearly specified and defined, including format changes and protocol translations.
  * Aggregation logic: It must be clearly determined to ensure that the data from multiple services is combined in a structured and helpful way and of value to the client.



Transformation and aggregation do increase the complexity of the API gateway, especially in the case of integration with existing infrastructure. This increases costs and resources. Maintaining and optimizing this logic can also be challenging as the number of APIs increases.

In modern, complicated digital systems, API gateways' transformation and aggregation capabilities are a robust integration layer between clients and backend services, and their value should not be underestimated. They help ease the development cycle and create a seamless and integrated digital experience.

[Gateway caching](contents.xhtml#sc3_375a)

Caching is an optimization technique enabled by the gateway and used to enhance the performance of the API services. The gateway caches copies of frequently accessed data and responses. Serving data from the cache reduces the need to engage with the backend services, decreasing latency and reducing workloads. Caching improves response times and system efficiency by implementing at a central API gateway level.

This is particularly beneficial for data that does not change frequently, such as product information or any other static content.

The benefits of caching in API gateways are as follows:

  * Reduced latency: Responses served from the cache are significantly faster than sending the request to the service to be processed every time through the entire backend stack.
  * Reduced backend load: Reducing the number of requests to the backend reduces the load on these systems.
  * Increased scalability: API gateways can handle more requests with the same hardware as the demand on the backend is reduced. Sizing infrastructure with caching enabled helps improve scalability.
  * Cost reduction: Reducing the infrastructure needed at the backend and eliminating network traffic all contribute to reducing the costs of the entire API ecosystem.



Implementing caching requires considering what needs to be cached and how long the cache is valid before the system must invalidate the cache. A key parameter in caching systems is Time-To-Live (TTL), where each cached item is associated with a TTL. This determines how long the data remains cached before being refreshed on the subsequent request to that data.

In addition, when data is deleted or updated, the cache needs to be invalidated, and the level of granularity of caching needs to be decided.

Another critical factor to consider is whether sensitive data can and should be cached to ensure end-to-end security, which is usually dictated by the type of data and the regulatory and compliance challenges that might arise.

A significant factor that must be evaluated in systems implementing caching is ensuring data consistency between the cache and the backend services, especially when the data might be frequently updated.

Caching at the API gateway level offers system integrators a powerful mechanism to enhance the performance and reliability of APIs. While they improve response times and reduce backend load, they require ongoing management to address future challenges.

[Monitoring, logging, and analytics](contents.xhtml#sc3_376a)

With the proliferation of APIs, there is a need to have real-time visibility into API performance, usage, and health that can be monitored automatically and continuously. API gateways can play a pivotal role by providing comprehensive monitoring and logging, leading to innovative analytical capabilities. These features help organizations to capture API metrics, understand usage patterns, identify issues proactively, and, most importantly, make informed decisions based on real-time data.

We will discuss a bit more on these capabilities in this section by looking at some key characteristics, such as:

  * Monitoring: The real-time observation of an API's health and performance is usually monitored continuously. By identifying potential parameter variations, one can quickly identify and address issues such as slower response time, downtime, or any other errors.
  * Logging: Records and stores detailed information about API requests and responses. It usually includes such data as timestamps, headers, and any errors that might have been thrown. Logs are beneficial for both debugging and audit/compliance issues. Furthermore, automated tools exist to examine these logs for AI-based analysis.
  * Analytics: The platform gathers a wealth of data. This captured data provides insights into API usage trends, patterns, and consumer behavior. These analytic data points help in capacity planning and optimizing API design based on API popularity and actual usage.



Most API gateways are configured out of the box to monitor and log API traffic. The level of granularity, which determines the level of detail captured, must be balanced with the overheads associated with processing and storing large amounts of log information. In addition, logging and monitoring could utilize significant resources which could impact API performance. Logs might contain sensitive information. They must be carefully set up to eliminate this possibility and comply with privacy laws and security standards.

API gateways offer advanced capabilities for deep insights into API performance and usage. Traffic analysis endpoint performance coupled with consumer insights helps administrators to identify and resolve issues quickly. By looking at these over time, strategic insights might be gleaned, informing future development priorities and influencing API strategy.

In addition, these logs and other data points might help satisfy compliance and security requirements.

While the benefits of implementing analytics, logging, and monitoring as essential API gateway components are numerous, the amount of data that could be generated and stored needs to be intelligently planned and managed to minimize performance impact.

[Additional features offered by API gateways](contents.xhtml#sc3_377a)

API gateways now form the backbone of an API ecosystem and offer some basic features like monitoring, logging, analytics, caching, transformations, caching, etc. In addition, vendors have been incorporating additional features into their gateway products to enhance their offerings and provide differentiation between the various products. A few of these features are described as follows.

[Developer portal integration](contents.xhtml#sc4_378a)

Many API gateways (e.g., Apigee) offer additional features beyond traditional API gateways' capabilities. Integrating developer portals as part of the API gateway narrows the gap between API producers and consumers. It provides a common way to communicate information on all aspects of the development and deployment lifecycle. This section examines the significance of developer portal integration with API gateways and how this benefits organizations.

Developer portals are dedicated websites that provide comprehensive support for developers. This includes:

  * API discovery: This facilitates exploring and identifying available APIs within an organization's API ecosystem. It allows developers to find existing APIs, understand their functionality, and then use them as required. Some API gateways facilitate this by maintaining an API registry and providing sophisticated search capabilities to discover the APIs through an interactive interface.
  * API documentation: The developer's documentation section contains detailed information on API endpoints, request and response formats, syntax descriptions, and usage examples with code in many instances. By having good documentation, developers can adopt APIs with minimal support. API specifications such as OpenAPI (Swagger) can be automatically published and constantly updated on the developer portal, ensuring updated and accurate information. API discovery.
  * API keys and authentication: API-based ecosystems frequently need API keys, tokens, etc., to access other parts of the developer portal, including the APIs. When developers register on the portal, API keys can be automatically generated and managed through this portal. By providing this as a self-service, the onboarding of APIs can be scalable for better developer productivity.
  * Testing and debugging tools: A developers portal has the tools and environments required to allow developers to test API calls, understand rate limits, and debug messages in real-time.
  * Usage metrics and analytics: The detailed information collected by the API gateway can be published on this portal, allowing developers insights into API usage metrics and offering avenues to improve the API's functionality and performance.
  * Support: Community forums, FAQs, and other support channels to promote a collaborative environment around the API ecosystem are all part of the developer portal. This is usually where feedback on functionality and performance issues can be given, enabling a culture of continuous improvement.



There are some key benefits to adopting a developer portal, such as:

  * Enhanced developer experience: By simplifying the discovery and onboarding process for developers, it is easier for developers to start using APIs quickly. The discovery process also helps create APIs with duplicate functionality.
  * Increased API adoption: Developer portals supply essential resources and tools to developers. This drives API adoption and encourages developers to focus on innovative business functionality.
  * Operational efficiency: By automating API management, documentation creation, and updates, developers can focus on developing applications rapidly with significantly reduced manual overhead.
  * Innersourcing: A centralized portal fosters community building and encourages product owners to source functionality from within the organization instead of looking outside the organization for solutions.



One major challenge that needs effort from the entire community is ensuring that the documentation is current. In addition, managing access and permissions needs to be as automated and user-friendly as possible.

We have discussed the major components that form the basis for API management, with the gateway being the central component of this ecosystem. The following section will examine the options available to deploy these gateways.

[Deployment patterns](contents.xhtml#sc3_379a)

API gateways can be deployed in different ways to ensure the deployment pattern can cope with the organization's scalability, performance, and security requirements. This section examines some common deployment patterns for API gateways and highlights their characteristics.

[Monolithic gateway](contents.xhtml#sc4_380a)

In this pattern, a single API gateway manages all API traffic for the organization. This is a highly centralized pattern, and while it simplifies management, configuration, and deployment, it has potential scalability and performance challenges in addition to being a single point of failure.

This deployment pattern is used by smaller organizations with fewer APIs and lower traffic volumes.

[Microgateway](contents.xhtml#sc4_381a)

This pattern is a decentralized approach to managing API traffic and is highly used for microservices architecture. In this pattern, multiple lightweight gateways are deployed closer to the microservices they serve. Each micro gateway manages a subset of the API traffic and is oriented to specific functions or services.

Having micro gateways closer to the service has the advantage of customized options for security and other policies. In addition, scalability and performance are improved, and faults can be easily isolated and rectified.

Microgateways do increase management and configuration complexity and the potential for duplication of the API gateway's policies.

[Hybrid gateway](contents.xhtml#sc4_382a)

In this deployment pattern, centralized and decentralized gateways (or gateway components) are scattered across the organization. A central gateway handles standard functions (e.g., authentication) and micro gateways for service-specific tasks.

This pattern allows for the scalability of micro gateways while balancing the simplicity of a monolithic gateway. This provides flexibility in managing specific services with its own micro gateway. This pattern is helpful for organizations transitioning from monolithic to microservices architecture, which needs a distributed architecture from the beginning. While this is a useful pattern to deploy, it requires additional levels of planning to balance centralization and decentralization against increased infrastructure complexity.

[Serverless gateway](contents.xhtml#sc4_383a)

This deployment pattern is a managed service that is provided to route requests to serverless functions (e.g., AWS Lambda and Azure Functions). This pattern aims to integrate seamlessly with serverless backends, providing all standard gateway services without establishing and managing our infrastructure.

This pattern is typical in event-driven architectures. However, the disadvantage is that it is dependent on the gateway provider. Limitations in this model include the lack of complete control and possible customizations in other patterns.

[Edge gateway](contents.xhtml#sc4_384a)

An edge gateway is a device or service that bridges local devices, systems, and/or networks (called the “edge”) and centralized systems like the cloud, data centers, or other networks. Edge gateways are typically deployed closest to the API consumers, usually at the network's edge. It processes, secures, and manages traffic or data close to the source of data generation. Hence, this pattern reduces latency by processing the requests and responses as close to the consumer as possible.

Some key functions of an Edge Gateway are described as follows:

  * Data aggregation: Edge gateways collect data from multiple devices or sensors (e.g., IoT devices, smart appliances, industrial control equipment).
  * Data filtering and processing: These edge gateways can preprocess and filter raw data, apply basic analytics, and send reduced data to the cloud or other systems.
  * Protocol translation: Some edge gateways can also convert or translate data between different communication protocols used by the edge devices and the protocols used by backend systems (e.g., MQTT to HTTP or other proprietary protocols to REST).
  * Security: Encryption, authentication, and firewall capabilities to secure the data in motion between edge devices and the cloud are also features that some edge gateways provide.



Examples of edge gateway platforms include AWS IoT Greengrass, Microsoft Azure IoT Edge, and Google Cloud IoT Edge.

Selecting the correct pattern is a strategic and practical decision depending on various factors. These factors include the architectural style (monolithic, microservices, etc.) prevalent in the organization, scalability requirements, fault isolation requirements, etc. Any chosen deployment pattern must align with the enterprise's overall API strategy and architectural goals.

[Popular API management and gateway solutions](contents.xhtml#sc2_385a)

The demand for API management solutions has skyrocketed over the last few years. Correspondingly, the number of API gateway solutions with features suited to API management has also increased rapidly. Today, there are a variety of solutions, from open-source to enterprise-grade platforms, each offering unique features and integration capabilities.

The following list provides a high-level overview of some popular API gateway solutions. They are not listed in any particular order.

  * Apigee from Google Cloud: It is a comprehensive API management platform that helps enterprises design, deploy, secure, monitor, and scale APIs. Apigee is well known for its developer portal, policy enforcement management, and analytics capabilities.

It is used by enterprises looking for robust and scalable API management solutions and has extensive features to support modern API-driven ecosystems across multiple environments and clouds. A helpful feature is its support for the monetization of APIs.

  * Azure API management from Microsoft: Azure API management is a cloud-based platform that allows businesses to manage their APIs across multiple environments. It has all the features of a modern management system, including rate limiting, caching, detailed analytics, OAuth, and OpenID connect.

It is integrated with Azure monitoring tools and is ideal for organizations already invested in the Microsoft ecosystem. It connects to APIs hosted on Azure, on-premise data centers, or other cloud environments, making it a versatile choice for diverse infrastructure.

  * AWS API gateway from Amazon: It is a fully managed service that provides a scalable solution for consuming, publishing, maintaining, and securing APIs at scale. It supports different protocols, including WebSocket APIs and RESTful protocols, and is geared toward businesses seeking a cloud-native API management system.

An impressive feature of this gateway is its deep integration with AWS services, including serverless support and integration with AWS Lambda.

  * Mulesoft AnyPoint platform from Salesforce: It is a complete set of products for API management that is part of its integration suite of products. It allows businesses to manage APIs across the API lifecycle. It suits applications looking for API management and integration services across different channels.
  * API Connect from IBM: It is a comprehensive API management platform, including a self-service portal for developers with security and governance and automated API creation. It is a good platform for organizations requiring both microservices and cloud-native development. It also offers strong integration and connects applications with data from older technologies, including mainframes.
  * Kong: It is a widely adopted open-source API gateway known for its extensible plug-in architecture. It supports RESTful and GraphQL APIs and is designed for modern architectures, including serverless, Kubernetes clusters and cloud-native applications. It is very well suited for customizable API management solutions.
  * WSO2 API Manager is an open-source API management platform with a complete API creation, publishing, and lifecycle management solution. It supports a range of API standards and protocols across different environments. It is ideal for organizations seeking a customizable API management platform with strong community support.
  * Tyk is an open-source API management and gateway platform offering on-premise and cloud versions. It has most of the features like quotas, rate limiting, access control, etc., and is geared towards enterprises requiring a high degree of control over their API traffic.

While it is open-source, it has options for enterprise support. It has multi-protocol support, including REST, GraphQL, and SOAP. It is reasonably customizable for specific needs.

  * Cloud Mashery from Tibco: This is a cloud-native API management platform for managing and scaling API ecosystems. It focuses additionally on data transformation capabilities and caters to businesses that want fine-grained control over their API strategy. It is flexible and robust, with solid API analytics and developer engagement support.
  * Gravitee.io: It is a relatively new open-source API management platform offering comprehensive tools for managing the entire API lifecycle. It is lightweight and includes API gateway functionality. It helps organizations streamline their API strategies and includes features such as rate limiting, authentication, access control, and detailed analytics.

Gravitee.io's focus on support for event-driven architectures sets it apart from other platforms. It caters to the typical request/response and pub/sub-event-driven APIs. Its focus is on emerging trends and hence supports microservices Kubernetes and provides support for GraphQL and gRPC.

  * Postman: This is a popular API development tool that has evolved into a comprehensive platform. We have discussed Postman as part of the Open Specification chapter, but here, it is presented as an alternative to API management.

Unlike traditional API management solutions, Postman does not offer an API gateway. Its focus is more on the other aspects of the API management lifecycle rather than acting as an intermediary between producers and consumers. It integrates with other API gateways to form a complete solution. It is presented here in this section for consideration since many organizations already have Postman in their ecosystem.




Selecting the right API gateway solution is a critical decision for any organization. This platform impacts an organization's API ecosystem's security, usability, and scalability, amongst many other factors.

[Sample criteria to choose the right API management platform](contents.xhtml#sc3_386a)

When selecting an API management platform, minimal criteria must be evaluated based on each organization's requirements. In this section, we want to highlight some of these categories and key criteria that will aid you in choosing the right platform.

The following table details some example criteria that must be evaluated while selecting a suitable API management platform. These criteria have been broken out into certain key categories that are of interest to different consumers of API management platforms. Examples include usability, security, performance, etc.

Usability and developer experience  
---  
Criteria |  Description  
Developer portal |  User-friendly developer portal offering extensive documentation, community features, and extensive and flexible API discovery  
API design and mocking tools |  Tools that help in API design, versioning, and mocking  
SDK and documentation generation |  Automatic creation of SDKs and documents from specifications  
Self-service and onboarding |  Ability for developers to self-register, explore APIs, and use them immediately  
API monetization |  Native support for charging for usage of APIs  
Security  
Criteria |  Description  
Authentication and authorization |  Support for standard protocols (OAuth2, JWT, OpenID Connect) to ensure secure access to APIs  
Threat protection |  Mechanisms to protect against common threats (SQL injection, cross-site scripting) and DDoS attacks  
Data encryption |  Ensure data in transit and at rest is encrypted using industry-standard encryption algorithms  
Compliance |  Tools should comply with relevant regulations (GDPR, HIPAA, etc.) and provide features to help enforce these requirements  
Bot detection |  Gateway can detect and alert teams when potential bots are targeting APIs  
Performance and scalability  
Criteria |  Description  
Caching support |  Ability to cache responses to improve API response times and reduce backend load.  
Load balancing |  Efficient distribution of API traffic across servers to ensure high availability and performance.  
Rate limiting and quotas |  Tools to control traffic flow to APIs, preventing overuse and potential abuse.  
High availability and disaster recovery |  Ensure the API management platform supports failover mechanisms and data redundancy to maintain uptime.  
Integration and interoperability  
Criteria |  Description  
Support for API standards |  Compatibility with REST, GraphQL, SOAP, and other API protocols for broad interoperability.  
Connector/plug-in ecosystem |  Availability of pre-built connectors or plug-ins to integrate with databases, messaging systems, authentication services, etc.  
Customization and extensibility |  Ability to add custom logic or integrate with external services through scripting or plug-ins.  
Legacy system integration |  Tools to expose and manage APIs from legacy systems, enabling digital transformation.  
Monitoring and analytics  
Criteria |  Description  
Real-time monitoring |  Capabilities to monitor API performance, usage, and health in real-time.  
Logging and auditing |  Detailed logs for API calls and the ability to audit them for security and compliance purposes.  
Analytics and reporting dashboard |  Tools to analyze API usage patterns performance metrics and derive insights for optimization.  
Alerting and notifications |  Automated alerts for system anomalies, breaches, or quota limits to quickly respond to issues.  
Lifecycle management  
Criteria |  Description  
Versioning support |  Features to manage multiple versions of APIs and smoothly transition between them.  
Deployment automation |  Tools to automate the deployment of APIs across different environments (development, staging, production).  
Lifecycle governance |  Policies and workflows to manage the API lifecycle from creation to deprecation, ensuring consistency and compliance.  
Collaboration tools |  Support for team collaboration in the API design and development process, including version control and role-based access.  
API gateway  
Criteria |  Description  
Routing and orchestration |  Ability to route requests to the correct backend services and orchestrate multiple backend calls as needed for a single API call.  
Protocol transformation |  Conversion capabilities between different protocols (e.g., from REST to SOAP or vice versa) to ensure compatibility between client and backend services.  
Edge caching |  Caching responses at the edge (closest to the client) to reduce latency and backend load.  
Self-service and onboarding |  Ability for developers to self-register, explore APIs, and use them immediately.  
Security policies |  Implementation of security policies at the gateway level to protect backend services.  
Support for protocols, SOAP, REST, gRPC, WebSockets, and others |  Allows for various protocols and the level of support for each protocol and format.  
Event-native API management |  Supports event-driven APIs, asynchronous APIs, and streaming APIs.  
  
Table 13.1: Example criteria

The above criteria are a minimal subset to jump-start your thinking. In a typical organization, there will be many more sections, including cost, existing technology stack use, vendor background, and history.

[Future trends in API management](contents.xhtml#sc2_387a)

We will end this chapter by discussing future API management trends. This field constantly evolves, and innovations, concepts, and products keep coming out.

Here are a few examples:

  * Machine learning and AI integration: AI can be leveraged to predict API behavior and performance issues. An advanced version of this could even automate the resolution process.
  * Growth of microservices architecture: Microservices architecture is rising and will drive the need for hybrid solutions, including more dynamic and flexible API management solutions.
  * Increased focus on developer experience: The experience of developers is now as important as the rest of the ecosystem, which implies that the usability of the developer portals is gaining significant traction. This will enable faster adoption of APIs, resulting in effective API monetization.
  * Blockchain for API security: A potential option is to use this technology to improve API security and data integrity.
  * Increased adoption of multi-cloud and hybrid architecture: In today's multi-vendor environments, there is a need for API Management solutions that can handle multiple clouds and on-premises data centers. Adopting cloud-native development is becoming crucial now, and API management solutions must support and fully integrate into this model.
  * Emergence of edge computing: Managing APIs right at the edge with the associated edge caching, edge security, and edge computing enables the API management solution to handle both edge and central solutions.
  * Integration with Dev/Ops and CI/CD pipelines: There is a growing need for solutions to integrate with diverse DevOps and CI/CD tools that provide automated testing, automated deployment and promotion, and monitoring of these pipelines.
  * Enhanced focus on security: APIs are becoming ubiquitous; hence, there is a need for solutions to deal with current and future threats. In addition, providing advanced security features, including AI-based security tools, will be critical.



[Conclusion](contents.xhtml#sc2_388a)

API management is an important aspect of a modern software development life cycle. Several companies provide API management products, and in this chapter, we start by providing the logical components that constitute API management.

As part of this chapter, we discuss a critical tool, the API gateway, which forms an essential aspect of API management platforms. We discuss different deployment patterns for API gateways and then dive into some key API platform products available today, including Apigee, Azure API Management, Mulesoft, etc. It is important to have clear and defined selection criteria for organizations before deploying API management platforms into their environment. To get the readers started, we have defined a set of categories and criteria within each category that will serve as a starter set of criteria for selecting the API management platform.

The next chapter discusses an upcoming field of understanding and using AI APIs that are now available to help developers seamlessly embed advanced AI capabilities into their applications. We will introduce the reader and offer insights into the different types of available AI APIs (for example, computer vision APIs, speech recognition, etc.

[Key terms](contents.xhtml#sc2_389a)

  * API gateway: It is a server that acts as a single entry point for APIs, handling tasks like request routing, composition, and protocol translation.
  * Routing and request handling: Directing incoming API requests to the correct backend service or endpoint.
  * Security enforcement: Applying security measures like authentication, authorization, and data encryption for APIs.
  * Rate limiting: A technique used to control the number of requests a client can make to an API within a specific time frame to prevent abuse and protect server resources.
  * Load balancing and failover: Distributing API traffic evenly across servers and ensuring service continuity during server failures.
  * Transformation and aggregation: Modifying API requests/responses and combining data from multiple APIs into a single output.
  * Caching at the gateway level: Temporarily storing API responses at the gateway to reduce backend load and improve response times.
  * Monitoring, logging, and analytics: Tracking API performance, logging events, and analyzing data to improve efficiency and troubleshoot issues.
  * Developer portal integration: Providing a platform for developers to access API documentation, generate API keys, and test APIs.
  * Deployment patterns: Strategies for deploying APIs and gateways, such as on-premise, cloud, or hybrid environments.
  * API dashboard: A user interface for visualizing and managing API usage, performance, and operational metrics.
  * API transformation: Modifying data formats or structures in API requests or responses to meet client or backend requirements.
  * API aggregation: Combining data or functionalities from multiple APIs to provide a unified service or response.
  * Policy enforcement: Applying pre-defined rules to API traffic, such as security, transformation, or rate-limiting policies.
  * Developer portal: A platform where developers can access API documentation, test APIs, and manage API keys.




# [CHAPTER 14Trends in AI APIs](contents.xhtml#ch14a)

[Introduction](contents.xhtml#sc2_390a)

Artificial intelligence (AI) has grown significantly in its adoption and the underlying technology in recent years. A more recent branch of AI algorithms is in the field of generative models. Generative AI refers to the subset of AI algorithms that can create new content, such as text, images, audio, and video, based on patterns the algorithm learns from existing data. The rise of generative AI has led to the development of powerful APIs that allow developers to integrate APIs that will enable them to build applications with these capabilities quickly.

We are all familiar with ChatGPT. Driving this chatbox is the OpenAI API, a set of large language model APIs that allow for accessing the AI models developed by OpenAI. These APIs bridge our applications and the cutting-edge AI models exposed as APIs. Examples of APIs for LLMs include models from Google, OpenAI, and Anthropic.

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_391a)

This chapter examines the exciting world of AI APIs, their emerging capabilities, and their profound impact across various industries. Some of the topics we cover include:

  * AI-driven API landscape
  * Specialization of AI APIs
  * Integration and AI agents
  * Interoperability between AI APIs
  * Security and ethics
  * Future trends in AI APIs
  * Serverless APIs



[Objectives](contents.xhtml#sc2_392a)

Today’s sophisticated machine intelligence-based systems require powerful hardware to process AI-based requests. This is because AI APIs are emerging as essential development tools to embed intelligence into applications seamlessly. A simple request to an AI API unlocks diverse functionality, including generative text-based content, computer vision, and speech. In this chapter, we discuss some of the revolutionary APIs that have been introduced and offer insight into their various applications and potential for disruptive architectures. In addition, we discuss the aspects of integration and the concept of AI agents. Further, we will discuss the transformative power of AI APIs in specific industries and how they will revolutionize healthcare, finance, and other industries. By the end of this chapter, you will gain a comprehensive understanding of how AI APIs are poised to shape the technology landscape.

[AI-driven API landscape](contents.xhtml#sc2_393a)

AI APIs are transforming software development in various ways as there is an ever-expanding availability of AI APIs. AI APIs enable businesses to leverage AI's power in their applications, products, and services. Some trends that are visible in the AI API-driven landscape are indicated as follows:

  * Increased adoption: AI APIs as a monetized business are becoming increasingly popular. Specialized APIs in different areas are becoming common, and their adoption is expected to grow. Certain reports (e.g., from Research and Market - Artificial Intelligence (AI) - Global Strategic Business Report) project that the AI market is estimated at US$87.4 Billion in 2023 and is projected to reach US$459.3 Billion by 2030, growing at a CAGR of 26.8% from 2023 to 2030.
  * AI use cases expansion: AI is used in various industries, from finance and retail to healthcare. These use cases are being enabled by APIs served from AI server farms with massive processing capabilities.
  * Integration with other technologies: AI-enabled APIs enable integration with other technologies such as IoT, blockchain, and virtual reality, allowing smartphones, smart home devices, and autonomous vehicles to harness AI power remotely.
